OVERVIEW: In 1975, following a jury trial, petitioner prisoner was convicted of first degree murder and was sentenced to life imprisonment under the indeterminate sentencing law (ISL) that was in effect at that time. In 1977, the determinate sentencing law (DSL) took effect. Unlike the ISL, the DSL contained different guidelines for parole release. The California Supreme Court determined that a life prisoner such as petitioner needed to be considered for parole release under the administrative guidelines of the ISL in effect at the time of the sentence, and under the regulations of the DSL. The state began to hold so-called Stanworth hearings for parole release date determinations for life prisoners convicted under the ISL. Petitioner contended that he was entitled to counsel at his Stanworth hearing and petitioned the court for writ of habeas corpus when counsel was not provided. The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the legislature did not intend under Cal. Penal. Code §§ 3041.5, 3065 that counsel be provided to all life prisoners at all parole hearings.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner prisoner's petition for writ of habeas corpus and held that petitioner was not entitled to counsel at a hearing for a parole release date under the new case law when he would not have been entitled to counsel under the law in effect when he was sentenced.

 

1033 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Edsel P. v. Superior Court


No. A027598


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


165 Cal. App. 3d 763; 211 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1767



March 18, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its orders denying a detention rehearing and finding petitioner unfit to be dealt with under the Juvenile Court Law, and to conduct a new fitness hearing consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile brought a petition for a writ of mandate to direct the trial court to vacate its orders denying a detention rehearing petition and finding petitioner unfit to be dealt with under the juvenile code. Petitioner claimed he could not be tried as an adult and was improperly detained where the evidence was insufficient that he committed an offense under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(c).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was charged with attempted murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 774 and 187, and assault with a firearm on a peace officer in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(c). The trial court detained petitioner on the basis of a detention warrant information sheet prepared by the probation department. Petitioner filed an application for a detention rehearing and also filed a request for a probable cause hearing. At the fitness hearing, the trial court denied the detention rehearing petition and found petitioner unfit for treatment as a juvenile because petitioner did not meet his burden of proof to overcome the presumption that he was unfit to be dealt with as a juvenile under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(c). Petitioner brought a petition for a writ of mandate to vacate the trial court's order. The appellate court granted the petition. Because petitioner challenged the sufficiency of the evidence that he committed a listed offense under § 707(c), the presumption that he was unfit could not be applied where the state failed to establish a prima facie case. Petitioner was entitled to the same constitutional protections as an adult. A new fitness hearing was ordered.


OUTCOME: The appellate court granted petitioner juvenile's petition for a writ of mandate vacating the trial court's order denying petitioner's motion for a detention rehearing. A new fitness hearing was ordered because the state failed to prove a prima facie case. Further, petitioner challenged the sufficiency of the evidence that he committed a crime which subjected him to trial as an adult. The presumption that petitioner was unfit was inapplicable.

People v. Moore


Crim. No. B001306


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


166 Cal. App. 3d 540; 211 Cal. Rptr. 856; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1856



March 15, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied April 12, 1985. Lillie P. J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1985. Mosk, J., and Lucas, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 801878, Alan B. Haber, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of conviction is affirmed. The judgment in the sanity phase is reversed and the case remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his convictions, entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for three counts of attempted murder and four counts of assault with a deadly weapon, as well as the jury determination that appellant was sane at the time of the crimes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his convictions for three counts of attempted murder and four counts of assault with a deadly weapon, as well as the jury finding that he was sane at the time the crimes were committed. On appeal, appellant urged three grounds for reversal. First, appellant claimed that his confession to a fellow inmate while incarcerated at the county jail was received into evidence in violation of due process because the inmate may have been a police informant. Second, appellant claimed that it was error for the trial court to discharge a regular juror and replace her with an alternate during the sanity phase of appellant's trial. Third, appellant claimed that the trial court erred in refusing a jury instruction which outlined appellant's possible institutionalization if found not guilty by reason of insanity. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction, but reversed the sanity determination and ordered it to be retried. Appellant's sanity proceedings may have been prejudiced by the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury on the consequences of a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions for attempted murder and assault with a deadly weapon were affirmed, but the determination that he was sane at the time of the crimes was reversed for retrial. The sanity proceedings may have been prejudiced by the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury on the consequences of a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity.

Ramey v. Murphy


No. A022622


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


165 Cal. App. 3d 502; 212 Cal. Rptr. 14; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1740



March 12, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 5, 1985, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied June 20, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 773378, Lawrence S. Mana, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that granted judgment in favor of defendant police department, on grounds that arrests made under Cal. Penal Code § 647c were made in bad faith and without probable cause and all material controverted issues were not resolved.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers instituted suit against defendant police department for declaratory and injunctive relief, alleging illegal expenditure of public funds because of defendant's enforcement of Cal. Penal Code § 647c and San Francisco, Cal., Police Code §§ 22 to 27. Defendant relied on § 647c as its main weapon against street prostitution. Plaintiffs contended that § 647c arrests were routinely made in objective bad faith without a reasonable expectation that prosecution or conviction would follow, that the arrests were made without probable cause, and that the trial court failed to resolve material controverted issues. The trial court granted judgment in favor of defendants. The appellate court affirmed and ruled that plaintiffs failed to prove an illegal expenditure of public funds. The court held that the trial court adequately resolved all controverted issues against plaintiffs and that the manner of arrests reflected practices and policies that guarded against acting without probable cause. The court found that the dismissal rates did not per se establish bad faith arrests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for defendant police department in plaintiff taxpayers' suit alleging illegal expenditure of public funds. Prostitution arrests were made under policies that guarded against acting without probable cause, and plaintiffs did not demonstrate that defendant acted with malice, corruption, or intent to harass or interfere with the exercise of protected rights.

 

1036 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Blasquez


No. F001266


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


165 Cal. App. 3d 408; 211 Cal. Rptr. 335; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1728



March 7, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 1, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 20631, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, psychiatrist and his bookkeeper, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), convicting appellants of multiple counts of presenting false Medi-Cal claims, one count of grand theft, and one count of conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a search warrant issued under Cal. Penal Code § 1524(c), appellant psychiatrist's medical office was searched, resulting in the seizure of various documents, including patients' records and billing records. Appellants, psychiatrist and his bookkeeper wife were convicted of presenting false Medi-Cal claims, grand theft, and conspiracy. On review, appellants contended that § 1524(c) deprived appellant psychiatrist of equal protection because it imperiled the careers of psychotherapists and other covered professionals by discouraging potential patients whose records could be scrutinized by the police. The court held the statute did not infringe on the right to work because it did not prevent anyone from practicing their profession and did not it make it more difficult for one to obtain a license needed to practice a profession; at worst, the impact of the statute was very indirect, collateral and marginal. The court held the statute did not violate the right to equal protection because the classification was rationally related to achieving the legitimate state purpose of combating white-collar crime by permitting search of suspects by normal warrant procedure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments convicting appellants, psychiatrist and his bookkeeper wife, because the statute permitting the search of appellant psychiatrist's records when he was suspected of criminal activity did not violate his equal protection rights, since the legislative classification was rationally related to a legitimate state purpose.

February 25, 1985

DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate in appellant's case against respondent employer.


OVERVIEW: While still on probation, appellant was discharged by respondent employer without a hearing before the civil service commission. Claiming that the city charter granted such a hearing, appellant sought a writ of mandate which was denied by the trial court. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that appellant's hearing rights created by the city charter were not vested so as to be immune from modification by the electorate. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant employee's petition for writ of mandate in a case against respondent employer. The court held that appellant had no right to a hearing regarding the termination of employment because the amendment of the city charter eliminated commission review for probationary employees.

Cohen v. Board of Supervisors


No. AO15565.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


164 Cal. App. 3d 886; 210 Cal. Rptr. 867; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1656



February 20, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Apr. 18, 1985 (See 40 Cal. 3d 277)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 784036, Ira A. Brown, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, taxpayer and attorney, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) denying their motion for a preliminary injunction seeking to prevent enforcement of a municipal ordinance regulating escort services.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, taxpayer and attorney, filed suit requesting declarative and injunctive relief, asserting that the municipal ordinance regulating escort services was unconstitutional and void on its face. The trial court summarily denied appellants' application for preliminary injunction. Because the validity of the challenged regulation presented only questions of law, it was therefore appropriate for the court to resolve the merits of the action. In so doing, the court reversed the trial court's decision and held that the escort service ordinance was invalid because it was in conflict with general laws in violation of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 7. The court concluded that there was no suggestion that, in either the police captain's analysis or the legislative history of the ordinance, there was a legislative purpose other than to control prostitution and aid in police investigation of that crime. The court ruled that the ordinance clearly had the substance of a penal statute and impermissibly attempted to expand police investigative powers beyond that encompassed in the California Penal Code.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of appellants', taxpayer and attorney, request for declaratory and injunctive relief and enjoined the enforcement of a municipal ordinance regulating escort services, holding that the substance of the legislation was to regulate criminal sexual conduct, an area which the state has occupied through general laws.

Shannon v. Sims Serv. Ctr.


No. B007549


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


164 Cal. App. 3d 907; 210 Cal. Rptr. 861; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1658



February 20, 1985

Code §1030(d), and an undertaking pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1030(a), (b). The trial court granted the motion and dismissed the action. Appellant sought review. People v. Avalos


Crim. No. 41017.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


164 Cal. App. 3d 850; 211 Cal. Rptr. 15; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1652



February 19, 1985.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-449063, William E. McGinley, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of murder with two special circumstances and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. Defendant was sentenced to state prison for life without possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. As to the murder count, the jury found true the first special circumstance that the murder was committed during or in flight after a robbery and the second special circumstance that the murder was committed in connection with a kidnapping. Defendant was sentenced to state prison for life without possibility of parole. The court reversed the conviction for kidnapping for the purpose of robbery and the finding of the special circumstance that the murder was committed in connection with a kidnapping because the asportation of the victim did not amount to kidnapping and therefore, the finding was not supported by the record. The court held that the deletion of the finding on the second special circumstance did not require reversal or modification of the sentence imposed for murder because a single special circumstance, alleged and found true, was sufficient to support defendant's sentence. The court found that the jury was properly instructed that before it could find the first special circumstance to be true, it had to find that defendant had the intent to kill and that the jury made such finding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for kidnapping for the purpose of robbery and the second special circumstance that the murder was committed in connection with a kidnapping because the asportations of the victim had not amounted to kidnapping and therefore, the finding was not supported by the record. Deletion of the finding on the second special circumstance did not affect defendant's sentence.

 

1042 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Long


Crim. No. F004123


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


164 Cal. App. 3d 820; 210 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1648



February 15, 1985 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Part II of this opinion is not certified for publication. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976 and 976.1.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 22262, Edward Kim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment rendered by the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) that convicted him of committing lewd or lascivious acts upon a child under 14 years old and ordered him to pay restitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of committing lewd or lascivious acts upon a child under 14 years old. The trial court sentenced defendant to prison and ordered him to pay a restitution fine pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 13967. Defendant sought review of the judgement. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in imposing the restitution fine without considering his ability to pay. The California legislature enacted several laws which operated to require trial courts to impose a "restitution fine" on persons convicted of any crime. Cal. Gov't. Code § 13967 provided a structure for the trial courts to impose various levels of restitution. Cal. Penal Code § 1202.4 was also amended to revise restitution provisions. Defendant argued that, were the mandatory language of § 1202.4 (a) given full effect, it would be unconstitutional. The court found defendant's contention unmeritorious and affirmed the judgment. The statute in the government code was different from that in the penal code in several respects and defendant's financial position was not required to be considered under § 13967.


OUTCOME: The trial court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of committing lewd or lascivious acts upon a child under 14 years old and that ordered him to pay restitution. The trial court did not have to consider defendant's ability to pay when imposing the restitution fine.

 

1043 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Travi-Care Corp. v. Hospital & Institutional Workers' Union


No. AO23427.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


165 Cal. App. 3d 598; 211 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1748



February 13, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg den. Apr. 18, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 84691, Ellis R. Randall, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital workers union sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which granted to respondent hospital corporation a preliminary injunction restraining picketing by appellant's members and prohibiting insulting, rude, coercive, or obscene language while picketing.


OVERVIEW: Respondent hospital corporation obtained a preliminary injunction to restrain picketing by appellant hospital workers' union and prohibit language that was insulting, offensive, rude, coercive, or obscene against respondents and persons wishing to do business with the hospital. On review, appellants contended that the language of the injunction was overbroad and unconstitutionally prohibited the exercise of protected rights under U.S. Const. amend I. The court held that freedom of speech meant the right to discuss publicly all matters of public concern without prior restraint or fear of punishment. In the context of labor, any restrictions on picketing had to be couched in the narrowest terms that would accomplish the pin-pointed objective. The court pointed out that not all speech was constitutionally protected. It distinguished between political statements couched in vulgar terms and unprotected obscene expressions that deal with sex in a manner appealing to prurient interests. The court held that the language respondent complained of had no political content. In affirming the trial court, the court held that the injunction was sufficiently precise as to the prohibited speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of an injunction against appellant hospital workers' union prohibiting language during picketing that was insulting, offensive, rude, coercive, or obscene, because such language had no political content.

 

1044 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Glenn


Crim. No. B004285


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


164 Cal. App. 3d 736; 211 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1638



February 13, 1985 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court this opinion is certified for partial publication. [Reporter's Note: The portion published reflects the directions of the court.]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 18875, Bruce A. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order imposing sentence) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed an appeal from judgment of Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which convicted her of 11 counts of forgery and which, in addition to a prison sentence, imposed a fine payable to state restitution fund on count 1 (Cal. Penal Code § 1202.4), contending restitution fine was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to 11 counts of forgery. In addition to a prison sentence, the trial judge imposed a fine payable to the state restitution fund on count 1 (Cal. Penal Code § 1202.4). Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the restitution fine. The court affirmed appellant's sentence. The language of section 1202.4 belied appellant's claim of vagueness. It required the judge to impose on a person convicted of a felony a restitution fine as provided in Cal. Gov't Code § 13967. The court rejected appellant's challenge based on the statute's lack of provisions to enforce her payment. These provisions were found in other statutes. It also rejected appellant's contention that the provision for imposition of the restitution fine regardless of a defendant's present ability to pay constituted an invidious discrimination on the basis of wealth in violation of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Imprisonment of a defendant for failure to pay the restitution fine was prohibited (Cal. Penal Code § 1205.5) and appellant failed to show that imposition of a restitution fine otherwise violated equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's sentence. The statute providing for the restitution fine was not vague and it did not constitute discrimination on the basis of wealth in violation of appellant's rights to equal protection because she could not be imprisoned for failure to pay the fine.

People ex rel. Franchise Tax Bd. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. B003338


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


164 Cal. App. 3d 526; 210 Cal. Rptr. 695; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1619



February 7, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 25, 1985. A petition for a rehearing was denied March 6, 1985, and the petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1985. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding respondent court to vacate its order denying the petition of the FTB for an order compelling respondent Safeco to comply with the subpoena duces tecum issued to and served upon Safeco by the FTB and to make an order compelling such compliance.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner board sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate its order, which denied petitioner's request under Cal. Gov't Code § 11187 for an order compelling real party in interest's compliance with an administrative subpoena duces tecum.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner board sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court to vacate its order denying its petition for an order to compel real party in interest's compliance with its administrative subpoena duces tecum. Petitioner argued that it could not be estopped or enjoined from the collection of any tax, and that the refusal of the respondent to compel compliance with its subpoena, in effect, prevented or enjoined the collection of personal income tax in violation of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 32. Upon examination of the subpoena, the court found that it was duly signed, the demand was not too indefinite, and the information sought was reasonably relevant to the duties of and intended investigation by petitioner. Therefore, the court concluded that the subpoena conformed to the legal standards of Cal. Gov't Code § 11180. Accordingly, a peremptory writ of mandate was issued commanding respondent to vacate its order.


OUTCOME: A peremptory writ of mandate was issued commanding respondent court to vacate its order, which denied petitioner board's request for an order compelling real party in interest to comply with the administrative subpoena duces tecum, because respondent erred in ruling that the subpoena was not regularly issued and that it violated the right to privacy.

People v. Hagerman


Crim. No. F003375.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


164 Cal. App. 3d 368; 213 Cal. Rptr. 286; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1605



February 1, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Parts I and III are not published as they do not meet the standards for publication contained in rule 976(b), California Rules of Court.


Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the County of Fresno, No. 292073-4, Mario Olmos and Gene M. Gomes, Judges. dagger


UNKNOWN  dagger Judge Olmos ruled on the Penal Code section 995 motion and the motion for a psychiatric examination; Judge Gomes presided at the trial of defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Fresnoone (California), which convicted defendant of forcible rape in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), forcible oral copulation in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c), kidnaping in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 207, a lewd and lascivious act upon a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(b), and sodomy by force with a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 286(c).


OVERVIEW: After abducting and assaulting two victims, defendant was convicted of forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), forcible oral copulation in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c), kidnapping in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 207, a lewd and lascivious act upon a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(b), and sodomy by force with a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 286(c). Defendant appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that Proposition 8, Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), dealing with rulings on the admissibility of evidence, had no effect upon Cal. Penal Code § 1112, which prohibited a trial court from ordering a psychiatric examination of sexual assault victims. The court found that the trial court's refusal to order a psychiatric examination of a witness was not tantamount to a Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), ruling on the admissibility of evidence which would be generated as a result of that examination. It found that a motion to enter a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity was not timely when the motion was first made after the rendition of a jury verdict; no case law supported defendant's request to change his plea after the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions because the trial court did not rule on the admissibility of any evidence when it refused to order a psychiatric examination of a victim, and defendant's motion to change his plea to one of insanity was untimely because it was made after the guilty verdict and after the trial court had denied his motion for a new trial.

 

1047 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hagerman


Crim. No. F003375


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


164 Cal. App. 3d 967; 213 Cal. Rptr. 286; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1663



February 1, 1985 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Part I (only) is not published, as it does not meet the standards for publication contained in rule 976(b), California Rules of Court.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified March 1, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 292073-4, Mario G. Olmos and Gene M. Gomes, Judges. +


+ Judge Olmos ruled on the Penal Code section 995 motion and the motion for a psychiatric examination; Judge Gomes presided at the trial of defendant.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Fresno County (California) which, upon a jury verdict, entered convictions against him for one count of forcible rape, four counts of forcible oral copulation, one count of kidnapping, one count of a lewd and lascivious act upon a child under 14, and one count of sodomy by force with a child under 14.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of one count of forcible rape, four counts of forcible oral copulation, one count of kidnapping, one count of a lewd and lascivious act upon a child under 14, and one count of sodomy by force with a child under 14. On appeal, defendant argued that the lower court erred in failing to exercise its discretion in ruling upon defendant's motion requesting it to order a psychiatric examination of one of the victims. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 1112 prevented the lower court from ordering the victim to undergo a psychiatric examination for the purposes of assessing her credibility; furthermore, the court found that Cal Const. art. I, § 28(d) did not repeal § 1112 where it dealt only with the admission and exclusion of known evidence. The court also held that defendant's counsel was not required to explore defendant's mental condition where there was no reason to suspect that an emotion or mental condition may have affected defendant's conduct. Finally, the court found that defendant's motion to enter a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity after the jury's verdict was not timely. Therefore, the court affirmed defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for forcible rape, four counts of forcible oral copulation, kidnapping, lewd and lascivious act upon a child under 14, and sodomy by force with a child under 14, holding, by statute, the lower court could not order the victim to undergo a psychiatric examination in order to assess her credibility, and the statute was not repealed by recent amendment dealing with admission and exclusion of evidence.

 

1048 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Classified Ins. Corp.


Civ. No. F003441


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


164 Cal. App. 3d 341; 210 Cal. Rptr. 162; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1602



January 31, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Madera County, No. 5713, Jack L. Hammerberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment and the order of November 21, 1983, are reversed, and the trial court is directed to vacate the forfeiture and exonerate the undertaking.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant surety sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Madera County (California) denying its motion to vacate bail forfeiture and exonerate bond and entering summary judgment against appellant after defendant failed to appear at a hearing on defendant's motion to set aside the information in an underlying criminal action.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with health code violations. The court set a trial confirmation hearing date and a trial date. Appellant surety posted defendant's bail. Prior to the trial confirmation hearing, defendant's counsel moved to set aside the information. The lower court forfeited defendant's bail because he did not appear. Appellant moved to vacate forfeiture and exonerate bond on grounds that the court had no jurisdiction to order bail forfeiture under Cal. Penal Code § 1305 (§ 1305). Appellant sought review of an adverse summary judgment. The court found that under § 1305, a court had authority to forfeit bail if defendant failed to appear for arraignment, trial, judgment, execution of judgment, or when his presence was otherwise lawfully required without sufficient excuse. Defendant's presence at the hearing on his motion to set aside was not a court-ordered appearance date. Any appearance obligation arose from his contractual relationship with appellant. Cal. Penal Code § 997 requiring defendants' presence at all proceedings was to protect defendant's due process rights. Summary judgment was reversed and the lower court was ordered to vacate forfeiture and exonerate bond.


OUTCOME: Appellant surety's adverse summary judgment and denial of its motion to vacate bond forfeiture and to exonerate bond was reversed. The lower court did not have jurisdiction to forfeit defendant's bail for failing to appear at a hearing on a motion to set aside the information because his presence was not legally required or ordered by the lower court. The lower court was ordered to vacate forfeiture and exonerate the bond.

 

1049 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Weller


No. A027439


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


164 Cal. App. 3d 44; 210 Cal. Rptr. 130; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1579



January 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 22, 1985, and petitioners' applications for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied April 25, 1985. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the applications should be granted.



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of writ of habeas corpus after they were convicted by the trial court (California) of trespass, a misdemeanor, and sentenced to 45 days in jail, suspended upon payment of a $ 150 fine and completion of a period of community service. Petitioners claimed that the trial court erred in barring their defense of necessity.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were convicted of trespass, a misdemeanor, after they entered the property of a missile manufacturer to offer leaflets to entering employees in an attempt to bring an end to the threat of nuclear holocaust. They had been disappointed in the political process and frustrated with lawful means of disseminating antinuclear weapons information. Petitioners sought a writ of writ of habeas corpus on the basis that the trial court erred in barring their defense of necessity. The court denied the writ of habeas corpus on the basis that petitioners' proof was legally insufficient to establish that they lacked an adequate alternative to criminal trespass. The court stated that U. S. Const. amend. I protected the right to lawfully protest any political decision and that neither the political process nor the avenue of lawful protest was an exhaustible remedy for an unwise policy decision.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied petitioners' motion for a writ of habeas corpus, holding that petitioners' proof was insufficient to establish that they lacked an adequate alternative to criminal trespass which would establish the issue of necessity.

McHugh v. Santa Monica Rent Control Bd.


Civ. No. B003191.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


164 Cal. App. 3d 52; 209 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1580



January 25, 1985 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Feb. 22, 1985; Hg. granted Apr. 25, 1985 (See 49 Cal. 3d 348)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC 81313, Lawrence J. Rittenband, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant rent control board sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared Santa Monica, Cal., City Charter § 1809(b), an ordinance that provided an administrative remedy to tenants, here real parties in interest, where plaintiff landlords had charged higher rent than permitted by law, unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Tenants, here real parties in interest, filed administrative complaints against plaintiff landlords that alleged plaintiffs had charged them excess rent. Pursuant to Santa Monica, Cal., City Charter § 1809(b), defendant rent control board ruled in favor of real parties and authorized them to withhold rent. Plaintiffs sought judicial review. The trial court granted summary judgment to plaintiffs and held that § 1809(b) was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that § 1809(b) was not unconstitutional, because the administrative adjudication authorized by the ordinance did not undermine the independence, integrity, or strength of the judiciary. The court stated that the ordinance involved no administrative intrusion into functions traditionally reserved to the courts. The court held that the procedures established by the law were far more conducive to the fulfillment of the legislative goal than a conventional court action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order below and held that the city ordinance was not in violation of the California constitution, because the administrative adjudication authorized by the ordinance did not undermine the independence, integrity or strength of the judiciary.

 

1051 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hampton


No. A019829


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


164 Cal. App. 3d 27; 209 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1578



January 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 22, 1985. White, P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 18, 1985. Bird, C. J., Broussard, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 8299, Richard H. Breiner and Joseph G. Wilson, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that dismissed the charges against respondent of possession of cocaine, driving under the influence of alcohol, driving with 0.10 percent blood alcohol, and resisting a public officer in performing his duties. Appellant argued that the warrantless arrest of respondent was constitutional because it occurred within a reasonable time after observing the driving offense.


OVERVIEW: Appellant charged respondent with possession of cocaine, driving under the influence of alcohol, driving with 0.10 percent blood alcohol, and resisting a public officer in performing his duties. The court reversed the order that dismissed these charges. The superior court incorrectly determined that respondent's misdemeanor arrest under Cal. Penal Code § 836(1) did not occur within a reasonable time after the police officer observed the offenses because less than 15 minutes had intervened. The superior court incorrectly held that the warrantless arrest was improper. The court held that it was constitutionally valid because the officer had probable cause to believe that respondent committed an offense while under the influence of alcohol and the arrest was made pursuant to the exigent circumstances relative to the need to preserve the blood-alcohol evidence. The court ruled that even if this warrantless arrest for a misdemeanor occurred inside respondent's home, it was proper because the officer's entry was justified on the theory of hot pursuit to prevent the dissipation or destruction of evidence. The superior court incorrectly granted respondent's motion to suppress the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that dismissed the charges against respondent of possession of cocaine, driving under the influence of alcohol, driving with 0.10 blood alcohol, and resisting a public officer in performing his duties. The court held that the warrantless arrest of respondent at home occurred within a reasonable time and was constitutional because probable cause existed with exigent circumstances to preserve blood-alcohol evidence.

 

1052 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Craven v. Crout


No. A015572


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


163 Cal. App. 3d 779; 209 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1535



January 17, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 15, 1985, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 207934, Gary E. Strankman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from are reversed, and the trial court is directed to strike them from the record.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) amending an order awarding her damages in a medical malpractice suit and allowing defendant health care providers to make periodic payments of the judgment award over a nine and one-half year period.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant was awarded medical expenses, damages, and future medical expenses by a jury in her medical malpractice suit against respondent health care providers. Two months after entry of judgment, respondents requested that the judgment be amended to allow for periodic payments of the future medical expenses pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7 (§ 667.7). Appellant sought review after the lower court ordered the periodic payments. The court summarily rejected appellant's equal protection argument where it found that the lower courts generally lost unrestricted power to change a judgment once it had been entered, unless statutorily authorized. While § 667.7 provided for periodic payments of future medical expenses damages of $ 50,000 or more, it did not expressly give the lower court the right to amend the judgment after it had been entered and the action was no longer pending. A party's request for periodic payment of future damages had to be made prior to entry of judgment. Because respondents did not timely move for relief, the order periodicizing judgment was void and had no effect. The orders were reversed.


OUTCOME: Orders allowing respondent health care providers to make periodic payments of appellant claimant's future medical expenses awarded to her in a medical malpractice suit were reversed. Respondents requested relief from the lump sum payment after judgment had been entered, and the lower court had no statutory authority to modify the judgment once it had been entered.

Remmenga v. Cal. Coastal Com


Civ. No. B004110


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


163 Cal. App. 3d 623; 209 Cal. Rptr. 628; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1521



January 14, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 17, 1985. A petition for a rehearing was denied February 13, 1985, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1985. Mosk, J., and Lucas, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 141959, Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision below is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied appellants' writ of mandate to compel respondent California Coastal Commission to issue a development permit without payment of the $ 5,000 fee required by Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30610.8.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners filed a petition with respondent California Coastal Commission to construct a single- family residence on their lot within a subdivision. Permission was granted subject to a condition that public access to the coast be provided. A superior court issued a writ of mandate that required respondent to set aside the decision. The legislature then added Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30610.8 which required an in-lieu fee of $ 5,000 for each permit. Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30610.3 provided for fees in lieu of dedication of right of way to the beach. The trial court denied appellants' petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent to issue a development permit without payment of the fee. The court affirmed and held that the legislature had ample basis upon which to conclude that construction of the proposed improvement would have a cumulative adverse impact on the public's constitutional right of access. Appellant's proposed improvement may well be a link in a chain barring access or making access more difficult and costly. Section 30610.8 was not a denial of the equal protection of the laws as guaranteed by state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of respondent California Coastal Commission and against appellant property owners because the fees in lieu of dedication of right of way to the beach were not unconstitutional. The legislature had ample basis to conclude that the construction of the improvement would have a cumulative adverse impact on the public's constitutional right of access.

Solberg v. Wenker


Civ. No. B004680


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


163 Cal. App. 3d 475; 209 Cal. Rptr. 545; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1509



January 8, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 13, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 118016, Thomas R. Adams, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former wife challenged an order by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which quashed a writ of execution by which she sought to collect child support arrearages from respondent former husband.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former wife left her marriage home with her two children without respondent former husband's knowledge in early 1971 or 1972. In July 1977, respondent signed an order for support by stipulation with the district attorney and agreed to monthly child support payments and arrearage. Appellant kept the whereabouts of the children away from respondent and did not allow visitation during this period of time. In 1983, a trial court ruled that appellant had actively sought to conceal the whereabouts of the minor children of the parties from 1972 to the present time and she was estopped from recovering child support payments for that period. The court found that a judgment for child support entered into as a result of an agreement between the district attorney and a parent not represented by counsel was voidable if the parent established that he was not advised of his right to trial and would not have otherwise executed the agreement. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment denying the writ of execution for child support because respondent met this burden.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order denying a writ of execution for child support was affirmed because respondent former husband had no counsel and did not understand the ramifications when he signed the order for support by stipulation. Furthermore, the deliberate sabotage of visitation rights by appellant former wife furnished grounds for modification.

People v. Sutton


No. A023026


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


163 Cal. App. 3d 438; 209 Cal. Rptr. 536; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1505



January 7, 1985 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Parts I and IV are not published, because they do not meet the standards for publication contained in California Rules of Court, rule 976(b).


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied January 30, 1985. As Modified January 30, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. CR 1534, Rothwell B. Mason, Judge. +


+ Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to imposition of the one-year enhancement under Penal Code section 667.5, subdivision (b), and as to imposition of the three-year enhancement under Penal Code section 12022.3, and the cause is remanded for resentencing to permit the trial court to exercise its discretion to strike those enhancements if it so chooses. The judgment is reversed as to imposition of the five-year enhancement under Penal Code section 667. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of conviction in the Superior Court of Lake County (California), for two counts of rape, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), two counts of forcible oral copulation, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a (c), and one count of assault with a deadly weapon, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245 (a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of rape, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), two counts of forcible oral copulation, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a (c), and one count of assault with a deadly weapon, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245 (a). At the outset of trial, defendant admitted a prior serious felony conviction, for which he received a five-year enhancement. Enhancements were also imposed for a prior felony and use of a deadly weapon. The court sentenced defendant to a total of 21 years, including full consecutive sentences for the two acts of forcible oral copulation. On appeal, the court held that the interruption between the two acts of rape was sufficient to constitute separate offenses within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(d) and warrant full consecutive sentences. The court also held that assault with a deadly weapon was not a serious felony under Cal. Penal Code § 1192.7(c)(23) and thus, the five-year enhancement had to be reversed. The judgment as to the remaining enhancements was also reversed and remanded for the court to exercise its discretion to strike them. In all other respects the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction for rape, oral copulation, and assault with a deadly weapon was reversed as to a five-year enhancement, as the assault with a deadly weapon offense was not a serious felony under the controlling statute. Judgment was reversed as to additional enhancements for a prior felony and use of a weapon and the case remanded for resentencing to permit the trial court to exercise its discretion to strike the enhancements.

 

1056 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Boehm v. County of Merced


Civ. No. F003195


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


163 Cal. App. 3d 447; 209 Cal. Rptr. 530; 1985 Cal. App. LEXIS 1506



January 7, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Merced County, No. 72561, Donald R. Fretz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying injunctive relief is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion. Appellants are entitled to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff indigents appealed an order from the Superior Court of Merced County (California) which denied injunctive relief from a reduction in assistance grant levels made by defendants, county and county board, and which denied plaintiffs' due process challenge to defendants' grant assistance termination procedure.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff indigents sought injunctive relief after defendants, county and county board, reduced general assistance grant levels. Plaintiffs argued that the assistance termination procedure violated due process. The trial court denied plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction, but did not rule on plaintiffs' due process challenge, and plaintiffs' appealed. The court held that it was arbitrary and capricious for defendants to reduce the grant levels without a factual determination of their minimum subsistence needs because the determination was necessary to satisfy the statutory mandate in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000, that defendants relieve and support their indigent residents. The court held that the due process argument was not precluded by untimely filed notices of appeal because it was reasonably clear what plaintiffs were trying to appeal from, and defendants were not misled or prejudiced by the notices. The court held that defendants' two-day limitation on filing a request for a hearing on the termination of general assistance was insufficient notice and violated the due process requirements of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying injunctive relief and denying plaintiff indigents' due process challenge to defendants, county and county boards', assistance termination procedure. Defendants were arbitrary and capricious when they reduced aid without making factual determinations of plaintiffs' minimum subsistence needs, and a two-day limitation on filing requests for hearings on aid terminations violated state and federal due process.

 

1057 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Koire v. Metro Car Wash


Civ. No. 30541.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


164 Cal. App. 3d 298; 209 Cal. Rptr. 233; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2916



December 27, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Jan. 10, 1985; Hg. granted Mar. 21, 1985 (See 40 Cal. 3d 24)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 317804, Edward J. Wallin and Richard L. Beacom, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff male customer appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) in favor of defendant businesses in plaintiff's civil rights action. Plaintiff argued the court erred in finding that the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51, did not preclude private businesses from offering price discounts based on gender.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff male customer filed a civil rights suit against defendant businesses alleging that they violated the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), Cal. Civ. Code § 51, by offering gender-based price discounts. The trial ruled in favor of defendants, finding that "Ladies Day" promotions were simply intended to encourage women to patronize defendants' establishments and that there was no showing that the prices paid by men were arbitrary, unreasonable, or unfair. Plaintiff appealed. The appeals court affirmed, finding no per se violation. The court ruled that the Act only prohibited arbitrary discrimination. The court noted that there was no discrimination involving defendant car washes because they all allowed an identical discount to men and women on different days of the week. Moreover, there was ample evidence to demonstrate that the discounts served an independent business purpose. The court noted evidence that more, not fewer, men attended defendant nightclub on the nights women were offered free admission. Thus, because the price schemes were not designed to and did not discourage a segment of the population from patronizing defendants' establishments, they did not violate the Act.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the trial court judgment which found that the Unruh Civil Rights Act did not preclude defendant businesses from offering gender-based price discounts. The court ruled that because the pricing policies did not have the effect of excluding or discouraging a segment of the population from patronizing defendants' businesses, there was no arbitrary discrimination.

 

1058 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re James D.


No. G000443.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


164 Cal. App. 3d 515; 209 Cal. Rptr. 244; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2917



December 27, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 21, 1985 (L.A. 32050)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-118402, James G. Merwin, Temporary Judge. * 


*  Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) dismissing a petition that charged defendant juvenile with possession of LSD after defendant's motion to suppress the contraband was granted. The state argued that the detention for suspicion of truancy was proper, and hence the evidence was properly seized during a pat-down search.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was stopped by police while walking on a sidewalk at 10:30 a.m. because the police believed him to be truant from school. Police discovered LSD in defendant's possession during a pat-down search. The state brought a juvenile proceeding against defendant, charging him with possession of LSD. The trial court dismissed the petition after granting defendant's motion to suppress. The state appealed. The appeals court affirmed. The court ruled that truancy stops based on mere suspicion or even probable cause did not permissibly fit into the mixed bag of "proper reasons" or "innumerable miscellaneous tasks" police officers are authorized and expected to perform, nor could they be justified on some statutory ground. The court further ruled that there is no lawful basis for a police detention for truancy on other than actual knowledge of a student's truant status before the encounter. Thus, because the police in this case acted only to investigate garden variety truancy, rather than actual truancy, the detention was improper.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the dismissal of the petition charging defendant juvenile with possession of LSD. The court ruled that because an improper sidewalk detention for suspicion of truancy led to the search and seizure of evidence from defendant's person, the motion to suppress was properly granted.

 

1059 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Maldonado v. Superior Court


No. A024351


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


162 Cal. App. 3d 1259; 209 Cal. Rptr. 199; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2873



December 21, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 17, 1985. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 14, 1985. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding respondeat superior court to set aside its order denying petitioner a trial by jury and to permit the matter of Robert A. Imhoff v. Sue Ann Maldonado, S.C.A. Case No. 16272, to proceed to jury trial. The stay heretofore imposed shall be dissolved upon finality of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, premises occupant, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), to grant her a trial by jury on her appeal de novo from a judgment rendered against her in an unlawful detainer action brought in the small claims division of the municipal court.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest filed an unlawful detainer action against petitioner, premises occupant, in small claims court that sought a judgment for possession of the premises at issue. Judgment was rendered against petitioner in small claims court. Petitioner sought a jury trial de novo on appeal. Respondent, The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, raised the issue of petitioner's entitlement to a jury trial. Petitioner sought writ relief to compel respondent to conduct a jury trial. The court granted the writ relief requested because the court found Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 592 to be clear and unambiguous in its provision for trial by jury in actions for the recovery of real property. The court held that Cal. R. Ct. 155, which required that a trial in superior court on appeal from a judgment from municipal court be conducted informally, was not inconsistent with a trial by jury in small claims appeals in unlawful detainer actions.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner, premises occupant's, request for writ relief to compel respondent, the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, to allow petitioner a jury trial because a party was entitled to a trial de novo by jury on appeal from an unlawful detainer judgment rendered in the small claims court.

Civil Serv. Com v. Superior Court


No. D001619


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


163 Cal. App. 3d 70; 209 Cal. Rptr. 159; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2881



December 21, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 11, 1985. The petition of real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 27, 1985.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling the superior court to order the office of San Diego County Counsel disqualified from further participation in this case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Civil Service Commission of San Diego County sought review of the decision of respondent trial court, the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which refused to disqualify the office of San Diego County counsel from the proceedings between petitioner and the county regarding petitioner's action to resolve a personnel dispute in another county department.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Civil Service Commission of San Diego County was responsible for administering the county's personnel system and had a considerable degree of independence in this regard. Petitioner investigated complaints from two former employees of another county department, and petitioner consulted with county counsel who also represented the other department. Consultations involved the extent of petitioner's authority to correct any violations and the status of the investigation. The county sought review of petitioner's remedy and used the same attorney who had consulted with petitioner. The court found that petitioner established an attorney-client relationship with the county attorney since petitioner operated with independence from the county. The attorney's actual possession of confidential information was irrelevant and disqualification was appropriate because a substantial relationship existed between the current and former representations. The court held that the county attorney who advised petitioner could not represent the county in a suit with petitioner over the same issue; therefore, the county's counsel office was prohibited from further participation.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the trial court to disqualify the county counsel's office from representing the county the proceedings between the county and petitioner county civil service commission because petitioner had established an attorney-client relationship with the county counsel's office and a substantial relationship existed between the current and former representations.

Jeneski v. Myers


Civ. No. B001328


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


163 Cal. App. 3d 18; 209 Cal. Rptr. 178; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2876



December 21, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 28, 1984. Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1985. Broussard, J., and Lucas, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 423494, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of the trial court are reversed. The case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Medi-Cal recipients, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), which refused to extend a preliminary injunction preventing respondents, Department of Health Services, its director, and the governor, from implementing a statute and regulations that deleted certain drugs from a list of those for which Medi-Cal would reimburse and putting others on a list requiring prior authorization.


OVERVIEW: Appellants Medi-Cal recipients sought to enjoin respondents, Department of Health Services, its director, and the governor, from implementing a statute that deleted certain drugs from a list of those for which Medi-Cal would reimburse and put others on a list for which prior authorization was required. The trial court entered a preliminary injunction enjoining respondents from removing any drugs from the Medi-Cal program until the completion of public hearings. Appellants challenged the trial court's judgment dissolving the preliminary injunction and denying appellants' request for modification. The court found that the preliminary injunction dissolved under its own terms. Appellants did not present a change in the controlling facts to support modification of the injunction, but they established that irreparable harm would occur if the injunction was not extended and respondents were allowed to illegally deny drugs. The court found legal flaws in the current system that compelled a finding that appellants were likely to prevail at trial; therefore, the court reversed the judgment and ordered the preliminary injunction extended in time and to include the prior approval system.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for the trial court to extend the preliminary injunction to include the prior approval system and to extend the time of the injunction because irreparable harm would occur if appellants were illegally denied drugs and the evidence showed that appellants were likely to prevail at trial.

People v. Ross


No. B001963.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


162 Cal. App. 3d 1043; 209 Cal. Rptr. 407; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2850



December 19, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Jan. 4, 1985; Hg. den. Mar. 14, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. AO26977, Ernest L. Kelly, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266i and sentenced him to probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266i and was sentenced to probation. The state appealed the probation sentence. The court concluded that because the effect of the trial court's order was to modify the verdict by reducing the statutorily required punishment, the order was appealable under Cal. Penal Code § 1238(a)(6). The state contended the trial court had erred in determining that a three-year minimum state prison confinement was unconstitutional because it constituted cruel or unusual punishment. The court concluded that the three-year minimum prison term for pandering did not constitute cruel or unusual punishment. The court held that the trial court had exceeded its authority in substituting its judgment for that of the legislature as to the appropriateness of the mandatory three-year length of imprisonment. The court affirmed the order granting probation, because to resentence defendant to a three year prison term under the challenged law, under the unique circumstances of the case, would be unnecessarily harsh.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order sentencing defendant to probation after he was convicted of pandering. Even though the trial court had exceeded its authority in granting defendant probation, to resentence defendant to the mandatory three-year prison term would be unnecessarily harsh under the circumstances of the case.

 

1063 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Johnson


No. A024945


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


162 Cal. App. 3d 1003; 209 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2846



December 19, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1985. Mosk, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 111772, Laurence Donald Kay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted defendant's motions to suppress evidence found in a warrantless search under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, and to dismiss charges of possession of cocaine under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, possession of marijuana for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359, and possession of a firearm under Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after police seized marijuana, cocaine, and a gun from a briefcase that was located between defendant and another man in the front seat of a car. Defendant was charged with possession of cocaine under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, possession of marijuana for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359, and possession of a firearm by a felon under Cal. Penal Code § 12021. Defendant filed a motion to suppress the evidence, which the trial court granted and dismissed the case. The court held that under Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), the exclusion of evidence in California conformed to federal practices that held that a court could not suppress evidence unless a defendant showed a personal, reasonable, and legitimate expectation of privacy in the place searched or the evidence seized. The court found that defendant made no such showing and, therefore, reversed the trial court's decision to grant the motion. The court also held that the exclusion of evidence did not implicate defendant's constitutional right against self-incrimination or his rights to a fair trial because he needed only establish that an illegal search invaded his own expectation of privacy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained after a warrantless search and held that defendant failed to make a showing that he had a personal, reasonable, and legitimate expectations of privacy in the front seat of the car where the evidence was seized.

 

1064 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Carroll v. State Bar


Div. No. 31635.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


162 Cal. App. 3d 1094; 209 Cal. Rptr. 740; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2852



December 19, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Pursuant to the Supreme Ct.'s order of Mar. 14, 1985, the Ct. App. vacated its opn. and refiled that opn. on Apr. 4, 1985. (See 166 Cal. App. 3d 1193). Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N22139, Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C455587, Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state bar of California sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ruled that the Funds for the Provision of Legal Services to the Indigent Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6210 et seq., established a voluntary, rather than mandatory, program for depositing nominal client trust funds in an aggregated account from which the interest was used to fund approved indigent legal services.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state bar of California challenged the decision of the lower court, which held that the Funds for the Provision of Legal Services to the Indigent Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6211(b), permitted lawyers to deposit nominal, short-term funds in interest-bearing accounts other than the Interest on Lawyer Trust Account Program (IOLTA), at the request of their clients. The court reversed and held that § 6211(a) was all inclusive, and clear and unambiguous in that it required nominal or short-term deposits of client trust funds to be placed in an interest-bearing trust account and any interest earned to be used to fund designated, specified legal services. The court held that § 6211(a) was intended to be mandatory. The court held that § 6211(a) was not unconstitutionally vague because it permitted appellant with approval of the supreme court to formulate and enforce rules of professional conduct pertaining to use of client funds. The court held that § 6211(a) was not a violation of U.S. Const. amend. V where it was construed as mandatory, because one must have more than an abstract need or desire to invoke the protections afforded by the Fifth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that the Funds for the Provision of Legal Services to the Indigent Act (Act) was voluntary rather than mandatory. The court held that the Act was clear and unambiguous in that it required nominal deposits of client funds to be placed in interest bearing trust accounts and any interest earned to be used for designated specified legal services.
