Bmw of N. Am. v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


Civ. No. 23195


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


162 Cal. App. 3d 980; 209 Cal. Rptr. 50; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2843



December 18, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied January 14, 1985, and the petitions of all respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied March 13, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 309794, William A. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a peremptory writ of mandate directing the respondent New Motor Vehicle Board to vacate its decision granting the protest of Hal Watkins Chevrolet, Inc. doing business as Hal Watkins BMW, and to issue a new decision denying said protest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant franchisor challenged the denial of a petition for a writ of administrative mandate pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 3000 et seq. in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), in an action regarding a decision by respondent New Motor Vehicle Board, which allowed a protest of respondent franchisee to prevent the establishment of a new automobile dealership.


OVERVIEW: Appellant franchisor sought a writ of administrative mandate directing respondent New Motor Vehicle Board (board) to vacate a decision in favor of respondent franchisee, who had a franchise agreement with appellant for an automobile dealership. After a market study, appellant decided to grant a franchise of the same line-make to a prospect over 15 miles away from respondent franchisee's place of business. Respondent franchisee filed a protest with respondent board, which adopted the ruling that the prospective franchise appointment constituted a modification of respondent franchisee's agreement without good cause. The trial court denied appellant's petition. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the matter to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandate. Respondent board acted in excess of its jurisdiction and erred in allowing the protest. The proposed dealership was outside of the relevant market defined in Cal. Veh. Code § 3062, which limited the right of an existing franchisee to object to the appointment of a new dealer to a 10-mile radius. The appointment of a new dealership did not constitute a modification of the existing agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which denied a writ of administrative mandate over respondent New Motor Vehicle Board's allowance of a protest to the establishment of a new automobile dealership, and remanded the case for the issuance of a writ of mandate. The court held that respondent acted in excess of its jurisdiction in allowing the protest of respondent franchisee, therefore, its decision was void.

 

1066 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aston


Crim. No. 11261.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


162 Cal. App. 3d 658; 208 Cal. Rptr. 754; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2816



December 13, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 13, 1985 (See 39 Cal. 3d 481)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Siskiyou County, No. 32291, James E. Kleaver, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions, entered by the Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California), for unlawful possession for sale of cocaine, a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed their convictions for unlawful possession for sale of cocaine, a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351. The trial court instructed the jury that it was unlawful to possess a controlled substance such as cocaine for sale. The people's chemical evidence showed only that the substance was simply cocaine. This led the jury to presuppose that the controlled substance statute regulated a discrete substance called cocaine, which was the substance possessed by defendants. The presupposition was false. Defendants requested an instruction that informed the jury that only one form of cocaine, l-cocaine, was a controlled substance. The trial court refused to give the instruction. On appeal the court reversed the convictions as a result of the trial court's failure to give the jury defendants' requested instruction. The court found that as a consequence, an element of the offense was effectively removed from jury consideration and proof by the people and was, therefore, reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions for possession for sale of cocaine because the trial court erred in not giving the requested jury instruction that only one form of cocaine was a controlled substance. The lack of that instruction provided that an element of the offense was removed from jury consideration and proof by the people.

 

1067 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Carrillo


No. A024211


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


162 Cal. App. 3d 585; 208 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2809



December 12, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 9602, Richard M. Silver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As in Lehr v. Robertson, supra, 463 U.S. 248, 260 [77 L.Ed.2d 614, 626, we conclude that due to the entirely different nature of their respective relationships with the child, the natural mother and the nonpresumed natural father are not similarly situated. The mother in this case continued the care and nurturing of the child following her birth; the father, on the other hand, provided none of the care and support associated with parenting. "'Parental rights do not spring full-blown from the biological connection between parent and child. They require relationships more enduring.'" ( Lehr v. Robertson, supra, 463 U.S. 248, 260 [77 L.Ed.2d 614, 626], quoting Caban v. Mohammed, supra, 441 U.S. at p. 396 [60 L.Ed.2d at p. 310], dis. opn. of Stewart, J., italics in original.) Due to appellant's failure to establish any parental relationship with the child, we hold that the difference in the rights accorded by Civil Code section 197 to mothers and nonpresumed fathers does not infringe appellant Victor Carrillo's right to equal protection of the laws and that there is no constitutional [***2] reason he may not be prosecuted for violation of Penal Code section 278. Accordingly, the judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, father and his wife, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) which convicted them of child stealing. The lower court found that defendant father was not a presumed father under Cal. Civ. Code § 7004. Consequently, the court concluded, he had no right to custody and therefore could be prosecuted under Cal. Penal Code § 278 for child stealing, as he was.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, father and wife, were convicted of child stealing under Cal. Penal Code § 278. Defendant father had denied the nonmarital child's paternity, but admitted it after blood tests indicated he was the father. Defendants challenged the constitutionality of § 278 applied in conjunction with Cal. Civ. Code § 197, claiming it denied them equal protection. Section 197 accorded different rights to mothers and nonpresumed fathers. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. Defendant wife had no rights to the child and could not act as defendant father's agent. Since defendant father was merely the alleged natural father of the child and not her presumed natural father, § 197 operated to deprive him of custody rights in the child commensurate with those accorded the mother. Because defendant father failed to establish any relationship with the child, the court held that that the difference in the rights accorded by § 197 to mothers and nonpresumed fathers did not infringe defendant father's right to equal protection and that there was no constitutional reason he could not be prosecuted for violation of § 278.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment convicting defendants, father and wife, of child stealing. Because defendant father failed to establish any parental relationship with the child, the court held that the difference in the rights for mothers and nonpresumed fathers did not infringe his right to equal protection and there was no constitutional reason not to prosecute him.

 

1068 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Flowers


No. AO24244.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


162 Cal. App. 3d 286; 208 Cal. Rptr. 268; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2736



November 30, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Dec. 21, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 111130, Robert L. Dossee, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, and sentenced him to a state prison term of 10 years, which reflected the sentence for the robbery and two enhancements which were run concurrently.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of one count of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. Appellant was then sentenced to a prison term of 10 years. The trial court sentenced appellant to five years for the robbery and to two five year enhancements, which the trial court then ran concurrently instead of consecutively. Appellant challenged his conviction and sentence. The court affirmed the conviction and held that there was no ex post facto application when additional terms of punishment under the enhancement provision of Cal. Penal Code § 667 were imposed, so appellant was not exposed to double jeopardy, and appellant was not denied equal protection. The court, however, held that the trial court erred when it ordered the two five year terms to run concurrently because the electorate had indicated with sufficient clarity that the mandatory provisions of § 667 were not subject to judicial discretion pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385. Therefore the court ordered that appellant's sentence was to be modified to reflect consecutively imposed enhancements, which made appellant's sentence 15 years instead of the original 10.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for robbery but modified appellant's sentence to reflect consecutively imposed enhancements, adding 10 years instead of 5 years to the sentence. The sentence that appellant should have received was mandatory and the trial court did not have the discretion to order the enhancements to run concurrently.

 

1069 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Graco, Inc. v. Colberg


Civ. No. 23432


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


162 Cal. App. 3d 322; 208 Cal. Rptr. 465; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2740; 1986 AMC 1086



November 30, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied December 27 and 28, 1984, and appellants' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied February 13, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 156915, Michael N. Garrigan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, manufacturer and retailer, challenged the decision from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which sustained the demurrer of respondent employer and denied appellants' cross-complaints against respondent for contribution and indemnity pursuant to the Federal Longshoremen's and Harbor Workers' Compensation Act, 33 U.S.C.S. § 901, et seq.


OVERVIEW: Decedent worked for respondent employer and was engaged in painting a barge. While painting, decedent used a spray painter produced by appellant manufacturer and sold to respondent by appellant retailer. There was an explosion in the work area, killing decedent. Decedent's estate filed a complaint for damages against appellant for wrongful death. Appellants cross-complained against each other. The trial court found that federal maritime law governed, and that maritime law precluded a cross-action for indemnity by third party tortfeasors against an employer who paid benefits pursuant to the Federal Longshoremen's and Harbor Workers' Compensation Act (LHWCA), 33 U.S.C.S. § 901 et seq. On review, the court found that because the barge upon which the accident occurred was not upon navigable water, the trial court erred in determining that maritime law governed all aspects of the case. However, the court still found that LHWCA was applicable. Accordingly, the court found that because of the exclusiveness of LHWCA, there could not an action against a maritime employer based upon its alleged negligence as a basis for contribution. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Trial court's decision which sustained the demurrer of respondent employer and denied appellants' cross-complaints against respondent for contribution and indemnity pursuant to federal workers' compensation act was affirmed. There could be no liability for contribution or indemnity on respondent because of the exclusiveness of the federal act, which protected respondent from liability.

 

1070 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Barenfeld v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. B002735


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


162 Cal. App. 3d 1035; 209 Cal. Rptr. 8; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2849



November 29, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 412381, C 422459, C 422425, C 425583, and C 432156, John L. Cole and Leon Savitch, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's granting of the City's motion for summary judgment and denial of the Plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners of buildings classified as high risk in an earthquake appealed the summary judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for defendant city and its refusal of their motion for a preliminary injunction in the suit they brought arguing Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 91.6801 et seq. (1981) was an unreasonable exercise of the police power because it took private property for public use without compensation.


OVERVIEW: After Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 91.6801 et seq. (1981) required earthquake hazard reductions, plaintiff owners of buildings classified as high risk brought suit against defendant city, arguing the ordinance was an unreasonable exercise of the police power because it effectively took private property for public use without compensation. Affirming the summary judgment for defendant, the court explained that, while a party's declarations in opposition to a motion for summary judgment normally only needed to raise an issue fact in order to defeat the motion, the trial court, faced with the standard of review for exercise of the police power, had an obligation to uphold the validity of the ordinance. Rejecting plaintiffs' argument that the trial court should have granted their request for a preliminary injunction, the court noted that the trial court correctly concluded that plaintiffs were not likely to prevail on the merits. The court added that it could not have been disputed that the trial court correctly balanced the hardships of potential financial loss and potential loss of life.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment for defendant city in the suit plaintiff owners of buildings classified as high risk in earthquakes brought, arguing that the ordinance requiring risk reduction was an unreasonable exercise of police power. The court explained the ordinance was within the scope of defendant's police power and the trial court correctly denied plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction.

 

1071 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cohan v. Alvord


No. B003698


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


162 Cal. App. 3d 176; 208 Cal. Rptr. 421; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2798



November 28, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 000673, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment for respondents is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property taxpayers appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendants, various taxing agencies, on plaintiffs' lawsuit challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 5151 as it applied to pay interest to property owners who earned more than $ 10 in interest on reassessed property values.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property taxpayers were part of a class that challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 5151. Under that section, the state paid interest to property owners who had their property reduced in assessed value. However, it was limited to those that earned $ 10 or more in interest. Plaintiffs earned less. The trial court granted defendants taxing agencies' motion for summary judgment. The court of appeals affirmed, rejecting plaintiffs' equal protection argument. Plaintiffs bore a heavy burden on establishing the unconstitutionality of a taxing classification. States were provided latitude in this area in order to promote convenience and the legitimate interest in collecting taxes. By setting a level at $ 10, the state was promoting administrative efficiency by not having to send a refund to those who earned miniscule interest. Nor was there any due process violation. U.S. Const. amend. V did not guarantee the right to judicial review of tax liability before payment. Plaintiffs had notice and an opportunity to be heard because they were provided hearings which resulted in refunds. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of defendants, various taxing agencies, was affirmed after plaintiff property taxpayers challenged the constitutionality of a taxing scheme which would not pay interest on reassessed property that earned less than $ 10. The statute promoted administrative convenience and did not violate plaintiffs' equal protection or procedural due process rights.

 

1072 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hoffman


Crim. No. 15765.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


162 Cal. App. 3d 376; 208 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2744



November 21, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Dec. 3, 1984; Hg. den. Feb. 14, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 62974, Michael I. Greer, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) convicting him of burglary pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant contended that the trial court improperly denied his motion in limine to prevent his impeachment with evidence of a prior conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of the trial court's refusal under Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(f) to prevent the state's use of his prior felony conviction to impeach his testimony in his charge for burglary. The court reversed the trial court's judgment because defendant's prior rape conviction was not relevant to impeach his credibility. The court held that only those prior felony convictions which were relevant to the witness' credibility could be used to impeach. The court held, under established rules of statutory construction, the language of Cal. Const. art I, § 28(f) which provided for the subsequent use of any prior felony conviction "without limitation for purposes of impeachment" did not eliminate relevancy as a prerequisite to admitting prior felony convictions for impeachment. A contrary interpretation created a significant constitutional due process problem because the control of evidence was central to ensuring a defendant's fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the defendant's conviction for burglary because the trial court improperly admitted evidence of defendant's prior conviction which was not relevant to defendant's credibility.

 

1073 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re LeDay


No. AO27744.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


161 Cal. App. 3d 1010; 208 Cal. Rptr. 8; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2727



November 19, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 14, 1985 (Crim. 24345)
Nov. 27, 1985, matter retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 5 for reconsideration in light of In re Ramirez (1985) 39 Cal. 3d 931.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, whose initial imprisonment was based on a crime committed before the effective date of Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c), which authorized extensions of a parole violator's recommitment terms for new misconduct, filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus after a California trial court postponed his release date for an act of misconduct committed in February 1984.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was sentenced to prison in October 1981, released on parole in April 1983, and recommitted to prison in July 1983 for parole violation with a release date of June 17, 1984. In April 1984 petitioner's release was postponed to August 1984 upon a finding that he had committed an act of misconduct in February 1984. Petitioner filed a petition for habeas corpus upon the contention that as applied to him, Cal. Penal Code § 3057(c), which was passed into law on January 1, 1984, was an unconstitutional ex post facto law. The court found that the provision of § 3057, regulating the length of time for which a parolee may be recommitted for violation of his parole, patently defined a legal consequence that attached to the crime for which the prisoner was incarcerated. The court found that as applied to petitioner, whose initial imprisonment was based on a crime committed before the effective date of § 3057(c), § 3057(c) was an unconstitutional ex post facto provision. Therefore, petitioner was entitled to be released from prison on parole on June 17, 1984. The time he spent in prison after that date could not be applied to increase his period of parole.


OUTCOME: The California law at issue, which authorized the extensions of parole violators' recommitment terms for new misconduct, was found to be an unconstitutional ex post facto law as applied to a parolee, such as petitioner, whose initial imprisonment was based on a crime committed before the law was enacted. Therefore, petitioner was entitled to be released from prison on parole on June 17, 1984.

 

1074 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. McDermand


Crim. No. 23406


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


162 Cal. App. 3d 770; 211 Cal. Rptr. 773; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2725



November 19, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1, California Rules of Court, this opinion is certified for publication, with the exception of issues IV through VIII, and X and XI.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied December 18, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 7722, Henry J. Broderick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), denying his motion for a new trial on his conviction of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, with special circumstances by lying in wait, claiming the special circumstance finding was not supported by the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, with special circumstances, by means of lying in wait. Defendant motioned for a new trial, asserting that the evidence of special circumstances was insufficient to justify imposing the death penalty, and that the trial court had erred in admitting defendant's confession and in deferring the state's cross examination of a defense witness until after defendant had testified. The trial court denied defendant's motion, but reduced defendant's sentence to life imprisonment without possibility of parole. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that defendant's extrajudicial statements could support a charge of special circumstances. The court found that the trial court's deferral of the cross-examination was harmless error, as the defense was based on the psychiatrist's testimony that defendant suffered from diminished capacity, and the witness would have been called regardless of defendant's testimony. Cal. R. Evid. 772. The court further found that the representations of the sheriff's department were limited to effecting defendant's surrender, not to produce a confession, and defendant's confession was voluntary.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of murder with special circumstances was affirmed, because his extrajudicial statements were sufficient to establish lying in wait, and defendant's confession was voluntary and not improperly induced by a sheriff who was merely trying to encourage defendant to surrender.

 

1075 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Valladares


Crim. No. B002491


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


162 Cal. App. 3d 312; 208 Cal. Rptr. 604; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2739



November 14, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 24, 1985. Broussard, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 903812, William A. Ross, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide that defendant is to receive 194 days of custody credits and 97 days of behavior credits, for a total of 291 days of credit. The superior court is directed to prepare an amended abstract of judgment to reflect the correct credits and to transmit a copy of the amended judgment to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, with the use of a knife, Cal. Penal Code § 12022, and sentence for three years, plus a one year enhancement for the weapon use.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, with use of a knife in the commission of the crime, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b). Defendant was sentenced to state prison for three years, plus an additional one-year enhancement for the weapon use. Defendant was given credit for days of presentence custody and conduct credit. Defendant challenged the conviction and sentence on the grounds that the trial court abused its discretion when it refused to grant defendant a continuance to secure attendance of a witness and the court miscalculated his custody credits. The court affirmed the conviction, but modified the sentence. The court held that the defendant did not meet any of the requirements to warrant grant of continuance. Balancing the benefit which defendant purported to anticipate against the likelihood that such benefit would result, the trial court was well within its discretion in denying the continuance. Also, the court found that defendant was not eligible for benefits of programs in which he did not participate, but his sentence credit incorrectly calculated. The sentence was modified to reflect the correct days for which defendant deserved credit.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified so that defendant was provided with more days of credit for behavior because the court incorrectly computed the credits he should have been awarded. The judgment was otherwise affirmed because the evidence was insufficient to support defendant's claim of error.

 

1076 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCowan


Crim. No. 12853.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


161 Cal. App. 3d 822; 208 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2712



November 13, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 27, 1985 (See 182 Cal. App. 3d 1)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 64599, Steven H. Rodda, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his sentence and convictions for first-degree murder, second-degree murder, and attempted murder from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California). Appellant asserted that Cal. Penal Code §§ 25, 28, and 29, which limited mental capacity evidence, were unconstitutional and relieved state's burden of proof and that trial court improperly instructed jury on insanity defense.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's convictions and sentence for first degree-murder, second-degree murder, and attempted murder. Appellant shot his estranged wife and her parents during an major episode of depression. The trial court prohibited appellant, citing Cal. Penal Code §§ 25, 28, and 29, from asking expert witnesses whether appellant had the requisite mental state at the time of the commission of the crimes. The court rejected appellant's argument the rulings violated due process rights, equal protection rights, the right to compulsory process, and unconstitutionally relieved the prosecution of its burden of proving all elements of the offenses. The court held that the restrictions imposed by the statutes were constitutional public policy determinations that eliminated capacity evidence and did not preclude appellant from presenting his defense. The court found that the trial court properly instructed the jury on the definition of the insanity defense by requiring that appellant prove both prongs of the M'Naghten standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions and sentence. The court held that the statutes were constitutional public policy determinations to limit capacity evidence and didn't deprive appellant of his right to put on a defense or relieve the state's burden of proof. The court held that the trial court properly instructed the jury that appellant was required to prove both prongs of the M'Naghten test in order to establish insanity defense.

 

1077 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Banks v. Bd. of Pharm.


Civ. No. B003693


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


161 Cal. App. 3d 708; 207 Cal. Rptr. 835; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2701



November 8, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 456704, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensed pharmacist and pharmacy sought review of the denial by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of their petition for writ of mandate forbidding respondent Board of Pharmacy from imposing three years of probation for negligence in their failure to insure adequate security and inventory control of four dangerous drugs.


OVERVIEW: After respondent Board of Pharmacy found mistakes in its audit of dangerous drugs in the records appellant licensed pharmacist and pharmacy kept, it imposed a three-year probation for negligence. Appellant sought review of the superior court's refusal to issue a writ of mandate against respondent's order. Affirming the decision, the court found sufficient evidence of negligent failure of security with an outside theft that appellants failed to report to respondent as the law required, and repeated thefts by employees. The court did not pass on the constitutionality of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11208 because there was sufficient independent evidence of guilt. Rejecting appellants' U.S. Const. amend XIV argument, the court said equal protection rights were not offended because the legislature could have reasonably believed that a pharmacist was more capable of safeguarding the drugs. The court added that under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code 4350.5, disciplinary repercussion could have flowed from violations of federal law. Finally, the court found no prejudice from respondent's refusal to admit the results of polygraph tests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment refusing the petition of appellant licensed pharmacist and pharmacy for a writ of mandate against respondent Board of Pharmacy's order of a three-year probation for negligence in safeguarding dangerous drugs and keeping records. The court held there was sufficient evidence to support the order because of outside and employee theft and inadequate record keeping, and appellants' rights were not violated.

 

1078 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Nguyen


No. A023302


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


161 Cal. App. 3d 687; 207 Cal. Rptr. 870; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2698



November 7, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 27735, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgement of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of unlawfully taking salmon and striped bass by use of a gillnet under Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8685.5 and possessing those illegally taken fish for sale under Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8685.6.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of unlawfully taking salmon and striped bass by use of a gillnet, Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8685.5, and possessing those illegally taken fish for sale, Cal. Fish & Game Code § 8685.6. Defendant appealed his conviction and claimed that because the statutes did not define "gill net", they were unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed and held that though the Fish and Game Code did not specifically define gill net, §§ 8685.5, 8685.6 were not invalidated under the void-for-vagueness doctrine because the term gill net had a long established and commonly accepted meaning that provided fair warning. The court found that §§ 8685.5, 8685.6 were also not unconstitutionally vague because defendant's conduct was squarely encompassed within a reasonable and practical construction of the term. The court determined that one of ordinary intelligence having fair notice of the species of fish protected, and of the prohibited method of taking them, would in the exercise of common sense have fair warning that use of certain nets might result in the forbidden taking of those fish by that method.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for illegally taking fish by gill netting and selling such fish, because the statutes under which he was convicted were not void for vagueness. Though the statutes did not define the term "gill net," there was a commonly accepted meaning which provided defendant with fair warning.

People v. Lovedy


Crim. No. 13238.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


162 Cal. App. 3d 499; 208 Cal. Rptr. 591; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2756



November 5, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Dec. 5, 1984; Hg. den. Jan. 17, 1985, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 61878, Roger K. Warren, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that upheld a jury verdict that found defendant guilty of forcible sodomy with a child under age 14 and sentenced defendant to 32 years of imprisonment after mental treatment was deemed to be of no benefit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant committed sodomy on two young boys and was convicted of two counts of forcible sodomy with a child under the age of 14 in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 286(c). Defendant was adjudged a mentally disordered sex offender (MDSO) and committed to the state hospital. After a period of treatment, it was determined that defendant was a danger to the health and safety of others and would not benefit from further care and treatment. The trial court then sentenced defendant to 32 years imprisonment that reflected consecutive upper terms for all offenses. The court vacated the sentence and remanded, but affirmed the conviction, holding that it was not clear that the trial court was aware that an alternative mode of consecutive sentencing was available. Withholding conduct credits from a terminated, sentenced MDSO for time spent in treatment did not deny equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction but remanded the sentence holding that withholding conduct credits from a terminated, sentenced MDSO for time spent in treatment did not deny equal protection. It was not clear the trial court was aware alternative consecutive sentencing existed, however.

 

1080 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Helmquist


Crim. No. 15696


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


161 Cal. App. 3d 609; 207 Cal. Rptr. 718; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2690



November 2, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Certified for publication with the exception of section III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied November 26, 1984, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1985. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 63259, Kenneth A. Johns and J. Perry Langford, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for receiving stolen property in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), on the basis that a search of his home violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d). Defendant contended that the evidence against him should have been suppressed because the affidavit that supported the search warrant did not contain probable cause, and included deliberate misrepresentations and omissions, which misled the magistrate.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's home was searched pursuant to a warrant issued upon a police sergeant's affidavit, which was based on information by two identified informants. Defendant pled guilty to receiving stolen property, but preserved for appeal objections to the search of his home. The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the police sergeant believed in good faith that the search warrant was based on probable cause when it was issued. The court ruled that because Cal. Const. art. I., § 28(d) required federal rather than state constitutional law to be applied to resolve questions of admissibility of illegally seized evidence, the test whether police possessed an objectively reasonable belief in the validity of the warrant applied to defendant's case retroactively. The court found that the police sergeant had no reason to believe that his statement was false or that he acted in reckless disregard of the truth, that there was no evidence of misrepresentation or omission in his affidavit, and that there was no basis to find that the magistrate had been misled. The court ruled that suppression of the seized evidence was not warranted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the police sergeant who swore out the affidavit used to search defendant's home had a good-faith belief that the information in his affidavit was true, and the affidavit did not mislead the magistrate. The court held that the state's constitution required application of federal exclusionary law, including the good-faith exception, and there was no rational basis to suppress the evidence.

Slayton v. Pomona Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. B001806


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


161 Cal. App. 3d 538; 207 Cal. Rptr. 705; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2684



November 1, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 406061, Leon Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Petitioners shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, parents and their children, sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their request for attorney's fees under the private attorney general statute, Cal.Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. In this suit petitioners obtained a peremptory writ that prohibited respondent school district and administrators from engaging in conduct that violated petitioners' rights.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, parents and their children obtained a peremptory writ in the trial court prohibiting respondent school district and administrators from engaging in conduct which violated petitioners' rights under California statutes and the state and federal Constitutions. The trial court denied petitioners' request for attorney fees under the private attorney general statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. The court reversed the trial court's order of denial because it found that there was no reasonable basis for the denial of the motion, as the requirements of § 1021.5 had been met. The court held that petitioners were successful parties because judgment was entered in their favor on all but one issue and they obtained a peremptory writ. The court found that petitioners' action vindicated important rights affecting the public interest because it enforced the fundamental right of free expression guaranteed by the state and federal constitution. The claim was found to confer a significant benefit on the public because all students would ultimately benefit. Finally, the court found the necessity and financial burden of private enforcement made the award of attorney fees appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied petitioners', parents and their children, request for attorney's fees in their suit against respondent school district and administrators. The court found that attorney's fees were proper under the private attorney general statute because petitioners' action was successful, it vindicated an important public right, conferred a significant benefit, and private enforcement was a financial burden.

 

1082 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Weaver


No. A024649


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


161 Cal. App. 3d 119; 207 Cal. Rptr. 419; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2644



October 24, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 17, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 87609, Thomas Charles Hastings, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted and sentenced him for attempted burglary of a residence with three prior serious felony convictions.


OVERVIEW: Two witnesses observed appellant as he left the scene of an attempted residential burglary. Appellant later admitted the offense. Pursuant to a plea agreement, appellant entered a plea of guilty to attempted burglary and admitted three of the four alleged prior serious felony convictions. The trial court sentenced appellant accordingly. On appeal, the court held that appellant's prior criminal convictions could be used to enhance his sentence. The court applied Cal. Penal Code § 667(a), which permitted sentence enhancement for a person convicted of a serious felony who previously had been convicted of a serious felony. Because appellant was convicted of attempted burglary, which was a serious felony, and had been previously convicted, the court held that the sentence was not impermissibly long. The court further held that the application of Cal. Penal Code § 667 did not violate ex post facto rules. Lastly, the court held that appellant's sentence did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment because the sentence imposed included permissible enhancement for appellant's prior serious felony convictions. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that convicted and sentenced appellant for attempted burglary of a residence with three prior serious felony convictions was affirmed. The sentence was not impermissibly long, the sentence did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment, and the application of the sentence enhancement law did not violate ex post facto protections.

Mezey v. Cal.


No. A018817


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


161 Cal. App. 3d 1060; 208 Cal. Rptr. 40; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2762



October 23, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 31, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 781138, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff instructor sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed her complaint against defendants, state, state board of control, and trustees of state colleges, alleging that, following a decision finding that a loyalty oath plaintiff had refused to sign was unconstitutional, defendants wrongfully refused to reinstate her.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff instructor sought review of the lower court's dismissal of her action against defendants, state, state board of control, and trustees of state colleges, claiming that, following a decision which found a loyalty oath plaintiff had refused to sign unconstitutional, defendants wrongfully refused to reinstate her. The facts showed that plaintiff was dismissed in 1950, the decision finding the loyalty oath unconstitutional occurred in 1967, plaintiff first requested reinstatement in 1967, her last request for reinstatement was in 1976, and the claim was not filed until 1981. The court held that a cause of action for refusal to reinstate accrued on the date the request for reinstatement was denied. The court found plaintiff's cause of action was not ongoing because the concept of continuing breach was not applicable, and even if the last date for accrual was in 1976, the last day for filing an action on a breach of contract claim would have been in 1980. The court found there was no reason for the lengthy delay in bringing a cause of action, and affirmed the judgment of dismissal.


OUTCOME: The lower court's dismissal of plaintiff instructor's claim for wrongful refusal to reinstate by defendants, state, state board of control, and trustees of state colleges, was affirmed. Plaintiff's cause of action for refusal to reinstate accrued on the date the request for reinstatement was denied, and there was no reason for the lengthy delay between plaintiff's first request for reinstatement in 1967 and filing of the action in 1981.

 

1084 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Daan


Crim. No. 15868


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


161 Cal. App. 3d 22; 207 Cal. Rptr. 228; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2634



October 22, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 20, 1984. Mosk, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 65652, James A. Malkus and Kenneth A. Johns, Judges.



DISPOSITION: We hold section 28(d) abrogates California's vicarious exclusionary rule, the court correctly denied the motion to suppress the evidence seized from Bryan and affirm Daan's conviction and sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied defendant's motion under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 to suppress the use of evidence of marijuana cigarettes, before defendant pleaded nolo contendere to a misdemeanor violation of sale of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with sale of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a). Defendant filed a motion to suppress marijuana cigarettes which a police officer seized from a third party after the officer witnessed defendant and the third party exchange money for the cigarettes. The trial court denied defendant's motion. Defendant then pleaded nolo contendere to a misdemeanor violation of sale of a controlled substance, and was sentenced to probation. On appeal, the court examined whether defendant had the vicarious right to assert the illegality of the third person's detention and the seizure of the marijuana. The prosecution contended that, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. I, § 28, all relevant evidence was admissible in defendant's criminal proceeding that was not violative of federal constitutional directions. The court stated that defendant could only challenge a violation of his own reasonable expectation of privacy. Therefore, the court held that defendant could not assert the illegality of the third party's detention and seizure. The court further held that the trial court correctly denied defendant's motion to suppress. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment convicting and sentencing appellant was affirmed. The denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence of marijuana cigarettes was proper because defendant could not vicariously assert the illegality of a third party's detention and seizure.

People v. Talamantez


Crim. No. 15331.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced him to life in prison without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder and three special circumstances. It was also charged that he had three prior felony convictions. After a denial of a series of pretrial motions, defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. The special circumstances of murder by torture were found to be true. Defendant was sentenced to life in prison without the possibility of parole plus two years for prior convictions. On appeal, defendant contended that the jury instructions were erroneous, that the crime of murder by torture as defined by the trial court was unconstitutionally vague, that statements made to an inmate violated his rights pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. In re Arias


Crim. No. 13498.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


160 Cal. App. 3d 804; 206 Cal. Rptr. 806; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2586



October 4, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 22, 1985 (See 42 Cal. 3d 677)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought a writ of habeas corpus from the Court of Appeal of California to prevent the use of an electronic sound monitoring security system in protestant chapel in a youth correctional facility.


OVERVIEW: Respondent youth correctional facility's new security system was to include electronic sound monitoring in the protestant chapel. Appellants contended that such a system would have a chilling effect on their religious practices and worship. The court determined that most of the activities conducted in the chapel were of a public nature, and that the system struck a proper balance between the security needs of the institution and appellants' right to religious freedom and privacy. The court found that respondent had satisfied its burden when it showed that the current security system did not provide the protections for the staff that would have been afforded by the new system, and that measures had been taken to protect the sanctity of private worship. The court found appellants did not present an equally effective alternative. The court denied appellant's petition.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of habeous corpus because the court determined that electronic monitoring of traditionally open and public religious activities did not violate petitioners' constitutional right of privacy.

 

1087 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Los Angeles v. Superior Court


Civ. No. B005694.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


160 Cal. App. 3d 489; 206 Cal. Rptr. 674; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2558



September 28, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 21, 1984 (See 176 Cal. App. 856)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought a writ of mandate or, in the alternative, a writ of prohibition to set aside respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County's order which found a sliding scale settlement agreement negotiated between real parties in interest and all defendants except petitioner, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.5, 877.6, was made in good faith. Petitioner argued § 877.5 was unconstitutional and the agreement was not in good faith.


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest were involved in a car accident and he alleged medical negligence against defendants, hospital and paramedics, and also alleged negligence against petitioner city in its detention, arrest, and custody of him. A sliding scale agreement was worked out, settling defendants moved for a hearing on good faith of the agreement, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6. Respondent trial court, after a hearing, determined that the settlement was in good faith. Petitioner appealed, seeking a writ of mandate or a writ of prohibition to set aside the agreement on the ground that § 877.5 was unconstitutional in that it denied nonsettling parties equal protection of the law and a right to a fair trial. Petitioner also argued that the agreement was not in good faith. The court stated that as long as parties did not design their sliding scale settlement agreements to tortiously injure the nonsettlors, but merely ignore them, nothing violative of the legislatively set public policy resulted and denied the petition for a peremptory writ. The court held that § 877.5 was constitutional and the sliding scale agreement was a good faith settlement.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a peremptory writ, finding that the statute was constitutional and the sliding scale agreement was a good faith settlement. The court stated that as long as parties did not design their sliding scale recovery agreements or settlement agreements to tortiously injure the nonsettlors, but merely ignore them, nothing violative of the legislatively set public policy resulted.

 

1088 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bradford


Crim. No. 12788


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


160 Cal. App. 3d 532; 206 Cal. Rptr. 899; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2562



September 28, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - See footnote 2, post, page 534.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of both parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied January 17, 1985. Lucas, J., was of the opinion that the hearing should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yuba County, No. 4490, Charles C. Dawson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The jury's finding the defendant suffered a prior conviction as alleged is vacated and the five-year term imposed for the enhancement is stricken. In all other respects, the judgment of conviction is affirmed. The trial court is directed to prepare a new abstract of judgment in conformity with this opinion and to furnish a certified copy thereof to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), entered upon a jury verdict, convicting him of first-degree murder, with a special finding that he was previously convicted of a "serious felony," Cal. Penal Code §§ 667 and 1192.7(c)(23), where defendant was convicted in 1976 of assault with a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), while a firearm-use enhancement for that offense was dismissed.


OVERVIEW: In 1976, pursuant to a plea agreement, defendant pled guilty to assault with a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), while an allegation of a firearm-use enhancement for that offense was dismissed. Several years later, defendant was charged with first-degree murder, with special enhancement allegations that included a charge that he had previously been convicted in 1976 of a "serious felony," Cal. Penal Code §§ 667 and 1192.7(c)(23). Defendant was convicted and he appealed. The murder conviction was affirmed in an unpublished opinion. In reversing the prior "serious felony" finding, the court interpreted § 1192.7(c)(23), which provided a list of serious felonies, to mean a prior felony in which an enhancement for use of a deadly or dangerous weapon was found to be true. The court then ruled that, because § 667 was first enacted in 1977 and defendant's prior conviction in 1976 was not accompanied by enhancement, that conviction was not a "serious felony."


OUTCOME: The murder conviction was affirmed in an unpublished opinion. The prior "serious felony" enhancement was reversed because the prior conviction for assault with deadly weapon was not accompanied by a true finding on the firearm-use enhancement, as required for that conviction to be deemed a "serious felony."

County of Sacramento v. Loeb


Civ. No. 22891


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


160 Cal. App. 3d 446; 206 Cal. Rptr. 626; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2553



September 27, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 300522, Michael J. Virga, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to include within the phrase "any current appropriations" the Short-Doyle appropriations as contained within the 1982, 1983 and 1984 Budget Acts. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the order of the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California), which granted to plaintiffs, counties, a peremptory writ of mandate that compelled the state to submit to the state board of control, with a recommendation to pay, plaintiffs' claim for amounts underpaid during the previous fiscal year for the state's share of expenditures under the Short-Doyle Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5600 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, counties, were granted a peremptory writ of mandate against the state that compelled the state to submit to the state board of control, with a recommendation to pay, plaintiffs' claim for amounts underpaid during the previous fiscal year for the state's share of expenditures under the Short-Doyle Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5600 et seq. The court affirmed the writ as modified and held that it was properly issued because it was an abuse of discretion for the state board of control to partially disapprove plaintiffs' concededly valid claims under Cal. Gov't Code § 16304.1. The court held that mandate was the appropriate remedy. The court held that if any part of the Short-Doyle appropriation remained unexhausted at the time the trial court entered its order, it was "available" for satisfaction of a prior Short-Doyle encumbrance within the meaning of § 16304.1. The court modified the judgment so that recourse could have been had to any unexpended, unencumbered funds that remained in subsequent appropriations. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 13332.15 was not applied retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the modified judgment that granted plaintiffs, counties, a peremptory writ of mandate against the state that compelled it to submit to the state board of control, with a recommendation to pay, plaintiffs' claim for amounts underpaid for the state's share of expenditures under the Short-Doyle Act. The writ was properly entered because it was an abuse of discretion to disapprove plaintiffs' valid claims.

 

1090 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Pines v. Tomson


Civ. No. 68622


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


160 Cal. App. 3d 370; 206 Cal. Rptr. 866; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2549; 1984-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P66,308



September 27, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On October 16, 1984, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C210776, James P. Natoli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That portion of the judgment which awards Pines and Aronek damages and paragraph 1 of the trial court's injunctive order, as modified by striking therefrom the third word of the last sentence of paragraph 1, are affirmed. Paragraphs 2 and 3 of the trial court's injunctive order are stricken and those portions of the judgment are reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court, which is directed to hear respondents' application for attorneys fees for services rendered on this appeal and to allow and fix the reasonable value of those fees. The parties are to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners and operators of a business telephone directory, challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted injunctive and monetary relief to respondent business owners in an action alleging discrimination on the basis of religion contrary to Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51, 51.5 and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 16721, 17200 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondents brought an action against appellants, who owned and operated a business telephone directory that required advertisers to affirm that they accepted Christ as their savior. Respondents who were Jewish alleged that appellants' refusal of their advertisement request in the directory constituted discrimination on the basis of religion. The trial court found in favor of respondents and awarded injunctive and monetary relief. On appeal, appellants maintained that respondents lacked standing, that a private nonprofit religious corporation was not a business establishment, and that regulation of the content of the directory violated appellants' rights to freedom of speech, religion, and association. The court held that having alleged that they have been damaged by appellants' refusal, respondents had standing. The court affirmed the lower court's order that enjoined appellants' discriminatory business practices against respondents as the order did not unconstitutionally impair protected freedoms. However, the court reversed part of the order that enjoined appellants from publishing certain editorial materials as the order violated appellants' rights to free speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order enjoining appellants' discriminatory practices because appellants' freedom of religion, association, and speech were not constitutionally impaired. The court reversed the order enjoining appellants from publishing certain editorial materials because the order violated appellants' right to free speech.

 

1091 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Benart


Civ. No. 29765


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


160 Cal. App. 3d 183; 206 Cal. Rptr. 495; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2531



September 20, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. OFL-19619, Duane M. Thomas, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The portions of the judgment appealed from are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an interlocutory judgment in dissolution proceedings from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) which awarded the family residence to respondent wife and ordered her to pay appellant an amount equal to the reduction in principal from the date of the marriage to the date of separation. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in failing to apply Cal. Civ. Code §§ 4800.1. 4800.2.


OVERVIEW: Respondent wife purchased a house before marrying appellant husband. After marriage, the house became the family residence and payments on first and second trust deed loans were made from community funds. Respondent later recorded a deed conveying the property to herself and appellant as joint tenants. Based on respondent's testimony in proceedings to dissolve the marriage, the trial court held that the presumption raised by the joint tenancy deed was overcome by evidence of an oral understanding or agreement that the property was to remain respondent's separate property. The trial court ordered respondent to pay appellant an amount equal to the reduction in principal from the date of the marriage to the date of separation. On appeal, the court agreed with appellant that the trial court erred in failing to retroactively apply Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1 and 4800.2 governing joint tenancy deeds to husband to wife during marriage. However, because there was no change in the property's fair market value between the dates of marriage and separation, the court found, the trial court reached the proper result, albeit for improper reasons. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: Finding that the trial court reached the proper disposition of the parties' property, albeit for the wrong reasons, the court affirmed the trial court's interlocutory judgment in proceedings to dissolve the parties' marriage.

 

1092 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Novar Corp. v. Bureau of Collection & Investigative Servs.


No. B002463


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


160 Cal. App. 3d 1; 206 Cal. Rptr. 287; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2514



September 13, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 449861, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state bureau filed an appeal from the decision of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant and its agents, servants, employees, and representatives from making statements that respondent corporation was an "alarm company operator" and must be licensed by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bureau filed an appeal from a preliminary injunction enjoining it and its agents, servants, employees, and representatives from making statements that respondent corporation was an "alarm company operator" and must be licensed by appellant. The court found that the grant or refusal of a preliminary injunction was within the discretion of the trial court and its order could be reversed only if abuse of discretion was shown. The trial court found that it was more probable that the respondent's deterrent system was not an alarm system within the definition of the Alarm Company Act, Bus. & Prof. Code, § 7590 et seq. The trial court determined that it was probable that respondent would ultimately prevail and issued the preliminary injunction. The declarations showed that respondent's deterrent system was based on the concept that a horn sounding and light flashing within the building would frighten an intruder away. The court could not say that the trial court abused its discretion in determining that the unit was not designed to signal the presence of a hazard requiring urgent attention and to which police were expected to respond.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which granted a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant bureau from making statements that respondent corporation was an "alarm company operator" and must be licensed by appellant because appellant failed to establish that the trial court abused its discretion in granting the injunction.

In re Marriage of Huxley


Civ. No. 65343


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


159 Cal. App. 3d 1253; 206 Cal. Rptr. 291; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2512



September 13, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 5, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 12, 1984. Bird, C. J., Mosk, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 946725, Huey P. Shepard, Frank Baffa and George R. Perkovich, Jr., Judges.



DISPOSITION: The matter is reversed as to that part of the interlocutory judgment not awarding reimbursement to Jack of separate property funds invested in the Palos Verdes condominium and the Redondo Beach apartment building, held jointly by Jack and Karin. In all other aspects, the judgment is affirmed. The order awarding attorney's fees and costs on appeal is affirmed. The parties to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband sought review of the judgment of marital dissolution from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: Appellant former husband contended that the trial court erred when it did not reimburse him for his contributions, from separate property, for the purchase of a condominium and an apartment building. The court held under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2, appellant was entitled to reimbursement for his separate property contributions to the properties that he and appellee former wife acquired as joint tenants during their marriage. The court noted that any reimbursement was limited to the separate property equity in the properties at the time of acquisition. Appellant also contended that the trial court abused its discretion when it ordered him to $ 12,500 towards appellee's attorney fees for the 15-day trial and $ 7,500 towards appellee's attorney fees on appeal. The court held that there was no abuse of discretion in the trial court's award of attorney fees to appellee. Judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part.


OUTCOME: Trial court's judgment pertaining to the award of attorney fees to appellee former wife was affirmed. However, trial court's judgment that denied appellant former husband reimbursement for the contributions he made from separate property for the purchase of two parcels of real property was reversed because appellant never waived his right to reimbursement.

People v. Barr


Crim. No. 15434.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


159 Cal. App. 3d 1129; 206 Cal. Rptr. 331; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2501



September 11, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Sept. 27 and Oct. 2, 1984; Hg. granted Dec. 13, 1984 (Crim. 24238)
Aug. 15, 1985, cause retrans to Ct. App., 4th Dist. Div. 1, with directions to refile its opn. and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish that opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Rules of Ct., rule 976 (c)(2)).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 56138, William T. Low and J. Perry Langford, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found defendant guilty of 14 counts of robbery, 1 count of attempted robbery, and 3 counts of felony false imprisonment, and sentenced defendant to prison for 31 years and 8 months.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty by a jury of all crimes charged in an 18-count information: 14 counts of robbery, 1 count of attempted robbery, and 3 counts of felony false imprisonment. Defendant was sentenced to prison for 31 years and 8 months. On appeal, the court held that the failure to appoint a class V attorney to defendant, who was charged with class V crimes, did not result in Pope error. Defendant was not entitled to any specific appointed attorney. Defendant's Hitch motion was properly denied because the physical evidence destroyed was not material to defendant's defense. The evidence contained no fingerprints or other identifying marks implicating or exculpating defendant. Despite the fact that Cal. Penal Code § 1102.5 was an unconstitutional attempt to undermine defendant's right to effective assistance of counsel, the court held that the charges against defendant were proved by overwhelming evidence. Unchallenged facts, multiple percipient witnesses, and unmovable hard nontestimonial evidence invited the jury to convict defendant. The court held that the errors did not compel reversal and affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found defendant guilty of 14 counts of robbery, 1 count of attempted robbery, and 3 counts of felony false imprisonment, and sentenced defendant to prison for 31 years and 8 months. Any errors made by the trial court did not compel reversal because the evidence of defendant's guilt was overwhelming.

People v. Anderson


No. AO17390.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


159 Cal. App. 3d 1034; 206 Cal. Rptr. 74; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2489



September 7, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. on den. rehg. Oct. 5, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 78136, Roland K. Hall, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which entered judgment on negotiated pleas of guilty to two counts of robbery by use of a firearm. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 motion to suppress a photograph taken during the execution of a judicially established invalid search warrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to two counts of robbery by use of a firearm. On appeal, defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 motion to suppress a photograph seized from his house four years earlier, pursuant to a search warrant that was judicially invalidated. The photograph was used as evidence against defendant in the trial court action. Defendant argued that because the search warrant was invalidated, the photograph and other fruits that led to his conviction should have been suppressed and that the absence of such evidence required dismissal of the charges against him. The state argued that defendant's subsequent crimes had removed the taint from the evidence obtained as a result of unlawful police conduct. The reviewing court agreed, holding that the passage of time and defendant's subsequent crimes attenuated the unlawfulness of the police search. The court affirmed the trial court's decision denying the motion to suppress and entering judgment on defendant's guilty pleas.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment entered on negotiated guilty pleas to two counts of robbery by use of a firearm because the unlawfulness of the prior search warrant that led to discovery of a photograph that implicated defendant was attenuated by defendant's subsequent crimes.

 

1096 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Javier A.


Crim. No. 43708


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


159 Cal. App. 3d 913; 206 Cal. Rptr. 386; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2484



August 31, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 28, 1984. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 21, 1984. Bird, C. J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J 556674, James P. Natoli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In compliance with principles of stare decisis enunciated in Auto Equity, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied his motion to suppress and his request for a jury trial in juvenile delinquency proceedings on charges of attempted murder and declared him a ward of the court to be committed to a youth facility.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was adjudicated a ward of the court and committed to a youth facility following juvenile delinquency proceedings. Before proceedings began, defendant moved to suppress a photograph of him and his girlfriend that was obtained during a search of her apartment. Another resident of the apartment had consented to the search. The trial court denied the motion to suppress and also denied defendant's motion for a jury trial. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed. The court held that the motion to suppress was properly denied because the state showed that permission to search was obtained from a third party who possessed common authority over or other sufficient relationship to the premises or effects sought to be inspected. The court next addressed the denial of defendant's request for a jury trial. The court acknowledged that under the In re Daedler case there was no constitutional right to a jury trial in wardship type proceedings such as this and that stare decisis bound it to uphold this decision. However, the court undertook an extensive and thorough historical review of the juvenile justice system in an effort to convince the higher court to revisit the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment declaring defendant juvenile a ward of the court and committing him to a youth facility on charges of attempted murder. The trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress a photo obtained during a search of his girlfriend's apartment because police obtained the consent of another resident of the apartment to conduct the search. Stare decisis bound the court to affirm the denial of jury trial request.
