Wilks v. Mouton


No. AO24878.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


159 Cal. App. 3d 792; 205 Cal. Rptr. 735; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2470



August 29, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. on den. rehg. Sept. 28, 1984; Hg. granted Nov. 1 1984


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C275654, John F. Cruikshank, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant elected official sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which ruled in favor of respondent city and confirmed the passage of the incorporation of city, as well as election of respondent city officials, in appellant's action alleging election misconduct and illegal voting.


OVERVIEW: Appellant elected official filed statements of contest challenging election results on grounds of election officials' misconduct and illegal voting. The trial court confirmed passage of the incorporation measure, and the election of challenged respondent city officials. Appellant argued that absentee ballots were illegal and void due to voter fraud and misconduct of election officials. Appellant argued that loss of secrecy resulted from improper procedures used in obtaining and returning ballots. The court reversed confirmation of election results and held that Cal. Elec. Code § 1013 prohibited a third party's manual delivery of absentee ballots to the county clerk. The court held that § 1013's requirement of personal delivery preserved secrecy, uniformity, and integrity of the voting process. The court held that presence of campaign workers, one of whom was a candidate on the ballot, constituted an unlawful intrusion upon the secret ballot guarantee. The court held remanded the case with directions to the trial court to identify and determine which ballots should be voided, followed by a declaration of the results of the elections discounting the voided ballots.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's order confirming election results because delivery of absentee ballots by a third person to the city clerk was a clear violation of statutory elections law. The court held that presence of campaign workers and votes which were cast with their assistance compromised the secrecy of some ballots.

 

1098 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Taylor


Civ. No. 22508


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


160 Cal. App. 3d 471; 206 Cal. Rptr. 557; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2555



August 29, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Butte County, No. 76944, Reginald M. Watt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded for retrial in order to determine the parties' respective interests pursuant to sections 4800.1 and 4800.2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), holding that the family house was respondent husband's separate property in a dissolution action. Appellant alleged that the characterization was unsupported by the evidence presented.


OVERVIEW: The parties, after they were married, purchased a lot and built a home entirely financed by respondent husband's separate property funds. Title was taken in joint tenancy. Appellant wife petitioned for dissolution, and a trial was held solely on the issue of whether the house was community or separate property. The trial court found that appellant had an understanding that the house was respondent's separate property, and an interlocutory judgment to that effect was entered. Appellant sought review of the judgment, contending that the characterization of the property was unsupported by the evidence presented. The court held that recent legislative enactments, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 4800.1 and 4800.2, applied to the characterization of property in a dissolution action. The question presented to the court was whether retroactive application was proper. The court held that the clear legislative intent included the retroactive application of the enactments to any proceeding not final as of a certain date, such as the parties' dissolution action. The judgment was reversed and remanded for consideration of evidence on the questions of separate property contribution and reimbursement.


OUTCOME: The judgment characterizing a house as respondent husband's separate property in a dissolution action was reversed and remanded because newly enacted statutes regarding community and separate property distribution were retroactively applicable to the parties' dissolution action.

 

1099 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Lynn


Crim. No. 15346


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


159 Cal. App. 3d 715; 206 Cal. Rptr. 181; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2466



August 28, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 24, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRN 7487, Anthony C. Joseph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which, after a jury trial, convicted defendant of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant strangled the victim with his hands. In order to make sure that the victim was dead, defendant tightened a rope around victim's neck and also stomped on victim's neck. Defendant then took victim's wallet and shirt, and left victim's body in a desolate area. A jury convicted defendant of first degree murder on theories that the murder was either premeditated or was by torture. Defendant contended on appeal that the evidence of premeditation and torture was insufficient, that the trial court provided confusing and erroneous instructions to the jury, and that the trial court erred in admitting a tape recording of defendant's statements made to the police and letters sent to defendant in jail into evidence. The court rejected defendant's contentions and affirmed the conviction. The court ruled that the evidence established defendant acted with premeditation, that the manner of killing was particular and exacting, and that the evidence substantiated a finding of torture murder. The court ruled that the jury instructions were appropriate and that defendant's evidentiary claims were either waived or harmless error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of first degree murder based upon premeditation and torture. The court ruled that defendant's strangulation of the victim in three different manners over a period of time substantiated the conviction and that the trial court properly instructed the jury regarding consideration of defendant's motives.

 

1100 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Williams


No. A023454


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


159 Cal. App. 3d 600; 205 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2454



August 24, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 20, 1984. Reynoso, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate, the inmate editor of the San Quentin News, filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the regulations set forth by respondent department of corrections for the publication of inmate newspapers were constitutionally invalid on their face.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was the inmate editor of the San Quentin News. He filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus and contended that the guidelines of the Department of Corrections for the publication of inmate newspapers were constitutionally invalid on their face. The court held that respondent department of corrections could censor newspapers in order to provide for the reasonable security of the institution and the reasonable protection of the public. It could also exercise control over the content of the newspaper to serve valid penological objectives; however, censorship could not go beyond that which was necessary to achieve a legitimate penological objective. The court concluded that the regulations conformed to the standards set forth for a limited scope publication, which functions as a public forum, and that respondent could exercise control over the newspaper as set forth in the regulations on the grounds that the censorship provided for the reasonable security of the institution as well as reasonable protection of the public and served valid penological objectives.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus on the basis that the challenged regulations censored the content of the inmate newspaper only to the extent necessary to provide for the reasonable security of the institution, to provide reasonable protection of the public, and to serve valid penological objectives.

 

1101 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Milse


Civ. No. 68540.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


159 Cal. App. 3d 471; 205 Cal. Rptr. 616; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2444



August 23, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 21, 1984 (See 182 Cal. App. 3d 203)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SWD 76226, George R. Perkovich, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband sought review of the decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which divided the property in the marriage dissolution and ruled that the home of the parties, held of record in joint tenancy, was actually the separate property of the wife.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-husband and ex-wife separated and appellant contended the evidence was insufficient to justify the trial court's ruling that the home of the parties, held of record in joint tenancy, was actually the separate property of the wife. The court affirmed the trial court's conclusion that the house was the wife's separate property, subject to appellant's right to reimbursement for his separate fund contributions and his share of community fund payments on the mortgage installments. The court concluded that parties to a marriage could change the character of their marital property by expressions of mutual consent regardless of the title form shown on the deed of conveyance. The trial court was permitted to accept wife's testimony as proof of an oral understanding that property held of record in joint tenancy was actually the wife's separate property. The court concluded that the retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1, which extended the statute of frauds to spouses as to property acquired during marriage and held in joint tenancy form, would be an unconstitutional deprivation of a vested property right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's division of property in the marriage dissolution and its ruling that the home of the parties which was held of record in joint tenancy was actually the separate property of the wife. The court concluded that an oral agreement that the residence was to be the wife's separate property overcame the statutory presumption that a joint tenancy residence acquired during marriage was community property.

 

1102 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Casa Blanca Convalescent Homes


Civ. No. 26915


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


159 Cal. App. 3d 509; 206 Cal. Rptr. 164; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2448; 53 A.L.R.4th 661



August 23, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 10, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 404471, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in all respects in affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant nursing home company appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), imposing civil penalties and an injunction against defendant for unlawful and unfair business practices and multiple violations of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 et seq. and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 17 and 22, with respect to defendant's operation of its facilities.


OVERVIEW: Defendant nursing home company, which operated nine licensed nursing facilities, was charged with multiple violations of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 et seq. and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 17 and 22, including allegations of an inadequate surety bond, inadequate staffing and nursing care, failing to maintain proper patient records, and permitting unsanitary conditions. Judgment was entered against defendant for 67 violations and substantial civil penalties, and an injunction was imposed. Defendant appealed. In affirming, the court ruled, inter alia, that the trial court's statement of decision was sufficient under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 632 and specific evidentiary findings were not required. The regulations defendant was subject to were reasonable uses of police power, were not vague or indefinite, and substantial compliance was not a defense. Defendant's conduct could be both unlawful and an unfair business practice. Moreover, there was substantial evidence of multiple breaches of law and continued flagrant misconduct as charged, justifying severe sanctions against defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because, inter alia, the trial court's statement of decision was sufficient, the regulations were not vague or unreasonable and substantial evidence showed multiple violations and continued flagrant misconduct by defendant in not having an adequate surety bond for patient deposits, failing to render treatment and maintain proper health records, giving treatment without an order, and permitting unsanitary conditions.

 

1103 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Rittenband v. Cory


No. A018991


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


159 Cal. App. 3d 410; 205 Cal. Rptr. 576; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2437



August 22, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 20, 1984, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 15, 1984. Mosk, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 745105, Leland J. Lazarus, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judges challenged an order by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which upheld the constitutionality of article 3.6 of the Judges' Retirement Law, Cal. Gov't. Code §§ 75075-75079. The statute provided reduced benefits for judges who chose to retire after age 70.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff judges sought review of a decision upholding the constitutionality of article 3.6 of the Judges' Retirement Law, Cal. Gov't. Code §§ 75075-75079, which encouraged judges to retire at or before age 70 by providing reduced retirement benefits after age 70. The trial court concluded that the statute served the legitimate state purpose of maintaining the competency and work capacity of the judiciary. Plaintiffs contended that strict scrutiny of the statute was required because the disparate treatment of judges based on age affected the fundamental rights to pursue an occupation and to pursue an elective office. The court stated that the statute need only meet the rational basis test because there was no fundamental right to either continue in the judicial occupation or to run for an elective office. The court found that the correlation between increasing age and decreasing ability to competently perform work was a logical assumption and concluded that the age distinction embodied in the statute rested on a rational basis. The court ruled that the state had a legitimate interest in maintaining a vigorous and competent judiciary and affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the constitutionality of the statute reducing retirement benefits for judges who retired after age 70 was affirmed. Plaintiff judges had no fundamental right to either continue in the judicial profession or to seek and hold an elective office and the statute was rationally related to the legitimate state purpose of assuring the competency of the judiciary.

 

1104 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kelly v. City of Fresno


Civ. No. F002574.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


159 Cal. App. 3d 110; 205 Cal. Rptr. 416; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2407



August 15, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn., Hg. granted Nov. 21, 1984 (S.F. 24825) May 29, 1986 Trans. to Ct. App., 5th Dist., for reconsideration in light of Lybarger v. City of Los Angeles, 40 Cal. 3d 822.
App. dism. October 2, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 292761-5, Blaine E. Pettitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which denied petitioner reinstatement before exhaustion of administrative remedies and denied petitioner's writ of mandate, which requested civil suppression of his statement obtained in violation of Cal. Gov't. Code § 3303(g), an injunction against further departmental violation of § 3303(g), and immediate reinstatement of petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner police officer became the focus of a criminal investigation. Respondent city's internal affairs department ordered petitioner to participate in an interview and was explicitly told that he had no civil rights and that he was not going to be read or admonished of his rights. Petitioner admitted involvement in crimes and was terminated immediately after the interview. The lower court denied petitioner's writ of mandate, which requested civil suppression of the interview statement and immediate reinstatement. The court affirmed the lower court's ruling on the reinstatement issue and held that petitioner failed to exhaust his administrative remedies and that the termination appeared to have been intended and accomplished without regard to the interview. The court reversed the lower court on all other grounds and held that Cal. Gov't. Code § 3303(g) granted petitioner a right to be silent in his interview and that the proper remedy was to exclude the improperly obtained statements from administrative consideration. The court found that excluding the improperly obtained information placed respondent in no worse position than if the right to silence was elected.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order, which denied interim reinstatement to petitioner police officer, because petitioner failed to exhaust his administrative remedies. The court reversed in all other respects because petitioner was entitled to his constitutional right to remain silent in the investigation interview by respondent internal affairs department and was entitled to exclusion of all improperly obtained statements.

 

1105 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Kevin G.


Civ. No. F003181.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


159 Cal. App. 3d 125; 205 Cal. Rptr. 424; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2408



August 15, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 19, 1984 (See 40 Cal. 3d 644)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 14599, George C. Barrett, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of the order from the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which declared defendant's prior juvenile adjudication for driving under the influence of alcohol was constitutionally infirm, and asserted that defendant did not have a right to appointed counsel in his hearing before a traffic hearing officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's prior juvenile adjudication for driving under the influence of alcohol was declared to be unconstitutional by the lower court because he was denied the right to appointed counsel in his hearing before a traffic hearing officer. The people challenged the ruling and asserted that by electing to proceed before a traffic hearing officer, defendant elected to have his misdemeanor procedurally treated as an infraction, for which there is no right to court-appointed counsel under Cal. Penal Code § 19(c). On appeal the court affirmed the judgment and held that defendant was charged with a misdemeanor and that having the matter heard before a traffic hearing officer did not transform it into an infraction. The court held that misdemeanant defendants were entitled to the assistance of court-appointed counsel and that the waiver signed by defendant and one of his parents was deficient because it did not advise defendant that he had a right to counsel if his case was heard by a traffic hearing officer. The court found that defendant was not properly advised of, noror knowingly waived, his right to counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that defendant's prior juvenile adjudication for driving under the influence of alcohol was unconstitutional because he was not properly advised of his right to appointed counsel in his hearing before a traffic hearing officer, and that the waiver executed by defendant and his parent was insufficient to constitute a knowing waiver of his rights.

 

1106 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Mark K.


No. F002248


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


159 Cal. App. 3d 94; 205 Cal. Rptr. 393; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2406



August 15, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 19, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 280169-4, James L. Quaschnick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent Department Of Social Services terminating appellant's parental rights to his minor child.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father was mentally disabled and had custody of his minor child after the mother was hospitalized for psychiatric evaluation. After several visits to the home, respondent Department of Social Services sought to declare the child free from the custody of appellant. The trial court took extreme precautions to ensure that appellant had adequate representation, a personal appearance, an examination by a court appointed psychiatrist and allowed protracted delays before granting respondent's motion for summary judgment terminating appellant's parental rights. The court reversed the judgment and found that parental rights were of such constitutional importance that they should not have been flung aside on a motion for summary judgment. The court found that no matter how careful the trial court was in protecting appellant's rights, the constitutional gravity of a termination proceeding required that appellant be given a full trial on the issues.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment against appellant father in favor of respondent Department of Social Services because the summary judgment procedure was inappropriate to the statutorily required time periods which have been enacted with consideration for the needs of the parent, the child, and the public and appellant had a right to be heard at trial.

 

1107 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (beasley)


No. F003742


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


159 Cal. App. 3d 131; 205 Cal. Rptr. 413; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2409



August 15, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the Superior Court of Fresno County to vacate its sentence in action No. 301445-3, to hold a further hearing which will allow the parties to present evidence regarding jail conditions as they would relate to defendant's incarceration, and, thereafter, resentence defendant in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prosecution applied for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Fresno County (California), to vacate its order placing handicapped drunk driving defendant on probation with no jail time imposed. Defendant was a repeat drunk driver who had suffered severe permanent injury as a result of his own conduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a wheel-chair bound drunk driver who had suffered severe permanent injury as a result of his own conduct, had been convicted for the third time of violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152, driving under the influence, and received probation with no jail time after his attorney argued, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385, that defendant's condition was "punishment enough," that the court should disregard the statutory language that required a year in jail, and that jail authorities would have problems housing him. On petitioner prosecution's appeal, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent court to vacate its sentence and hear evidence regarding jail conditions as they related to defendant's incarceration and resentence him. The court found that judicial sentencing had to comply with specific legislative directions unless cruel and unusual punishment resulted and that § 1385 was not directly applicable. The court noted that there was no authority that self-inflicted injuries excused mandated incarceration, but noted that a defendant's condition could transform the confinement into cruel or unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner prosecution's request for a writ of mandate, directed respondent trial court to vacate its order granting disabled defendant's probation with no jail time, and directed that it hear evidence regarding jail conditions before resentencing defendant because respondent was required to follow the statutory one-year sentence unless it resulted in cruel and unusual punishment due to defendant's physical condition.

 

1108 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Adoption of Baby Boy D.


No. B002775


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


159 Cal. App. 3d 8; 205 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2398



August 13, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SEAD 3457, W. James Turpit, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings in which the trial court is directed to hold a full evidentiary hearing on the issue of custody and to decide the matter in accordance with the standards enunciated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural father sought review of the order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ruled in favor of appellees adoptive parents and terminated appellant's parental rights, which required only consent of the natural mother for adoption of appellant's minor child.


OVERVIEW: Appellant natural father challenged the award of custody of his minor child to appellees adoptive parents. The trial court held that termination of parental rights only required that the natural mother need consent to appellees' adoption of the minor child. Appellant asserted that under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV, his parental rights could not be terminated and custody of his child could not be awarded to appellees adoptive parents. The court reversed the order of the trial court that terminated appellant's parental rights, and the case was remanded to the trial court to determine the issue of custody. The court held that the trial court was required to determine whether the award of custody to appellant would be detrimental to the child, and if so if it was in the child's best interest to award custody to appellees. The court found that a nonpresumed father was included in the doctrine of parental preference.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order in favor of appellant natural father and remanded the case for a determination of custody of minor child to appellees adoptive parents, because as a nonpresumed father, appellant was to be included in the hearing process, as required by the doctrine of parental preference.

 

1109 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Michael R. v. Jeffrey B.


Civ. No. B002317


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


158 Cal. App. 3d 1059; 205 Cal. Rptr. 312; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2382



August 2, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 71605, William L. Peck, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of respondents is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured minor sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondents assaulting minors that dismissed appellant's complaint for damages in a negligence action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured minor's amended complaint alleged respondents assaulting minors was involved in joint activity in aiding and abetting the others in the perpetration of shooting marbles in a manner that exposed appellant to serious injury. The trial court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment. The court reversed the summary judgment in favor of respondents because verbal encouragement to commit assault with a deadly weapon was affirmative conduct sufficient, as a matter of law, to impose civil liability for damages ensuing from that assault. The court held that the harm in this case was clearly foreseeable. Further, there was a proximate connection between the verbal encouragement and the shooting. The court found that appellant's opposition to respondents' motion for summary judgment raised a triable issue of fact whether respondents actively encouraged, solicited or conspired to injure appellant, and that verbal encouragement was included within the parameters of Cal. Penal Code § 653(f) to constitute negligence per se.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment in favor of respondents assaulting minors because the verbal encouragement to commit assault with a deadly weapon was affirmative conduct sufficient, as a matter of law, to impose civil liability for damages ensuing from that assault; thus, a triable issue of fact existed.

 

1110 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Horn


Crim. No. 12926


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


158 Cal. App. 3d 1014; 205 Cal. Rptr. 119; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2380



August 1, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 27, 1984, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 4, 1984. Lucas, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 64497, Ronald W. Tochterman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter a judgment of not guilty by reason of insanity and to take such further proceedings as are required by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted her of vehicular manslaughter, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 192(3)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of vehicular manslaughter. The court reversed the conviction because the trial court's express findings established she was legally insane under the M'Naghten test at the time of the incident. The court reviewed the history of the insanity defense and the events surrounding passage of Cal. Penal Code § 25(b), which statutorily defined the insanity defense. It determined that although the statute used the conjunctive "and," the test of insanity necessarily must use the disjunctive "or" form. It then determined that § 25(b) reinstated the California M'Naghten right and wrong test as the standard for the insanity defense. When it applied this test, the court determined that since the trial court held that appellant was incapable of distinguishing between right and wrong at the time of the incident, it expressly found the appellant met the second prong of the M'Naghten test and, therefore, appellant was entitled to a judgment of not guilty by reason of insanity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the matter with directions to enter a judgment of not guilty by reason of insanity because the trial court's express findings established that appellant was legally insane under the M'Naghten test by finding she was incapable of distinguishing between right and wrong at the time of the incident.

 

1111 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Traina


Crim. No. 13149.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


158 Cal. App. 3d 872; 205 Cal. Rptr. 85; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2368



July 30, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, the Reporter of Decisions is directed to publish the introduction and parts III and VI of this opinion and not publish parts I, II, IV, V and VII.


Hg. granted Oct. 4, 1984. (See 168 Cal. App. 3d 305)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 66255, Ronald Tochterman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant criminal appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sentenced him to robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 213, with an enhancement of five years for a prior robbery conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 667. Defendant appealed that he was entitled to a more favorable provision in Cal. Penal Code § 667.5 and that his sentence exceeded twice the base term in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1170.1(g).


OVERVIEW: Defendant criminal was sentenced for a robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 213 and an enhancement of five years for a prior robbery conviction, under Cal. Penal Code § 667. The court affirmed the convictions and found that the rule of statutory construction by which a single penal statute was susceptible of two interpretations, the one more favorable to defendant should have been applied, was not applicable because defendant's offense was punishable by two separate statutes and, under Cal. Penal Code § 654, multiple convictions were not prohibited, only multiple punishments. The court found that the law did not require that defendant receive the benefit of the more favorable of two applicable statutes. The court found that defendant could not have been sentenced under Cal. Penal Code § 667.5 because it only covered enhancements because of prior prison terms, not prior convictions, and Cal. Penal Code § 667 was the applicable statute. The court also found that under Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(f), defendant's argument that his term exceeded twice his base term in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1170.1(g) was meritless, because the article superseded the rule as it applied to enhancements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence imposed by the trial court, and found that defendant criminal was not entitled to the California Penal Code section he had cited because it only covered enhancements related to prior prison terms, not prior convictions only, as in defendant's case. The court also found that although his sentence exceeded twice the base term, it was allowable for enhancements under the California State Constitution.

Cal. ex rel. Dep't of Transp. v. Superior Court


No. A024759


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


159 Cal. App. 3d 331; 205 Cal. Rptr. 518; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2428



July 26, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to sustain the State's demurrer without leave to amend and to grant the State's motion for partial summary judgment and motion to strike with respect to real parties' claims for personal injury damages. Respondent court is further ordered to vacate and set aside its order imposing sanctions against the State.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed a petition for a writ of mandate and prohibition seeking a reexamination of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which denied the state's demurrer, motion for partial summary judgment, and motion to strike, and granted sanctions against the state in a personal injury action by plaintiff property owners, here, real parties in interest.


OVERVIEW: After a mudslide, plaintiff property owners alleged damages for property injuries under the California Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 900 et seq. Plaintiffs, here, real parties in interest, filed an amended complaint in respondent superior court alleging personal injuries, among other things. Respondent trial court denied petitioner's demurrer, motion for partial summary judgment, and motion to strike, and ordered sanctions against the state. The state sought a writ of mandate, which was granted by the court and directed respondent to sustain the state's demurrer and previous motions. The Tort Claims Act barred the action because the amended complaint alleged a factual basis for recovery that was not fairly reflected in the claim filed with the state. The claim did not give any indication that the mudslide caused personal or mental injuries or resulted in damages for such injuries. The order of sanctions was vacated because the state's motion for partial summary judgment was not a motion for reconsideration of its demurrer and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1008 sanctions were inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate that respondent superior court sustain the state's demurrer and grant the state's motion for partial summary judgment and motion to strike. The California Tort Claims Act barred real parties property owners' personal injury action because their property injury claim did not indicate personal injuries. The sanctions order was vacated because they were granted under an inapplicable statute.

In re Jamil H.


No. A019549


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


158 Cal. App. 3d 556; 204 Cal. Rptr. 816; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2338

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 89488, Wilmont Sweeney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment or order is reversed. The cause is remanded and the trial court directed to sustain the demurrer with leave to amend to cure the defect discussed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Alameda County (California), which sustained the prosecution's petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 that appellant violated § 626.8, a misdemeanor, by entering a school ground for an unlawful purpose.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution filed a petition under Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, alleging that appellant minor had violated § 626.8 by entering school grounds for an unlawful purpose. The trial court sustained the petition. Appellant contended the petition was vague and did not give him proper notice of the charge against him. The court reversed. The court found that the argument at the hearing on appellant's demurrer did focus generally on the issue at hand, and thus appellant did not waive it. The court held that the pleading failed to state which law or regulation the prosecution intended to rely upon to show appellant entered the school grounds without lawful business.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court sustaining the prosecution's petition because the petition did not inform appellant minor of which law or regulation the prosecution intended to use to prove that he entered the school grounds without lawful business. The cause was remanded so that the trial court could sustain appellant's demurrer with leave to amend to cure the defect.

 

1114 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Capitol Records v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 21260


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


158 Cal. App. 3d 582; 204 Cal. Rptr. 802; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2341



July 24, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 22, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 26, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 255286, William H. Lally, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff record company sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California), which determined that defendant board properly imposed use tax on plaintiff's acquisition of certain master sound tapes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant board imposed a use tax on plaintiff record company's acquisition of certain master sound tapes, made by independent producers, and used in the production of phonograph records. The trial court upheld defendant's determination. Plaintiff appealed, arguing that it was entitled to take advantage of a tax exemption given to movie studios relating to the acquisition by major movie studios of movies made by independent film producers and also useful in the production of phonograph records. The appellate court rejected plaintiff's argument, holding that plaintiff failed to meet its burden of proving that its acquisitions of master tapes were within the same class as the acquisition of movies by movie studios. The court noted that plaintiff acquired original master tapes from independent producers by purchase, whereas the exemption for movies was based on lease transactions. While the court found superficial similarities between master sound tapes and movies, it held that a sound tape was not a movie for the purposes of the sales or use tax.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed an order that upheld defendant board's imposition of use tax on plaintiff record company's acquisition of certain master sound tapes. The court held that plaintiff was not entitled to take advantage of a tax exemption given to movie studios because plaintiff acquired the original master tapes from independent producers by purchase, whereas the exemption for movies was based on lease transactions.

People v. Sims


Crim. No. 6678.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


158 Cal. App. 3d 469; 205 Cal. Rptr. 31; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2329



July 20, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Part II (only) is not published, as it does not meet the standards for publication contained in rule 976 (b), California Rules of Court.


Hg. den. Oct. 25, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish thisopn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 283216-0, Blaine Pettitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), convicting defendant of grand theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1). The jury also found that defendant had suffered the two prior convictions pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667.5(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with grand theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1). There was substantial evidence that he stole a television from his sister. The information further alleged that he had suffered two prior felony convictions. During trial, defense counsel stated that defendant wanted to admit the priors. After a full advisement, defendant announced that he did not wish to admit the priors and his attorney requested the court try the matter after the jury had returned its verdict on the underlying charge. Defendant did not personally waive his right to trial by jury. After the verdict was returned and the jury excused, defendant was again advised of the requirement regarding his prior convictions and he stated that he wanted the issue tried by the same jury that tried the underlying offense. The trial court impaneled another jury that found defendant had suffered the two prior convictions. Defendant argued the procedure regarding the prior convictions was improper and double jeopardy invalidated the verdict. The appellate court affirmed the order because the procedure regarding the prior convictions did not unconstitutionally burden defendant's right to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of grand theft and finding defendant had suffered the two prior convictions because there was no evidence of the kind of governmental oppression that might compel invocation of the double jeopardy clause and the defense counsel properly consented to having a different jury try the issue of defendant's prior convictions then the substantive offense jury.

County of Sacramento v. Loeb


Civ. No. 22891.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


158 Cal. App. 3d 423; 204 Cal. Rptr. 756; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2325



July 19, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Aug. 13, 1984 (See 160 Cal. App. 3d 446)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 300522, Michael J. Virga, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state officers and agencies, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted a writ of mandate directing appellants to submit respondent counties' claims for the state's share of expenditures under the Short-Doyle Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5600 et seq., to the State Board of Control, to determine whether any current appropriation was available, and to pay the claims.


OVERVIEW: During an audit, it was discovered that respondent counties had been underpaid for the state's share of expenditures under the Short-Doyle Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5600 et seq., which required the state to repay 90 percent of expenditures made in connection with locally administered and locally controlled community mental health programs. The State Board of Control partially disapproved the claims, and the legislature deleted the claims from the special appropriations bills submitted to it. Respondents then sought a writ of mandate from the trial court to direct that the claims be paid. The trial court granted the petition. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment, holding that the Board had no discretion to partially disallow the claim, and therefore the trial court properly granted the mandamus petition. The court further held that the writ properly ordered appellants, state officers and agencies, to determine whether any appropriations were available before being ordered to pay respondents' claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting respondent counties' petition for a writ of mandate, holding that the State Board of Control abused its discretion by partially disapproving respondents' valid claims and, therefore, a writ of mandate was an appropriate remedy. The trial court properly ordered the Board to determine whether any current appropriation was available before being ordered to make payment.

Ahwahnee Hills School v. County of Madera


Civ. No. 7516.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT,


158 Cal. App. 3d 385; 204 Cal. Rptr. 628; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2321



July 17, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Aug. 3, 1984; Hg. den. Sept. 27, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 27998, Donald R. Fretz, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, operator of group homes and associated individuals and entities, sought review of decisions of the Superior Court of Madera County (California), which denied appellants' application for a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus against respondents, Madera County, Madera County Planning Commission, and Madera County Board of Supervisors, in one case; and which sustained respondents' demurrer without leave to amend in second action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant group home and associated individuals applied to respondent Madera County Planning Commission for conditional use permits to enable them to increase occupancy of their homes for children. After the permits were denied after an appeal to respondent Madera County Board of Supervisors, appellants submitted a claim for damages on behalf of two minors for violation of the right to privacy under Cal. Const.; respondent board of supervisors denied the claim. Appellants next filed a petition for administrative mandamus seeking review of the decision of the board of supervisors on behalf of two children on grounds of denial of a fair hearing and abuse of discretion. A second action sought damages and other relief, and the trial court sustained a demurrer based on immunity. On appeal, the court held that the trial court erred in applying the substantial-evidence test in reviewing the evidence, because the proper test was the independent-judgment test given that a fundamental vested right was involved. The court thus reversed and remanded that cause. The court also ruled that Cal. Gov't. Code § 818.4 immunized respondents in the action for damages, and thus affirmed the demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the superior court as to application of the substantial-evidence test in the mandamus action, ordering that, upon remand, the trial court apply the independent-judgment test in light of the fundamental vested right involved. The court affirmed the trial court's sustaining the demurrer in the second action, because respondent county and county officials were statutorily immunized as to the claims advanced.

 

1118 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Arco Distribs. v. Atl. Richfield Co.


Civ. No. 66562


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


158 Cal. App. 3d 349; 204 Cal. Rptr. 743; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2318



July 17, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 3, 1984, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 14, 1984. Broussard, J., Reynoso, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. B 002911 and C 423917, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In 2d Civil No. 66562, the preliminary injunction issued on October 1, 1982, is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court to allow respondents California ARCO Distributors, Inc. and Little Oil Company, Inc. to file an amended complaint and an amended application for a temporary restraining order and order to show cause re preliminary injunction alleging such violations of the Petroleum Marketing Practices Act as they are able to demonstrate.

In 2d Civil No. B002911, the appeal taken from the April 19, 1983, order denying Atlantic Richfield Company's motion to dissolve the preliminary injunction issued on October 1, 1982, is dismissed as moot.

Each party is to bear its own costs on both appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant gas station franchisor appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which preliminarily enjoined defendant under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 20999.1 from changing terms of financing of product deliveries to plaintiff franchisees. Defendant argued that § 20999.1 was preempted by the Petroleum Marketing Practices Act, 15 U. S. C. S. § 2801 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Following the trial court's award of preliminary injunctive relief to plaintiff franchisees enjoining defendant gas station franchisor from changing the terms for payment of product deliveries, defendant appealed, arguing that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 20999.1, under which the trial court granted relief, was preempted by the Petroleum Marketing Practices Act (PMPA), 15 U.S.C.S. § 2801 et seq. The court reversed and remanded. The court ruled that preemption was compelled whether explicitly stated in the federal statute's language or implicitly contained in its structure and purpose. The court found no explicit intent in PMPA to preempt the field but held that state law was nullified to the extent that it actually conflicted with federal law. The court found that § 20999.1 and PMPA were both enacted to protect franchisees, but that PMPA was intended to protect the interests of franchisers also. Because § 20999.1(d) restricted franchisor changes differently, it was not the same as PMPA, and the court ruled that, to that extent, it was an obstacle to congressional purposes and objectives and was preempted by PMPA. Therefore, the trial court lacked jurisdiction to grant the relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of relief to plaintiff franchisees enjoining defendant gas station franchisor from changing the terms of payment for product deliveries. The court ruled that the state statute which the trial court enforced was preempted by federal legislation on the same subject and the court, therefore, was without jurisdiction to award the relief. The case was remanded.

 

1119 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bale v. San Jose Police Dep't


No. A017927


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


158 Cal. App. 3d 168; 204 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2298



July 10, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 82612, John Schatz, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Due to our disposition of this issue, we do not address appellant's remaining arguments. The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant slot machine owner challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), that ordered the destruction of slot machines seized by respondent police department after denying appellant's motion to have the machines declared antiques and therefore legal under Cal. Penal Code § 330.7.


OVERVIEW: Respondent police department seized slot machines belonging to appellant slot machine owner after an expert determined that the machines were not antique. The trial court denied appellant's motion to have the machines declared antique and therefore legal under Cal. Penal Code § 330.7 and ordered the machines destroyed under Cal. Penal Code §335a. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that prohibitions against slot machines were inapplicable if the machines, by any reasonable definition, were antique. The court found that Cal. Penal Code § 330.7 was unconstitutionally vague, but chose not to void the defense. Instead, the court found that the statute, by providing a defense to prohibitions against slot machines for those that were antique with no guidelines as to what constituted an antique slot machine, created an ambiguous exception to Penal Code prohibitions which had to be liberally construed in favor of persons seeking its protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment ordering the destruction of slot machines seized by respondent police department after denying appellant slot machine owner's motion to have the machines declared antique and therefore legal because prohibitions against slot machines were inapplicable if the machines, by any reasonable definition, were antique. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

1120 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Guzman v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B002042


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


158 Cal. App. 3d 190; 204 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2300; 49 Cal. Comp. Cases 544



July 10, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of respondent Union Oil Company of California for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 3, 1984.



DISPOSITION: The September 21, 1983, order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board denying Jose Guzman's petition for reconsideration, and its June 1, 1983, finding and other rescinding the April 12, 1983, findings and award of the workers' compensation judge are annulled; and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner worker sought review of the order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), that denied reconsideration of its order rescinding an award and the findings of the workers' compensation judge that petitioner was an employee of respondent company under Cal. Lab. Code Labor § 3360.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner worker and his cousin had regular employment as gardeners, and on the weekends, the cousin would contract for extra piecework unrelated to either one's regular employment. The cousin won a bid on a pruning job with respondent company and the cousin asked petitioner to do the job for a 50/50 split of the bid amount. While pruning trees at a station owned by respondent company, appellant slipped and injured his foot. A worker's compensation judge found petitioner to be an employee of respondent company under Cal. Lab. Code § 3360 and entitled to an award. Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (WCAB) rescinded the findings upon reconsideration and denied petitioner's subsequent motion for reconsideration. Petitioner sought review. The court annulled the WCAB's order rescinding the determination that petitioner was an employee. The court held that § 3360 was clear, broad, and unambiguous in its declaration that it created an employment relationship between partnerships and the person having the work executed. The court determined that § 3360 applied to petitioner even if the partnership was found to be of an independent contractor type.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (WCAB) and held that the WCAB erred when it determined that respondent company was not an employer of petitioner worker at the time that petitioner sustained an injury to his foot. The court explained that an employment relationship was created under the applicable statute and entitled petitioner to an award.

Edwards v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 30639.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


158 Cal. App. 3d 159; 204 Cal. Rptr. 509; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2297



July 9, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 30, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, convicted of murder and attempted murder, sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Orange County (California) to vacate its order which denied petitioner's motion to enter a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole, and its order that permitted amendment of the prosecution's notice of aggravating evidence to be introduced at the penalty phase of his trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of murder and attempted murder, and the jury found the special circumstance of intentional killing while lying in wait. The jury was unable to determine whether petitioner's penalty should be death or life imprisonment without parole. Respondent trial court dismissed the jury in accordance with Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(b). Petitioner asked the court to compel respondent to vacate its order that denied his motion to enter a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole, and its order that permitted amendment of the prosecution's notice of aggravating evidence to be introduced at the penalty phase. The court directed respondent, on remand, to exercise its discretion as to whether a basis existed to bar imposition of the death penalty. The court held that the prosecution's amendment to a notice of aggravating evidence could be allowed after the guilt phase, provided that good cause existed for the amendment and no prejudice to petitioner resulted. Issuing a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate its order allowing the amendment, the court found that the amendment would have been prejudicial to petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the cause to respondent trial court for the exercise of its discretion to determine whether a basis existed for sentencing petitioner to life imprisonment without possibility of parole. The court also issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the lower court to vacate its order allowing amendment of the prosecution's notice of aggravating evidence.

 

1122 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Collins v. Department of Motor Vehicles


No. AO19497.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


158 Cal. App. 3d 74; 204 Cal. Rptr. 373; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2289



July 3, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 20, 1984 (S.F. 24795)
App. dism. Sept. 19, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 265208, V. Gene McDonald, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant department of motor vehicles sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), that granted a peremptory writ of mandate and directed the cancellation of an order suspending respondent's driver's license for a six months pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Respondent was convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of former Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a). He was fined and not placed on probation, and the trial court recommended that his license not be suspended. Nevertheless, appellant department of motor vehicles (DMV) suspended respondent's driving privileges pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(a)(1). At the time of his offense, § 23102 prohibited driving under the influence, and specified the punishment for a first conviction of the offense, fine or imprisonment in county jail or both. The version of § 13352 then in effect provided that DMV was required to suspend a first offender's license only if the court so ordered, whereas at the time of his conviction, DMV was required to suspend a first offender's license unless the court granted probation. DMV argued that the actual effect of amended § 13352 was prospective rather than retroactive, and therefore, its operation was valid. Affirming the lower court decision, the court held that amended § 13352(a)(1), should not have applied to respondent, who was convicted under former § 23102(a) because the amended provision could not be applied retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that respondent department of motor vehicles was without authority to suspend respondent's license, and the petition for writ of mandate was properly granted.

 

1123 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. George


No. A011504


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


157 Cal. App. 3d 1053; 204 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2265



June 29, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Parts 1, 2, 3, and 4 are not certified for publication. The facts need not be published because they are not necessary to the section to be published. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 976.1).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-9395, Robert E. Carey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded to the trial court for exercise of the court's discretion to determine whether or not there is a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances, and for such further sentencing proceedings if and as required after the ruling on that issue. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187. The court found that §190.2 was constitutional because the law of California provided corrective mechanisms for individualizing, when appropriate, a potential punishment of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. The court further stated that the trial court had jurisdiction to impose a sentence of life in prison without the possibility of parole in a noncapital case. The court found that appellant was statutorily eligible for the death penalty. The court reasoned that if the trial court could not impose a life sentence in noncapital cases, a prosecutor who wanted only a sentence of life without possibility of parole would have to expose the defendant to the possibility of death in order to achieve the lesser sentence. Last, the court held appellant was entitled to a hearing to permit the trial court to consider whether to exercise its discretion under § 1385 to dismiss special circumstance findings in order to make it possible for appellant to be eligible for parole and remanded that portion of the judgment. Otherwise, the judgment appealed from was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court remanded a sentencing issue to the trial court to determine if there was a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances and affirmed the rest of the judgment. The court held appellant could be sentenced to life in prison without possibility of parole because he was statutorily eligible for the death penalty and that the statute was constitutional because the sentence could be individualized.

Feminist Women's Health Ctr. v. Philibosian


Civ. No. 69838


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


157 Cal. App. 3d 1076; 203 Cal. Rptr. 918; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2268



June 29, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 17, 1984, and the petitions of all respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied September 20, 1984. Lucas, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 413606, Eli Chernow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court which is directed to enter a declaratory judgment consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant women's center sought review of a judgment from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted respondent district attorney and respondent Catholic league summary adjudication in appellant's suit seeking to enjoin respondent attorney's release of fetuses for a private, non-secular burial ceremony. Appellant claimed that respondent attorney's action violated establishment and free exercise clauses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant women's center appealed a judgment that granted respondent district attorney and respondent Catholic league summary adjudication of issues in appellant's suit that sought to enjoin respondent attorney's release of fetuses for a private, non-secular burial ceremony. The fetuses were recovered from a doctor who had not complied with state law regarding the proper disposal of fetal tissue. The court reversed the judgment. The court agreed with appellant's claims that the proposed release of the fetuses violated the establishment and free exercise clauses of the state constitution. Cal. Const. amend. art. I, § 4 and art. XVI, § 5. The court ruled that, under strict scrutiny analysis, the proposed release showed an unconstitutional bias and partiality towards respondent attorney's religious beliefs. The court ruled that the proposed release: (1) lacked a clear, secular purpose; (2) would have impermissibly demonstrated governmental support for respondent's religious beliefs, and; (3) created excessive political entanglement with religion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that granted respondents district attorney and Catholic league summary adjudication of issues in appellant's suit seeking to enjoin respondent attorney's release of fetuses for a private, non-secular burial ceremony. The state free exercise and establishment of religion clauses precluded respondent attorney from releasing the fetuses to a private cemetery where respondent Catholic League planned a religious ceremony.

Honey Springs Homeowners Ass'n v. Bd. of Supervisors


Civ. No. 28701


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


157 Cal. App. 3d 1122; 203 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2271



June 29, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied July 19, 1984. Lewis, J., * was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted. The petitions of all respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied September 27, 1984. Kaus, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 490638, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the superior court with directions to issue a writ of mandamus requiring the Board to vacate its cancellation of the Williamson Act agreements and to conduct such further proceedings as it deems appropriate consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) cancelling a 10-year contract established under the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 51200 et seq., and approving a land development project because the development was rural and did not leapfrog growth from other population centers.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners contended the trial court was wrong in approving a controversial land development project. Petitioners asserted that respondent Board of Supervisors of San Diego County wrongly cancelled a 10-year contract signed by prior landowners. The land was designed as agricultural preserve under the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 51200 et seq. The company, real party in interest, contended that the land was rural and thus would not violate legal restrictions against urban sprawl. Respondent decided that the Williamson Act contract could be canceled for the project because the development was rural. In approving the project, respondent found that its development was not big enough to be considered urban, and therefore did not constitute leapfrog growth. The court reversed and remanded. The court held that respondent ran afoul of laws detailing when land may be removed from agricultural preserve status and approved for development. Respondent had to reconsider the project and the issue of contiguity. In deciding not to consider a new law that would allow the project, the court said, the legislature had no right to pass the bill because it tried to interpret a previous law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandamus requiring respondent Board of Supervisors of San Diego County to vacate its cancellation of the Williamson Act agreements. The court was satisfied the record presently contained little, if any, evidence to suggest respondent could have factually supported a finding that the project met the statutory requirement of contiguity.

Universal Life Church v. Cal.


No. G001012


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


158 Cal. App. 3d 533; 205 Cal. Rptr. 11; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2335



June 29, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 22, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 411348, Theodore E. Millard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. In addition, the stay is dissolved, and the petition for writ of supersedeas is denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant community care facilities sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted respondent State of California's motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin appellants from operating in appellants' action for declaratory and injunction relief. Appellants claimed that they was exempt from licensure under the Community Care Facilities Act, Cal. Health & Safety. Code § 1500 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant community care facilities filed an action for declaratory and injunctive relief to enjoin respondent State of California from inspecting their facilities. Respondent presented a motion for preliminary injunction enjoining appellants from operating that the trial court granted. On appeal, appellants contended that they were exempt from licensure under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1505(g). The court rejected this contention, finding that appellants were not exempt from obtaining licenses because appellants did not provide care and treatment in the form of prayer or spiritual means as defined under § 1505(g). The court held that respondent was entitled to an injunction as a matter of right because appellants were not exempt and were operating without licenses in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1508. The court rejected appellants' claim that § 1505(g) was unconstitutional due to lack of standing. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting respondent State of California's preliminary injunction to enjoin appellant community care facilities from operating. The court found that appellants were not exempt from licensure and were operating without licenses. Therefore, the grant of the preliminary injunction was proper.
