Nally v. Grace Community Church


Civ. No. 67200.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


157 Cal. App. 3d 912; 204 Cal. Rptr. 303; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2256



June 28, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 30, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NOC 18668B, Thomas C. Murphy, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents brought suit for wrongful death and intentional infliction of emotional distress against defendant church and pastors, alleging that their conduct had caused plaintiffs' son to kill himself. They appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) granting summary judgment to defendants.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the lower court's order granting summary judgment to defendant church and pastors whom plaintiff parents had sued for wrongful death and intentional infliction of emotional distress, alleging that their conduct had caused the suicide of plaintiffs' son. It found that defendants had failed to establish that there was no triable issue of fact with respect to plaintiffs' claims, finding that there was evidence that defendants, knowing the decedent's suicidal tendencies, had exacerbated his guilt, anxiety, and depression with reckless disregard and that they had engaged in extreme or outrageous conduct by encouraging him to kill himself. The court then held that the action was not barred by U.S. Const. amend. I, writing that although defendants' religious beliefs were constitutionally protected, this did not shield them from liability for their actions.


OUTCOME: In a case where plaintiff parents filed suit against defendant church and pastors for wrongful death and intentional infliction of emotional distress, alleging that defendants had caused the suicide of plaintiffs' son, the court reversed the lower court's grant of summary judgment to defendants. It found that defendants had not proven that there was no triable issue of fact and that plaintiffs' claims were not barred by the First Amendment.

 

1128 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Deal v. Mun. Court


Civ. No. 69418


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


157 Cal. App. 3d 991; 204 Cal. Rptr. 79; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2261



June 28, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 24, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 56303, Harry E. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Real party in interest and appellant landlord challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) which granted respondent tenants a writ of mandate wherein respondent municipal court was ordered to vacate its denial of respondents' motion to quash service of summons and grant the motion.


OVERVIEW: Respondent tenants sought to quash service of a summons in an unlawful detainer action filed by real party in interest and appellant landlord for the eviction of tenants. Under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1167, a defendant in a detainer action had five days after service of summons to answer or otherwise plead. Respondent municipal court denied the motion to quash, and respondent tenants petitioned for a writ of mandate. The lower court granted the writ thereby ordering respondent municipal court to vacate its order and grant the motion to quash. The court of appeal reversed. Section 1167 was neither a due process nor an equal protection constitutional violation. The detainer statutes were created to expeditiously allow for the orderly eviction of tenants and protect the landlord's property. Tenants had other forms of defenses which could be raised, and the state-provided answer form contained these defenses in non-technical language. Furthermore, tenants could get extensions of time to answer for good cause shown.


OUTCOME: Judgment granting a writ of mandamus to respondent tenants and ordering respondent trial court to vacate its order denying respondent tenants' motion to quash service of summons reversed following real party in interest and appellant landlord's eviction action against respondent tenants. The five day period for tenants to answer or otherwise plead following service in an unlawful detainer action was not unconstitutional.

 

1129 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Oceanside Mobilehome Park Owners' Ass'n v. City of Oceanside


Civ. No. 28680


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


157 Cal. App. 3d 887; 204 Cal. Rptr. 239; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2255



June 27, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 19, 1984, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N-17905, Gilbert Nares, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which preliminarily enjoined appellant from applying and enforcing its mobilehome park rent control ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mobilehome park owners' association challenged the constitutionality of appellant city's mobilehome park rent control ordinance and requested a preliminary and permanent injunction, a declaration of its unconstitutionality, attorney fees and costs, and damages. The trial court entered an order preliminarily enjoining appellant from applying and enforcing its ordinance. On appeal, the court concluded that the delay inherent in the rent adjustment procedures of the ordinance was not substantially greater than was practically necessary and, thus, was reasonable and constitutionally permissible. Further, the court concluded that the ordinance's automatic or permissive adjustment formula permitting the rent to be increased by a certain percentage in order to guarantee park owners a fair Net Operating Standard (NOI) was facially constitutional. The ordinance permitted park owners to adjust for inflation to maintain the same NOI they enjoyed in the rental market before rent control. The court held that the trial court erred in holding the ordinance did not include general market conditions as a factor in setting and adjusting rents. The court reversed the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's preliminary injunction against appellant city's application and enforcement of its mobilehome park rent control ordinance. The ordinance insured a just and reasonable return under general market conditions, and the trial court erred in holding the ordinance did not include general market conditions as a factor in setting and adjusting rents.

 

1130 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hudson Properties Co. v. Governing Board


Civ. No. 51648.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


157 Cal. App. 3d 627; 203 Cal. Rptr. 740; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2232



June 25, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted July 25, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 434178, Read Ambler, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which found null and void any agreement by defendant school district to sell the property to defendant former owners pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 39369.5. Plaintiff bidder challenged that portion of the judgment holding that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to order the sale of the property to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that Cal. Educ. Code § 39369.5 applied retroactively to defendant former owners, that defendant former owners had granted an option to defendant speculator, and that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to compel sale of the property to plaintiff bidder. The court found that § 39369.5 operated retroactively and that defendant former owners were entitled to the right to purchase property being sold by defendant school district at the tentatively accepted highest bid made by plaintiff. The court found that defendant former owners' agreement to purchase the property with funding from defendant speculator and later with funding from defendant corporation and then convey the property to defendant corporation was not an option but an assignment, which was invalid under the statute. The court reversed and concluded that defendant school district accepted plaintiff's tentative bid and, if defendant former owners declined to exercise their right to repurchase the property, defendant school district must sell to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed with directions to the trial court to order defendant school district to give defendant former owners the opportunity to exercise their right of first refusal and if they did not exercise that right, to sell the property to plaintiff bidder. The court found that defendant owners assignment of their right of first refusal to defendant speculator was invalid.

 

1131 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Olsaver


No. F001960.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


157 Cal. App. 3d 596; 204 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2230



June 22, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified July 20, 1984; Hg. granted Sept. 13, 1984 (Crim. 23979) May 1, 1986 Trans to Ct. App., 5th Dist., for reconsideration in light of People v. Fritz (1985) 40 Cal. 3d 227
Opn. filed June 6, 1986 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 286790-1, Leonard Irving Meyers, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which sentenced defendant to the mid term of three years for robbery and imposed a five-year enhancement for his prior conviction of manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and sentenced to the mid term of three years for robbery and a five-year enhancement for a prior conviction of manslaughter, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667. Defendant appealed, challenging § 667 on three theories: (1) the enhancement provision constituted an ex post facto law; (2) the provisions constituted cruel and unusual punishment; and (3) the trial court abused its discretion in failing to strike the enhancement pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385. The court affirmed. Because defendant committed the robbery well after the enactment of § 667, no ex post facto proscription arose from the enactment of that statute. Furthermore, because § 667 applied only to certain serious felonies, the court saw no basis to declare the statute cruel or unusual punishment per se. Finally, the court held that there was no abuse of discretion because § 667 was not sufficiently specific to divest the trial court of its discretion to strike a prior conviction which was alleged for purposes of sentence enhancement. Moreover, under § 1385, the interests of justice would not be served by unnecessary appellate review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision sentencing defendant to the mid term of three years for robbery and imposing a five-year enhancement for his prior conviction of manslaughter because no ex post facto proscription arose because the robbery was committed well after the enactment of the provision.

In re King


Crim. No. 17470


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


157 Cal. App. 3d 554; 204 Cal. Rptr. 39; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2227



June 22, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the North Orange County Judicial District of Orange County, No. NM8306257, Daniel T. Brice, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The Municipal Court for the North Orange County Judicial District is ordered to modify the conditions of probation by eliminating the requirement that King register as a sex offender pursuant to section 290. The writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the condition of probation, which ordered him to register as a sex offender pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290 entered by the Municipal Court for the North Orange County Judicial District of Orange County, (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to one count of misdemeanor indecent exposure in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 314(1). The trial court suspended imposition of sentence and granted three years summary probation. As a condition of probation, defendant was ordered to register as a sex offender pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290. Defendant sought on appeal a writ of habeas corpus contending the mandatory registration requirement violated the constitutional prohibition against cruel or unusual punishment. The court concluded that the continuing penalty of sex offender registration was out of proportion to the crime of misdemeanor indecent exposure, and that registration pursuant to § 290 cannot constitutionally be imposed as punishment for persons convicted of misdemeanors under § 314.1, therefore the judgment was modified to eliminate the registration requirement.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court by eliminating the requirement that defendant register as a sex offender, and otherwise denied the writ of habeas corpus.

 

1133 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Andre R.


No. A021534


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


158 Cal. App. 3d 336; 204 Cal. Rptr. 723; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2316



June 21, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, Juvenile Court No. 119824, Wilmont Sweeney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which adjudged defendant to be a person described in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 upon the finding that he violated Cal. Penal Code § 12021.5 for possession of a concealable firearm by a minor unless such minor had written permission or was accompanied by a parent or guardian.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed an order adjudging him to be a person described in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 upon finding that he committed a misdemeanor in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.5 for possession of a concealable firearm by a minor unless such minor had written permission or was accompanied by a parent or guardian. The appellate court affirmed the judgment of conviction because written parental permission excused a minor from the sanctions imposed by § 12021.5 and would have to be raised by defense counsel, and there was sufficient evidence to support the conviction because there was circumstantial evidence linking him to the gun. The appellate court stated that the existence of written parental permission did not define or describe the offense. The offense was the possession of a concealable firearm by a minor. Subjecting defendant to the burden of producing exonerating evidence would not have been unduly harsh or unfair. The appellate court also stated that there was ample evidence, albeit circumstantial evidence, to support defendant's conviction. The appellate court did not address the order of probation because this issue was rendered moot by a subsequent order.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of a concealable firearm by a minor because written parental permission excused defendant, a minor, from the sanctions imposed by the statutory provision and would have to be raised by defense counsel, and there was sufficient evidence to support defendant's conviction because there was circumstantial evidence linking him to the gun found on the sidewalk.

 

1134 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Maita


No. A019840


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


157 Cal. App. 3d 309; 203 Cal. Rptr. 685; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2205



June 19, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 19, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-106000, Zerne P. Haning III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged his convictions in the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) on charges of pimping, pandering, keeping a house of ill fame and keeping a house used for prostitution stemming from his management and ownership of a theater specializing in sexually explicit live entertainment.


OVERVIEW: Following his conviction on charges related to his management and ownership of a theater specializing in sexually explicit live entertainment, appellant contended that his convictions could not stand because the on-stage performances at the theater were protected by U.S. Const. amend. I, the evidence was insufficient to support the pimping and pandering convictions, and the evidence of appellant's management and operation of the theater was derived from unlawfully seized evidence. The court acknowledged that the state's unquestioned authority to prosecute under the pimping and pandering laws presented an incidental restriction on appellant's First Amendment freedoms. However, that restriction was no greater than was essential to further the substantial government interest in controlling prostitution. The court found that the pimping and pandering laws did not prohibit live, nude entertainment, but merely directed that the entertainers could not have sexual relations with the audience. The court concluded that the application of the statutes in question to the on-stage performances at the theater did not unconstitutionally infringe upon freedom of speech or expression.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment on the basis that the government had a substantial interest in limiting prostitution and its related commercial endeavors of pimping and pandering and the activities at appellant's theater were not constitutionally protected.

J. W. Jones Cos. v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 28765


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


157 Cal. App. 3d 745; 203 Cal. Rptr. 580; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2242



June 14, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 2, 1984, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1984. Lucas, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 485536, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the court is directed to vacate the injunction enjoining further exercise of the FBA ordinance and to deny the petition for writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) granting plaintiffs' request for a peremptory writ of mandate requiring defendants to set aside a resolution imposing a facilities benefit assessment on undeveloped property.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted plaintiffs' request for a peremptory writ of mandate requiring defendants to set aside a resolution imposing a facilities benefit assessment (FBA) on undeveloped property, and enjoining defendants from adopting any FBA resolutions imposing similar assessments on any other property in the city. The court held that the FBA was a valid exercise of defendants' police power to impose charges upon property within a designated area to pay for public facilities that served the needs of those who reside in the area. The court stated that the charges were not special taxes that required voter approval under Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 (Proposition 13). Therefore, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and directed the court to vacate the injunction that enjoined further exercise of the FBA ordinance, and to deny the petition for a writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because a facilities benefit assessment was a valid exercise of defendants' police power to impose charges upon property within a designated area to pay for public facilities that served the public. The charges were not a special tax that required voter approval.

 

1136 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mitchell v. Superior Court


No. AO20782.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


156 Cal. App. 3d 1033; 203 Cal. Rptr. 379; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2158



June 7, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified July 6, 1984; Hg. granted Sept. 13, 1984 (S.F. 24790) (See 43 Cal. 3d 107)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners and appellant manager challenged a judgment of respondent, the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found each of the three guilty of 62 contempts arising out of the operation of a theater and imposed jail terms and substantial fines.


OVERVIEW: Respondent court held appellant owners and appellant manager in contempt for violating a preliminary injunction against allowing lewdness, obscenity, and prostitution at their theater pursuant to the Red Light Abatement Law, Cal. Penal Code § 11225 et seq. The court reversed as to appellant manager, because he was named neither as a defendant in the complaint, nor in the order to show cause re contempt, nor in the affidavits in support of the order to show cause. The court found that due to arithmetic error appellant owners could be punished for, at most, 56 separate acts of contempt. The court concluded that the contempt in the case was criminal, because the sanctions were plainly punitive. The court found that the concurrent six-month jail terms, considered alone, met the test for petty contempt; therefore, appellants were not entitled to a jury trial on that issue alone. The court concluded that fines of $ 1,000 for each act of contempt to be assessed against appellants should be deemed petty for purposes of jury-trial analysis. Therefore, the court concluded that appellants were not entitled to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment as it applied to appellant manager, because he was not properly made a defendant to the contempt proceeding. The court modified the judgment as to appellant owners by reducing the number of separate acts of contempt adjudged therein from 62 to 56; as so modified, the judgment was affirmed.

 

1137 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Battaglia


Crim. No. 44424


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


156 Cal. App. 3d 1058; 203 Cal. Rptr. 370; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2160



June 7, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 563615, Coleman A. Swart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in which he was found guilty of two counts of lewd or lascivious acts with a child under age 14, alleging his U.S. Const. amend. V rights were violated because admissions made to a psychotherapist were admitted and testimony of victim's mother were hearsay.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sexually molested his stepdaughter on two occasions. Several days later, appellant sought help from a neuropsychiatric institute. He was assigned to speak with a licensed clinical social worker and full time employee at the emergency walk-in psychiatric unit for evaluation. The clinical worker suspected appellant had sexually molested his stepdaughter and, under legal duty pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 11166, relayed the information given him by appellant to the UCLA child abuse center and contacted the campus police, who in turn notified the police department. Appellant was arrested and made a motion to suppress the admissions made to the clinical worker, and the testimony of both the victim and her mother. The trial court denied the motions and held the clinical worker's testimony was admissible although he did not testify. The court affirmed, holding that statements made to the clinical worker were voluntary and the patient-client privilege did not apply; the testimony would have been admissible had he testified. Prosecutor could introduce evidence to prevent the assumption that the offense did not occur because no complaint was made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions because a clinical social worker was not bound by the patient-client privilege, nor was he obligated to give appellant Miranda warnings, because appellant made statements voluntarily and was not in the custody of the state.

Sandrini Bros. v. Agric. Labor Relations Bd.


Civ. No. 7533


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


156 Cal. App. 3d 878; 203 Cal. Rptr. 304; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2142



June 1, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Parts I through III (including the appendix) are not published, as they do not meet the standards for publication contained in California Rules of Court, rule 976(b).


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 8, 1984. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION:  The order of the Board is enforced with the direction that when and if a court order is obtained pursuant to the provisions of Labor Code section 1160.8, it shall thereafter bear interest at the rate of 10 percent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner agricultural employer sought review of an order of respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (California), which found that petitioner committed unfair labor practices in the discharge of certain employees, the real parties in interest, and by engaging in unlawful surveillance of union organizers attempting to speak to the real parties, in violation of the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, Cal. Lab. Code § 1153(a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board found that petitioner agricultural employer engaged in unfair labor practices under Cal. Lab. Code § 1153(a), part of the Agricultural Labor Relations Act (Act), by discharging certain employees, the real parties in interest, and engaging in unlawful surveillance of union organizers who attempted to speak to the real parties. Respondent ordered petitioner to make the real parties whole for their losses plus interest. Petitioner sought review of the ruling. The court affirmed and noted that the case would be remanded for respondent to conduct back pay proceedings. The court held that respondent was not a court under Cal. Const. art., VI, § 1 and an award by respondent was not a court judgment. Respondent's remedies were designed to effectuate public policy and not merely to compensate for private loses. Therefore, the interest rate of ten percent under Cal. Const. art., XV, § 1 was inapplicable to an award under the Act. However, any court order obtained for back pay must bear interest at the rate of ten percent as provided by art. XV, § 1 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 685.010(a), because it then became a court judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board's order finding that petitioner agricultural employer committed unfair labor practices. If and when a court order was obtained for back pay for certain employees, the real parties in interest, who were wrongfully discharged by petitioner, the order must bear interest at the constitutional and statutory rate of ten percent.

 

1139 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Jaffe v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


No. A022259


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


156 Cal. App. 3d 719; 202 Cal. Rptr. 812; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2126



May 31, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 785659, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unemployment insurance appeals board challenged a judgment from the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandate requiring payment of five-sevenths of a particular week's unemployment benefits to respondent claimant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant unemployment insurance appeals board affirmed the decision of the administrative law judge, which found respondent claimant ineligible to receive benefits for a particular week because he was unavailable to work two days in order to observe Jewish holidays. Respondent filed a petition for peremptory writ of mandate. The trial court granted the writ and ordered payment of benefits for the week at issue, minus two-sevenths of the amount of said benefits to represent the days he was unavailable. Appellant challenged. The court held that there was no valid constitutional distinction between a permanent disqualification and a temporary interruption of unemployment benefits. Under appellant's interpretation of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1253(c), respondent was required to surrender his constitutional right of free exercise of religion in order to receive state benefits to which he would otherwise have been entitled. Under these circumstances, the trial court's interpretation of § 1253(c) to permit apportionment of benefits on a daily, rather than a weekly, basis was constitutionally compelled. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate ordering payment of five-sevenths of a particular week's unemployment benefits to respondent claimant because the denial of benefits for the entire week was contrary to respondent's constitutional right to free exercise of religion. Thus, permitting apportionment of benefits on a daily, rather than weekly, basis was constitutionally compelled in the instant case.

People v. Younghanz


Crim. No. 17111


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


156 Cal. App. 3d 811; 202 Cal. Rptr. 907; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2135



May 31, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 15, 1984. A petition for a rehearing was denied June 20, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1984. Mosk, J., and Grodin, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C 48775, James L. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), in which he was convicted of two counts of incest under Cal. Penal Code § 285, one count of sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286(b)(1), and one count of oral copulation under Cal. Penal Code § 288(a)(b)(1), and sentenced to a total of four years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant revealed to a counselor that he had been having intercourse with his deaf-mute daughter for eight years. The abuse was reported to the authorities pursuant to the Child Abuse Reporting Act, Cal. Penal Code § 11165 et seq. Defendant was convicted of incest under Cal. Penal Code § 285, sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286(b)(1), and oral copulation under Cal. Penal Code § 288(a)(b)(1), and sentenced to four years' imprisonment. Defendant challenged his conviction, and the court affirmed. The court denied defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of the Child Abuse Reporting Act because the right to seek a cure for an illness was not a fundamental right and the state's interest in protecting children was more than justified. The disclosure requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 11166 did not violate defendants rights under U.S. Const. amend. V because the psychologist was not a "state agent" and defendant waived his rights when he revealed incriminating information after being told of the reporting requirement. Defendant failed to show prejudice due to his failure to receive a complete record of his daughter's testimony because he was fluent in sign language.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for incest, sodomy, and oral copulation based on the sexual abuse of his daughter. The court found that the Child Abuse Reporting Act did not violate defendant's right to due process because defendant failed to show a fundamental right and the state had a strong interest in protecting children. Defendant's fifth amendment rights were not violated because the counselor was not a state agent.

Communications Satellite Corp. v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. A016317


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


156 Cal. App. 3d 726; 203 Cal. Rptr. 779; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2127



May 31, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1984, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 746930, Donald B. King, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter a judgment in favor of defendant and appellant Franchise Tax Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant franchise tax board challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California), which ordered appellant to refund the disputed taxes plus interest to appellee corporation in appellee's action for refund of income taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellee corporation brought suit against appellant franchise tax board for a refund of income taxes appellee paid pursuant to appellant's reassessment. The complaint alleged that the taxes were illegally, erroneously, and arbitrarily determined. The trial court entered a judgment for appellee. Appellant challenged. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court with directions to enter a judgment for appellant. The application of a taxing statute to uncontradicted facts was a question of law; thus, the court was not bound by the findings of fact made by the trial court. Appellant relied on the authority granted to it by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25137 in reassessing appellee's income taxes. Furthermore, appellant's method of calculating the property factor was reasonable because it was authorized by § 25137(d). Likewise, appellant's calculation of the sales factor was reasonable because it applied the concept of sales in the state, which was provided in Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25134. The state taxes did not violate the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and appellant's application of the statute was not an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment ordering appellant franchise tax board to refund the disputed taxes plus interest to appellee corporation because appellant was authorized by statute to recalculate the taxes and its calculation was reasonable under the law. The court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to enter a judgment for appellant.

 

1142 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Scott v. Superior Court


No. B003176


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


156 Cal. App. 3d 577; 202 Cal. Rptr. 920; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2113



May 30, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner natural father, a California resident, sought a writ of mandate to vacate an order of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion for summary judgment in a support suit filed by his adult dependent daughter, who was the real party in interest and a New York resident.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner natural father, a California resident, moved for summary judgment in a suit for support filed by his adult dependent daughter, real party in interest. Real party in interest suffered from a mental disability that rendered her extremely disturbed and unable to function independently. Real party claimed that she was financially in need within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Code § 206. Petitioner claimed that under California's governmental interest approach to choice of law questions, New York was the only state having a true interest in the application of its law. The trial court denied petitioner's motion. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to vacate the order denying his motion. The court denied the writ and concluded that the Uniform Reciprocal Enforcement of Support Act, which was codified in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1650-1699, required the application of California law as the law of the state where the alleged obligor was present. Cal. Civ. Code § 206, which imposed a primary support obligation upon California resident parents, served legitimate and compelling state interests, did not create any invidious class distinctions, and did not penalize interstate migration.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and held that the Uniform Reciprocal Enforcement of Support Act, which required application of the law of the state where the obligor was present, mandated that California law apply in the support suit filed against petitioner natural father by his adult dependent daughter, the real party in interest. Respondent superior court properly denied petitioner's motion for summary judgment.

 

1143 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



L.A. County Fed'n of Labor v. County of L.A.


No. B002356


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


160 Cal. App. 3d 905; 207 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2597



May 29, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-435415, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent union's peremptory writ of mandate enjoining enforcement of a county charter section which provided for the discharge of striking employees.


OVERVIEW: The voters adopted a charter amendment that provided for the discipline and discharge of striking employees of appellant county. The trial court granted respondent union's peremptory writ of mandate enjoining enforcement of the amended charter section. The court affirmed and held that one provision was preempted by state law, other provisions were unconstitutional and the remaining portions could not be severed. The provision barring improvements in working conditions prior to the next "meet and confer" negotiations were preempted by the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 3500 et seq. The disciplinary provisions applicable to any employee who instigated, or participated in a strike were unconstitutional because those terms were not defined and the language was too broad. The provision requiring that the employee agree to be bound by the disciplinary sections was invalid because the disciplinary sections were unenforceable. The invalid provisions could not be severed because the remainder would be unintelligible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that granted respondent union's peremptory writ of mandate. The court held that the "meet and confer" subdivision of the charter amendment was preempted by state law, other provisions were unconstitutional as overbroad, and the severance of the invalid provisions would make the remainder unintelligible.

 

1144 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Pasillas v. Agric. Labor Relations Bd.


No. A021137


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


156 Cal. App. 3d 312; 202 Cal. Rptr. 739; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2092



May 24, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied June 20, 1984, and the applications of petitioners Pasillas and Navarro for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied August 8, 1984. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION: The claims of Navarro concerning retroactive application of the union security provision and her free speech and association rights, as well as the UFW's exhaustion of remedies argument, all lack merit. To that extent, we agree with the Board's decision, although we have concluded that the Board misconceived the extent of its review function under section 1153, subdivision (c), of the Act and therefore never undertook to balance the need for the penalty of suspension. However, the Board's decision is upholdable on the basis of its due process ruling alone. The decision in 8 A.L.R.B. No. 104 is therefore affirmed, and the order is enforced in full.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner agricultural workers challenged the decision of respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (California), upholding real party in interest union's expulsion or suspension from employment for loss of good standing as a result of their crossing picket lines during a strike when their collective bargaining agreement had expired.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner union workers brought unfair labor charges against real party in interest union claiming that they could not be discharged from employment under a union security agreement. Petitioners were expelled or suspended for alleged strikebreaking activities occurring during a hiatus between valid collective bargaining agreements, when no union security provisions were in force. Respondent compelled each of their discharges for bad standing after newly negotiated collective bargaining agreements containing union security provisions went into effect. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the security provisions utilized were properly given retroactive effect because the language of the California Agricultural Labor Relations Act neither required nor forbid retroactive application. The court further concluded that retroactive application did not substantially impair petitioners' protected associational activities. Finally, the court held that respondent labor relations board did not abuse its discretion in concluding that respondent's rule requiring a member to respect its strikes and picket lines was a reasonable condition within the meaning of Cal. Lab. Code § 1153(c).


OUTCOME: Respondent agricultural labor relations board's decision was affirmed and the order suspending or expelling petitioner union workers from employment for loss of good standing was enforced in full because petitioners' claims concerning retroactive application of real party in interest union's security provision and free speech and association rights, as well as the exhaustion of remedies argument, lacked merit.

 

1145 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Smith


Crim. No. 11314


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


155 Cal. App. 3d 1103; 203 Cal. Rptr. 196; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2058



May 17, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 12, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 36199, Robert W. Conyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, Smith's convictions of tax fraud are affirmed as misdemeanor violations of Revenue and Taxation Code section 19401 for the years 1971 and 1973 and the cause is remanded for resentencing unless within 30 days of the issuance of the remittitur the People file a written demand for a new trial on these counts. If such demand is filed, a new trial shall take place. 49



49 As recently stated in People v. Alexander (1983) 140 Cal.App.3d 647, at page 666 [189 Cal.Rptr. 906]: "An appellate court is not restricted to the remedies of affirming or reversing a judgment. Where the prejudicial error goes only to the degree of the offense for which the defendant was convicted, the appellate court may reduce the conviction to a lesser degree and affirm the judgment as modified, thereby obviating the necessity for a retrial. (See Pen. Code, § 1260; People v. Harris (1968) 266 Cal.App.2d 426, 434-435 [72 Cal.Rptr. 423.] Such a modification of judgment is appropriate where there was error in failing to instruct on lesser included offenses but the evidence clearly establishes guilt of a lesser degree of the offense for which he was convicted. ( People v. Bailey (1974) 38 Cal.App.3d 693, 700 [113 Cal.Rptr. 514].)"

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction, entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), of tax fraud for various years, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 19405 and 19406, and one count of theft, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 487(1). Defendant contended that it was improper to use a presumption that official duty had been regularly performed, as it imposed an improper burden of proof.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for tax fraud and theft. He disagreed with the court's finding of no discriminatory prosecution and contended the wrong burden of proof was imposed. It was presumed that official duty had been regularly performed. This rebuttable presumption affected the burden of proof, and imposed upon the party against whom it operated the burden of proof as to the nonexistence of the presumed fact. This presumption was employed in assessing the defense of discriminatory prosecution, and imposed the burden of proof on defendant. While the burden imposed was not clearly identified, the indications were that it was erroneously high, but harmless. Defendant also challenged the characterization of his theft conviction as embezzlement. While the transaction could not have been embezzlement, sufficient evidence supported a conviction of theft by false pretenses. However, the prosecution presented its case on alternate theories, some of which were legally incorrect, and the reviewing court could not determine on which theory the ensuing general verdict of guilt rested, so the conviction could not stand. The court also addressed state tax crimes.


OUTCOME: The convictions of tax fraud were affirmed, but as misdemeanor violations of the state's Revenue and Taxation Code. The cause was remanded for resentencing, unless within 30 days of issuance of the remittitur the state filed a written demand for a new trial. If such demand is filed, a new trial should take place. As to the count of theft, the prosecutor relied on an embezzlement theory which could not have been sustained as a matter of law.

 

1146 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Taylor Bus Service v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 27623.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


156 Cal. App. 3d 820; 202 Cal. Rptr. 433; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2136



May 14, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified June 12, 1984; Hg. den. July 13, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 356334, Robert R. Fitzgerald, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bus service sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which denied petition for a writ of mandamus under Cal. Civ. P. Code § 1094.5 and charged registration fees and penalties after a hearing officer found that appellant used buses without paying fees.


OVERVIEW: Respondent motor vehicles department claimed that appellant bus service used buses for nonexempt transportation without paying the requisite registration fees. After unsuccessfully attempting to obtain appellant's business records through an administrative subpoena, respondent sought a search warrant under an affidavit which alleged that the records would establish a violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 484/487, grand theft. When a hearing officer charged appellant registration fees and penalties and the trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5, appellant sought review. The court reversed. The trial court considered illegally seized evidence when it reviewed appellant's records, which fell under the exclusionary rule on an invalid search warrant, and absent that evidence, decision lacked substantial evidence. Appellant's conduct did not fit the crime of theft under Cal. Penal Code §484. The trial court erred in failing to recognize that hearing officer committed two errors of law in finding the search warrant valid and in allowing records into evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed denial of appellant's petition for writ of mandamus because the trial court considered illegally seized evidence when it reviewed appellant's administrative records which fell under the exclusionary rule on an invalid search warrant, and absent the inadmissible evidence, the record lacked substantial evidence to support decision.

 

1147 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Fresno v. Superior Court


No. F003641.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


155 Cal. App. 3d 678; 202 Cal. Rptr. 313; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2021



May 10, 1984.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of a judgment from respondent Superior Court of Fresno County (California) which entered summary judgment for real parties in interest taxpayers in their suit against petitioner that claimed that City of Fresno, Cal., Ordinance 83-116 was an invalid tax as a violation of state constitution.


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest taxpayers sued petitioner city, claiming that City of Fresno, Cal., Ordinance 83-116 was invalid as a violation of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, which limited the amount of property taxes that could be assessed. After respondent superior court held that the tax was invalid and entered summary judgment for real parties in interest, petitioner sought writ of mandate asking the court to direct respondent to set aside its summary judgment and enter summary judgment for petitioner. The court issued a writ of mandate to set aside summary judgment for real parties in interest and to enter summary judgment for petitioner. The court held that the tax levied by Ordinance 83-116 was valid to the extent the proceeds of the tax were used to fund the level of retirement benefits in existence at the time petitioner adopted the charter. The court concluded that level of retirement benefits as of the date of the adoption of the city charter had to be construed to include all of the indebtednesses incurred by petitioner in maintaining the level of retirement benefits that existed in 1957 for all current and future employees.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate issue that directed respondent superior court to set aside summary judgment for real parties in interest taxpayers because tax was valid to the extent the proceeds were used to fund the level of retirement benefits in existence at the time petitioner city adopted the charter.

 

1148 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mann v. Mack


No. B001665


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


155 Cal. App. 3d 666; 202 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2020



May 10, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 4, 1984, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 20, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC 62465, Thomas C. Murphy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individuals sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which rendered judgment in favor of defendant city and police officer in plaintiffs' cause of action that alleged a violation of U.S. Const. amend IV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff individuals contended that defendant city and police officer violated their constitutional rights when defendant city repeatedly investigated noise complaints against plaintiff and defendant officer entered plaintiffs' home to arrest one of the plaintiffs. On appeal, plaintiffs argued that the trial court erred when it concluded that the arrest did not involve a violation of plaintiffs' constitutional rights or a cause of action for invasion of privacy. The court stated that it was clear that plaintiffs did not put on evidence sufficient to sustain their allegations that the police action in generally investigating complaints of noise disturbances at the plaintiffs' home violated plaintiffs' right to privacy. The court stated that defendant police officer had probable cause to arrest plaintiff son due to the noise violation and when plaintiff son failed to come out of the home when ordered to do so, defendant police officer was acting reasonably when he concluded that plaintiff son was attempting to evade him. The court stated that entry into the home to forestall plaintiff son's escape was an exception to the warrant requirements. Affirmed.


OUTCOME: Trial court's judgment in favor of defendant city and police officer was affirmed. Defendant police officer's entry into the home after plaintiff son attempted to evade arrest was an exception to the warrant requirements and not violative of plaintiffs' rights.

 

1149 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Fresno v. Superior Court


No. F003641


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


156 Cal. App. 3d 1137; 202 Cal. Rptr. 313; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2168



May 10, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 7, 1984, and the petition of real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 11, 1984.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing the Superior Court of Fresno County in action No. 300260-7 to set aside its summary judgment in favor of Taxpayers and order denying summary judgment in favor of the City, and to enter a partial summary judgment in favor of the City upholding the tax to the extent the proceeds thereof are to be utilized to meet the City's obligation to maintain retirement benefits at the level that existed under the CERS and FAPRS as of the date of the approval of the 1957 City Charter for all present and future employees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city filed a petition for a writ of mandate to direct respondent, the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), to vacate its order granting real parties in interest's motion for summary judgment and enter summary judgment in petitioner's favor thereby upholding an ordinance enacted by petitioner which imposed a property tax in order to raise proceeds for petitioner's employees' retirement system.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city enacted an ordinance which imposed a property tax to raise proceeds for payment into petitioner's employees' retirement system. The ordinance stated the funds were measures approved by the voters prior to the effective date of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, which limited the amount of property taxes that could be assessed. Real parties in interest sued petitioner asserting the ordinance violated art. XIII A, and respondent granted summary judgment in their favor. Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of mandate asking the court to direct respondent to enter summary judgment in its favor. The court held that here, only petitioner's charter had been approved by real parties. However, because the charter provided for the establishment of pension funds, so long as retirement benefits established by ordinances already in existence would not be reduced or diminished, and because there were in existence at the time of the charter ordinances which contained provisions entitling benefits at retirement, real parties' approval of the charter constituted prior approval of petitioner's ability to raise taxes to fiance retirement systems costs under art. XIII A. Writ granted.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ directing respondent to vacate its order granting summary judgment in real parties' favor and to enter summary judgment in petitioner's favor, where because real parties' adoption of petitioner's charter constituted prior approval of petitioner's ability to raise taxes to finance retirement costs, trial court's invalidation of ordinances adopted in order to raise proceeds to finance those systems constituted error.

 

1150 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Western Airlines


Civ. No. 28920


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


155 Cal. App. 3d 597; 202 Cal. Rptr. 237; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2011



May 8, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 26, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 494202, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Western contends state regulation of advertising by interstate air carriers unconstitutionally burdens interstate commerce. However, Western points to nothing in this record supporting such contention as a matter of law. Nothing in this record suggests California's false advertising statutes as a matter of law discriminate against air carriers engaged in interstate commerce or impinge upon an area of interstate commerce requiring uniform regulation. (See Head v. New Mexico Board, supra, 374 U.S. 424, 429 [10 L.Ed.2d 983, 988].) Any contention such statutes as applied to regulate Western's advertising unconstitutionally burden interstate commerce involves factual issues beyond the scope of demurrer. The superior court should have overruled Western's demurrer. The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) sustaining a demurrer on the basis of federal preemption in appellant's action against respondent airline for unfair competition, in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17500 and Cal. Civ. Code § 1770.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state sought civil penalties and an injunction against respondent airline for alleged false and misleading advertisements, in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17500 and Cal. Civ. Code § 1770. The trial court sustained a demurrer, finding that appellant's action was preempted by § 49 U.S.C.S. § 1305, and U.S. Const. art. I, § 8. The court reversed the trial court's decision. While observing that 49 U.S.C.S. §§ 1302, 1381 gave the Civil Aeronautics Board authority to regulate airlines against unfair and deceptive business practices, the court held that these statutes did not preclude states from also regulating these matters. Moreover, the court held that the regulatory schemes set forth in California law did not conflict with §§ 1302, 1381, and did not interfere with the objectives of these federal statutes. Finally, the court ruled that appellant's action was not preempted by U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, finding no evidence that California's false advertising statutes discriminated against air carriers engaged in interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court sustaining a demurrer because California's laws prohibiting unfair business practices were not preempted by federal statutory law or the Commerce Clause.

 

1151 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Interstate Marina Dev. Co. v. County of L.A.


Civ. No. 66560


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


155 Cal. App. 3d 435; 202 Cal. Rptr. 377; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1998



May 7, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. WEC 059213A and WEC 059213B, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporate lessees challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of respondent county declaring the county rent control law valid in all respects and constitutional as applied to appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporate lessees entered into a series of leases with respondent county and filed an action for declaratory relief, specific performance, and injunctive relief after respondent enacted a rent control law. The trial court declared the county rent law valid in all respects and constitutional as applied to appellants. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in its rulings. The court determined that the finding that a liveaboard ordinance was unconstitutional was not a collateral estoppel on the constitutionality of the rent law because the two laws differed significantly. A stringent examination under the Contracts Clause, U.S. Const. Art. I, 10, cl. 1, found that there was no unconstitutional impairment of the contract by the rent law because the control was a reasonable exercise of police power, necessary, and properly tailored to its legislative purpose of safeguarding tenants during a time of low vacancies and high rents. The rent law comported with constitutional due process, did not deprive appellants of equal protection of the laws, and did not breach the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order in favor of respondent county declaring a county rent control law valid in all respects and constitutional as applied to appellant corporate lessees because the law did not unconstitutionally impair the contract between appellants and respondent and was a reasonable exercise of police power.

 

1152 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Teachers Ass'n v. Cory


Civ. No. 23229


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


155 Cal. App. 3d 494; 202 Cal. Rptr. 611; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2002; 5 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1417



May 7, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 9, 1984. The petitions of real parties in interest and interveners for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied August 20, 1984.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the respondent State Controller to comply with his legal and ministerial duties imposed by section 23401 et seq. and to transfer the full amounts required by those provisions without limitation by the provisions of the State Budget Acts of 1980-1981, 1981-1982, 1982-1983 and 1983-1984. The Governor shall bear the costs of these proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners teachers association, individual public school teachers, teachers' retirement board, and individual board members sought an original peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent state controller (California) to transfer funds from the General Fund to the Teachers' Retirement Fund for underfunded prior years.


OVERVIEW: In 1979 Cal. Stat. 282, the legislature modified the method of state funding of the Teachers' Retirement Fund and required respondent state controller to transfer funds into it on a monthly basis with the amounts to be determined under Cal. Educ. Code §§ 23401 and 23402. For four years, the budgetary appropriation was for a reduced amount in lieu of the statutory appropriation. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate requested by petitioners teachers association, individual public school teachers, teachers' retirement board, and individual board members and directed respondent to transfer the full statutory amount without the budgetary limitations. The court held that Cal. Educ. Code §§ 23401 et seq. implied an intent to grant private rights of contract with the state's teachers and that when the promise to permanently fund the pension was accepted by the teachers by initial or continued employment, a contract was formed. The court found that the legislature had not retained power to modify the payment amounts and the underfunding was an impermissible impairment of a contractual funding obligation under both U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The court issued an original peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent state controller to transfer funds from the General Fund to the Teachers' Retirement Fund for underfunded prior years because an implied right of contract existed and the reduction of funds was an impermissible impairment of contract under both the U.S. and California constitutions.

 

1153 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Municipal Court


No. AO24300.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


154 Cal. App. 3d 996; 201 Cal. Rptr. 709; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1941



April 25, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted May 25, 1984.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal who was arraigned on misdemeanor theft and prostitution charges sought a writ of mandate to compel the San Francisco Municipal Court to consider her for diversion, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1001 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner criminal was charged with felony grand theft and prostitution, a misdemeanor. Subsequently, the grand theft offense was reduced to a misdemeanor. Thereafter, petitioner applied for an order of diversion, and the application was denied on the sole ground that, since the grand theft charge had been filed as a felony in the first instance, it could have been charged as a misdemeanor and she was ineligible for diversion. Petitioner contended that the local guidelines which deprived her of the benefits of the diversion statute denied her equal protection of laws under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court granted the application, holding that to the extent that Cal. Penal Code § 1001.2(b) purported to confer upon the local district attorney the right to control criteria for diversion, it was violative of Cal. Const. art. III, § 3. In so concluding, the court acknowledged that the Legislature had power to establish criteria governing diversion provided equal protection guarantees were scrupulously observed. Once having done so, however, it could not vest in the judiciary powers which are subject to prosecutorial control.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding the San Francisco Municipal Court to determine whether, in its view, petitioner should be diverted, notwithstanding the fact that she was charged, in the first instance, with a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1) as a felony.

 

1154 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pennell v. City of San Jose


No. AO11914.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


154 Cal. App. 3d 1019; 201 Cal. Rptr. 728; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1943



April 25, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 26, 1984 (See 42 Cal. 3d 365)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 435458, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), that ruled that a city ordinance violated appellee landlords' due process rights under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city adopted the San Jose Cal., Ordinance No. 19696, §§ 5703.28(c)(7), 5703.29, 5704.1, and 5704.2, titled San Jose Rental Dispute Mediation and Arbitration Ordinance (ordinance) to impose limits upon the rents paid by tenants, and which also imposed a rental unit fee to pay for the costs of administering the ordinance. The court affirmed the judgment that found that §§ 5703.28(c)(7) and 5703.29 of the ordinance violated appellee landlords' due process rights under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV, but that the rental unit fee, under §§ 5704.1 and 5704.2, was not a special tax and did not circumvent Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 4. The ordinance exhibited a constitutional infirmity upon its face, because it was totally arbitrary and unjustifiable to base rent upon a tenant's financial condition and ran counter to the purpose of rent control regulation. It also amounted to a forced subsidy on the part of the appellees. However, the rental unit fee imposed by the ordinance to pay for the cost of administering the hearing process for rent determinations was not a special tax and did not require a vote.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the ordinance in question, which based rents that appellee landlords' could charge tenant's based upon the tenant's financial situation, violated the due process clauses of the United States and California constitutions. Also affirmed was the determination that a rental unit fee imposed by the ordinance was not a special tax.

People v. Courie


No. AO17184.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


155 Cal. App. 3d 415; 201 Cal. Rptr. 736; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1995



April 25, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified May 17, 1984; Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Part III certified for publication. (Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1.)


Hg den. July 19, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 106049, S. Lee Vavuris, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of attempted voluntary manslaughter, and assault with a deadly weapon, which was entered by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted voluntary manslaughter and assault with a deadly weapon. In addition, an allegation of great bodily injury was found true. Appellant was sentenced to the middle term of two years for attempted voluntary manslaughter. Three years were added for the infliction of great bodily injury. Defendant argued that his punishment was cruel and unusual because it was greater than he would have received for the completed crime. The court concluded that attempted voluntary manslaughter, as punishable at the time sentence was imposed, was excepted from the application of the enhancement statute, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7, insofar as the punishment imposed for attempted voluntary manslaughter would be greater than the punishment imposed for voluntary manslaughter under similar circumstances. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 1260, permitted the court to reduce the punishment and the sentence was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the sentence of defendant and remanded the case back to the trial court to resentence defendant because the court found enhancement to the sentence should not have been applied.

 

1156 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. H & H Properties


Civ. No. 65067


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


154 Cal. App. 3d 894; 201 Cal. Rptr. 687; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1931



April 23, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 21, 1984. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 20, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 363687, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent developer a demurrer where respondent failed to comply with the county rent regulation ordinance. The ordinance in question, Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance No. 12148 was enacted following respondent's approval for conversion of rental property into condominiums.


OVERVIEW: Respondent developer had approval for conversion of rental buildings into condominiums. Subsequently, before the conversion was completed, the City of Los Angeles passed a rent regulation ordinance that provided for relocation assistance for tenants who were evicted. Respondent appealed. The court held that respondent had no vested rights argument and was required to provide the relocation assistance. The court found that all developers with vested rights were bound by the county rent control ordinance. Rent control was a valid exercise of the police power, and it affected the vested rights of every landlord who owned property before the rent control measure took effect. The court found no reason to exempt developers who were in the midst of a condominium conversion from an admitted hardship that affected the rest of the class of residential landlords.


OUTCOME: The court held that rent control was a valid exercise of the police power and applied to every landlord who owned property before the rent control measure took effect, including respondent developers in the midst of a condominium conversion. The vested rights argument was not valid, and respondent had to pay according to the rent control ordinance for the privilege of engaging in a condominium conversion in Los Angeles County.

 

1157 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aston


Crim. No. 11261.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


154 Cal. App. 3d 818; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1927



April 20, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Rehg. granted May 9, 1984 (See 162 Cal. App. 3d 658)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Siskiyou County, No. 32291, James E. Kleaver, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of their convictions in the Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California), for unlawful possession for sale of cocaine, a controlled substance under Cal. Health & Safety Code §11351.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought review of their convictions for unlawful possession for sale of cocaine, a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351. Defendants proffered a jury instruction, which stated that Cal. Health, & Safety Code § 11055(b)(4) banned only "l-cocaine" and not "d-cocaine." Defendant's also tendered testimony that "d-cocaine" was not chemically equivalent to "l-cocaine" and that the prosecution's tests did not discriminate between "l-cocaine" and "d-cocaine. The trial court denied the jury instruction and the admission of the testimony, but the appellate court reversed, finding that instructional and evidentiary errors constituted prejudicial error. Under § 11055(b)(4), only "l-cocaine," not "d-cocaine," was a controlled substance, and that the failure to so instruct the jury unconstitutionally relieved the state of the burden of proving that the substance possessed by defendants was controlled "l-cocaine." Alternatively, the errors fatally compromised the jury verdict by depriving the jury of the definitional standard by which to determine whether the substance was "l-cocaine."


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendants' convictions for unlawful possession for sale of cocaine, holding that an uncontrolled form of cocaine known as "d-cocaine" existed and reversible error resulted when the trial court refused to instruct the jury and denied the admission of related defense testimony that "d-cocaine" existed.

Goggin v. State Pers. Bd.


Civ. No. 67518


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


156 Cal. App. 3d 96; 202 Cal. Rptr. 587; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2070



April 20, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 18, 1984. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 18, 1984.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to set aside a decision of respondent California State Personnel Board. The trial court found that appellant had properly been discharged for abandoning his job under Cal. Gov't Code § 19503.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state employee worked as a youth counselor and took sick leave for his obsessive-compulsive disorder. Appellant did not leave a correct address or telephone number where he could be reached and failed to document when he might be released to return to work. Several months later, after appellant was located in another part of the state, working at a new job, he was discharged. Respondent California State Personnel Board affirmed the action. Appellant unsuccessfully sought a petition for a writ of mandate to set aside respondent's decision and then sought further review. The court affirmed, finding that the automatic resignation provision contained in Cal. Gov't Code § 19503 applied because the evidence established that appellant had abandoned his job and had been given proper notice of his termination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant state employee's petition for a writ of mandate to set aside the decision of respondent California State Personnel Board. The court found that respondent could reasonably believe appellant had abandoned his job because he did not return to work and took a new job in another part of the state.
