County of Alameda v. Mosier


No. A018506


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


154 Cal. App. 3d 757; 201 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1922



April 19, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 7, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H525248, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion to set aside the stipulated judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the superior court with directions to afford Mosier a new hearing at which he may present evidence of his lack of knowledge of his rights at the time the stipulated judgment was executed and of the effect of that knowledge on his decision to execute the stipulated judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied appellant's motion to set aside the stipulated judgment of paternity in respondent county's paternity action against appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father met with the family support division of the district attorney's office and agreed to sign a stipulated judgment with the understanding that he was only agreeing that if he was the child's father and could support the child, that he would do so. After respondent county filed a paternity action, appellant filed a motion to set aside the stipulated judgment of paternity, arguing that he was 19 years old, unrepresented by counsel, and did not know the nature of the agreement. The trial court denied appellant's motion. The court reversed the trial court's denial of appellant's motion to set aside the stipulated judgment, holding that appellant had the right to counsel in his paternity case. The court found that appellant's agreement would be voidable if he could establish that he was not advised of his right to trial on paternity and would not otherwise have executed the agreement. The court, therefore, remanded the cause to the trial court with directions to allow appellant to present evidence of his lack of knowledge of his rights at the time the stipulated judgment was executed and of the effect of that knowledge on his decision to execute the stipulated judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of appellant father's motion to set aside the stipulated judgment of paternity, holding that appellant's agreement would be voidable if he could establish that he was not advised of his right to trial on paternity and would not otherwise have executed the agreement.

 

1160 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Feno


Crim. No. 15196


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


154 Cal. App. 3d 719; 201 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1920



April 18, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 4, 1984. Staniforth, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 21, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR57555, Raul Rosado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant auto dealer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), entered on jury verdicts, in which appellant was convicted of making sales of securities which violated the Corporate Securities Law of 1968, Cal. Corp. Code §§ 25110 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant dealer solicited money from investors to fund used car purchases. Appellant repaired and resold the cars, sharing his profit with his investor. While each investor had his own arrangement, each signed a contract, "owned" specific cars, and made money only when "his" cars were sold. A jury convicted appellant of selling securities in violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25110. Appellant appealed, arguing that what he sold was not a "security," as statutorily defined, and that the jury was wrongly instructed. The court agreed, and reversed. The court found that while the lower court correctly instructed the jury that appellant had the burden of proving entitlement to any exemption from regulation, the lower court erred in describing all joint ventures as so "exempt." The error was exacerbated when, over appellant's objection, the state argued that appellant had to prove a joint venture exemption, and by the lower court's failure to instruct the jury on the defense burden of proof. Finding that the net effect of the errors was to lessen the state's burden of proving the "security" element of the offense beyond a reasonable doubt, the court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant auto dealer's conviction, by a jury, of selling securities in violation of state law, finding that the lower court erred when it instructed the jury that interests sold by appellant were exempt, rather than having the jury determine whether the interests were securities, and subject to regulation. Because the error could have resulted in a lessening the state's burden of proof, the error was prejudicial.

 

1161 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Schmid v. Lovette


Civ. No. 54157


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


154 Cal. App. 3d 466; 201 Cal. Rptr. 424; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1901



April 13, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 217880, Martin E. Rothenberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs are awarded reasonable attorney's fees in connection with prosecuting this appeal. The matter is remanded to the superior court for determination of the amount of the fee award both in the action below and on appeal and for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants school district and its officials challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) that awarded plaintiff employee attorney fees in connection with her action against defendants. In the underlying case, plaintiff was granted injunctive relief and Cal. Educ. Code §§ 7001, 7003 and 7006, which required defendants' employees to deny membership in the Communist Party, were declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendants school district and its officials appealed a judgment that awarded plaintiff employee her attorney fees in connection with her successful action against defendants that challenged the constitutionality, and enforcement by defendant, of Cal. Educ. Code §§ 7001, 7003 and 7006, which required defendants' employees to deny membership in the Communist Party. The court affirmed the judgment. The court ruled that under the "private attorney general" doctrine, as embodied in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 and under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in awarding plaintiff her attorney fees. The court ruled that because the purpose of those statutes was to encourage private citizens to enforce their civil rights, and because the public at large benefitted from the vindication of such rights, the award of attorney fees in plaintiff's case was warranted. Moreover, the court specifically ruled that for purposes of § 1988 and other similar federal statutes, defendants' good faith belief that they were required to enforce the underlying statutes was not a "special circumstance" that rendered the award of attorney fees inappropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that awarded plaintiff employee attorney fees in connection with her successful action against defendant school district and others on the constitutionality of statutes requiring the disaffirmance of membership in the Communist Party. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in awarding plaintiff her attorney fees because the public benefitted from plaintiff's vindication of her civil rights.

 

1162 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Polos


Crim. No. 17239.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


154 Cal. App. 3d 448; 200 Cal. Rptr. 749; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1899



April 12, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 19, 1984 (Crim. 23851)
Opn. filed Aug. 29, 1986 not for publication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought a writ of habeas corpus after being adjudged in contempt of court for her refusal to disclose information sought to be discovered by the state under Cal. Penal Code § 1102.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was ordered by the trial court to turn over a report prepared by an investigator for the defense in a criminal matter, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1102.6. The investigator testified on cross-examination that the report contradicted a victim's testimony. The trial court held petitioner in contempt when she refused to turn over the document on the ground that disclosure would violate her client's privilege against self-incrimination. On petitioner's application for a writ of habeas corpus, the court stayed the contempt order and held that petitioner should not be penalized with a contempt citation for a good faith assertion of her client's self-incrimination privilege absent a determination that disclosure of the information sought could not possibly have a tendency to incriminate. The court rejected the additional arguments that the disclosure would deny the client effective assistance of counsel, where the attorney-client privilege did not attach to statements given to a defense investigator acting as an agent of the attorney, or that the document was protected from disclosure as work product.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the trial court's contempt order and sentence, and granted petitioner attorney's habeas corpus petition. The court held that the contempt citation was improper absent a determination by the trial court regarding whether the document petitioner was ordered to disclose to the state could not possibly violate her client's self-incrimination privilege.

 

1163 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McCaffrey v. Preston


Civ. No. 26969


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


154 Cal. App. 3d 422; 201 Cal. Rptr. 252; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1897



April 12, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N 14844, Don Martinson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff neighbors challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that denied them a permanent injunction against defendants' operation of a residential care facility for six or fewer persons. Community Care Facilities Act (Act), Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1500 et seq. Plaintiffs claimed that the Act violated equal protection and due process and that restrictive covenant in defendants' deed precluded the venture.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff neighbors appealed a judgment that denied them a permanent injunction against defendants' operation of a residential care facility for six or fewer persons, as permitted by the Community Care Facilities Act (Act), Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1500 et seq. The court affirmed the judgment. The court rejected plaintiffs' contention that the Act violated equal protection and due process. The court ruled that the classification of such facilities as "residences," which thereby negated the "business" features of such facilities, bore a rational relationship to the legitimate state interest of moving persons out of institutions and into "normal" family-like surroundings. Moreover, the court ruled, for due process purposes, notice and an opportunity to be heard, as required by zoning and land-use statutes, were not applicable under the Act because the use of the land was not substantially altered. The court also rejected plaintiffs' claim that "residence only" restrictive covenants in the parties' deeds precluded defendants' facility. The court ruled that the covenants were personal to the original grantor and that his quit claim deed to defendants' destroyed those covenants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that denied plaintiff neighbors a permanent injunction against defendants' operation of a residential care facility for six or fewer persons under the Community Care Facilities Act. The classification and licensing of such facilities as "residences" did not offend equal protection or due process principles. Restrictive covenants in the deeds for the parties' property were personal and did not run with the land.

 

1164 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Carrillo v. Whittier Union High School Dist.


Civ. No. 67858.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


154 Cal. App. 3d 144; 201 Cal. Rptr. 75; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1871



April 5, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. July 12, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-311912, Charles H. Older, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Mexican-American citizens and voters, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which confirmed the constitutionality of appellee school district's use of an at-large system to elect school board members.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, Mexican-American citizens and voters, sought review of an adverse judgment in an action challenging the constitutional validity of electing members of the district's school board by an at-large system. The trial court found that appellee school district's at-large system had neither the purpose nor the effect of discriminating against Mexican-Americans. The court concluded that there was sufficient evidence to negate appellants' claim of discriminatory effects resulting from alleged ethnic bloc voting and to support a finding that racial polarization did not exist in any appreciable degree. The court found that appellee was responsive to the needs and interests of the Hispanic population within its ambit of governance. The court stated that there was substantial evidence to support the trial court's determination that the system did not operate to achieve a dilution of Hispanic voting power in derogation of the equal protection clause of the California Constitution. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of appellee, finding that appellee's present system, taken as a whole, did not infringe constitutional principles.


OUTCOME: The judgment confirming the constitutionality of appellee school district's practice of electing school board members by an at-large system was upheld. Appellants, Mexican-American citizens and voters, failed to prove that that use of the at-large system resulted in a dilution of Hispanic voting power in violation of the equal protection clause of the California Constitution.

 

1165 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Owens v. City of Signal Hill


Civ. No. 68360


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


154 Cal. App. 3d 123; 201 Cal. Rptr. 70; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1869



April 4, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 20, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 64523, John A. Arguelles, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Finally, Owens contends that these ordinances were enacted for an impermissible reason: to drive her enterprise out of business. It is true that discriminatory purpose will invalidate an otherwise valid law. ( Washington v. Davis (1976) 426 U.S. 229 [48 L.Ed.2d 597, 96 S.Ct. 2040].) There is, however, no presumption of improper purpose. The burden of proof is on Owens. ( Arlington Heights v. Metropolitan Housing Corp. (1977) 429 U.S. 252, 270 [50 L.Ed.2d 450, 468, 97 S.Ct. 555].) Owens simply makes bald charges. Nothing in the record points to anything in the legislative history, or in the legislation itself, to support her theory. We find no reason to doubt that the City's ordinances are intended to prevent prostitution. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant massage parlor owner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that denied her request for an injunction on constitutional grounds against the enforcement of respondent city's ordinances regulating massage establishments and technicians.


OVERVIEW: Appellant massage parlor owner sought an injunction on constitutional grounds against enforcement of respondent city's ordinances regulating massage establishments and technicians. The trial court denied appellant's request. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's denial. The court held that regulation of massage businesses did not involve a suspect classification and was reviewable for a rational relationship to a permissible state goal. The court held that respondent's regulations on training and hours of operation were rationally related to the goals of ensuring the health of patrons and discouraging prostitution. The court held that ordinances voiding existing permits and requiring new permits to continue in business were not ex post facto laws because they did not punish failure to obtain the new permits before the ordinances took effect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant massage parlor owner's request for an injunction against enforcement of respondent city's ordinances regulating massage establishments and technicians. The court held that the ordinances were constitutional as business regulations.

Karlin v. Zalta


Civ. No. 67698


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


154 Cal. App. 3d 953; 201 Cal. Rptr. 379; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1939



March 29, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 24, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 14, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-393912, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) dismissing her claim in a class action suit alleging combination and conspiracy in unreasonable restraint of business of insurance in violation of Cal. Ins. Code §§ 790.02 and 790.03(c).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a class action claim alleging that the insurance defendants combined and conspired to offer members of defendant physician council cheaper medical malpractice insurance for the sole purpose of monopolizing the insurance business. Thereafter, plaintiff and patients who constituted members of her class were charged excessive premiums. Defendants denied conspiracy in restraint of trade of insurance or to establish monopoly. They also asserted that plaintiff lacked standing and failed to exhaust administrative remedies. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court dismissing the claim. It held that the ratemaking aspect of the insurance business enjoyed unique antitrust exemption. Further, the court held that plaintiff's failure to exhaust administrative remedies was an incurable jurisdictional defect justifying dismissal of the action.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of plaintiff's claim was affirmed because the ratemaking aspect of the insurance business enjoyed exemption from antitrust laws. Moreover, plaintiff did not exhaust available administrative remedies.

Maloney v. Department of Motor Vehicles


No. AO21068.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


153 Cal. App. 3d 699; 200 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1817



March 27, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. May 31, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 108982, Richard M. Sims, Jr., * Judge.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Court of Appeal sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate after respondent department of motor vehicles held, at defendant's request, a hearing at which it determined that there was adequate cause for the suspension of defendant's driver's license for drunk driving.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of the trial court's denial of his request for a writ of mandate after respondent department of motor vehicles determined that adequate cause existed for the suspension of defendant's driver's license for drunk driving. Defendant contended that at the hearing, his right to cross examine the police officers who conducted the field sobriety tests was unconstitutionally limited by the hearing examiner, who stated that he would not allow detailed cross-examination about the field tests. The court affirmed the trial court's decision, finding that the decision was thorough and showed sufficient evidence to find that defendant was not prejudiced by the erroneous limitation because he was allowed full cross examination of the officers. The court stated it would overlook technical oversights and rejected defendant's argument that a temporary legislative misnumbering of the drunk driving laws made his arrest illegal. The court chastised respondent for conducting hearings wherein the complaint of a citizen challenging the suspension of his license was heard by one of respondent's own employees, because such hearing, at best, gave a tarnished perspective of justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's request for a writ of mandate after respondent department of motor vehicles determined that adequate cause existed for the suspension of defendant's driver's license for drunk driving. The record showed that defendant was allowed to fully cross examine the police officers who conducted the field sobriety tests which led to his arrest.

 

1168 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Beheler


Crim. No. 5136.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


153 Cal. App. 3d 242; 200 Cal. Rptr. 195; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1773



March 19, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Apr. 11, 1984; Hg. den. May 17, 1984, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 20967, John Michael Nairn, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicting him of felony murder with special circumstances and attempted robbery, and sentencing him to life imprisonment without parole. The court had previously reversed appellant's conviction for a lack of a Miranda warning, but the United States Supreme Court had reversed the court's decision and remanded.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of felony murder with special circumstances and attempted robbery after appellant's companion shot and killed a woman during an attempted robbery. Appellant was drunk at the time of the robbery. The court reversed appellant's conviction, but the United States Supreme Court reversed the court. On remand, the court struck the special circumstances finding and reduced appellant's conviction to voluntary manslaughter. There was absolutely no evidence that appellant harbored an intent to kill; the evidence showed he was drunk to the point of stupor. Appellant did not participate in the killing, could not prevent the shooting, and called the police and assisted in their investigation. Appellant's punishment was grossly excessive compared to that of his companion, who had been convicted of voluntary manslaughter. The court held the sentence for either first or second degree murder would violate the prohibition of Cal. Const., art. I, § 17, against cruel or unusual punishment. Therefore, the court reduced appellant's conviction to voluntary manslaughter.


OUTCOME: The court struck the special circumstances finding and reduced appellant's first degree felony murder conviction to voluntary manslaughter and, as so modified, affirmed the judgment as to convictions of both voluntary manslaughter and attempted robbery. There was no evidence that appellant had had an intent to kill.

Estate of Cirone


No. AO23609


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


153 Cal. App. 3d 199; 200 Cal. Rptr. 511; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1769



March 19, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 10, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 13, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 105234, Eugene M. Premo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant comptroller challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which sustained respondents' objection to the imposition of state inheritance tax, claiming that the language of Proposition 6, which repealed the state inheritance and gift tax, was not applicable to the estates of persons dying at any time on the day of its passage.


OVERVIEW: Appellant comptroller challenged the trial court's order sustaining respondent executor and others' objection to the imposition of state inheritance tax, claiming that Proposition 6, which repealed the state inheritance and gift tax, was not applicable to the estates of persons dying at any time on the day of its passage. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. Proposition 6 provided that it was operative as of its date of passage and applicable to the estates of persons dying on or after such date, and the court determined that Proposition 6 was effective from the beginning of the day it was passed. The court rejected appellant's contention that Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 6 prohibited repeal of the inheritance tax for the entire day of the date of passage, because § 6 was inapplicable to actions taken by the electorate. Finally, the court determined that Cal. Const. art. II, §10(a) did not make the repeal of inheritance tax effective on the day after passage because there was no constitutional bar to retroactive repeal of the inheritance tax by initiative and because § 10(a) was inapplicable to an initiative measure providing when it takes effect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that there was no restriction on the power of the electorate to make its repeal of the inheritance tax applicable to the estates of those dying on the day of the enactment of the initiative.

Kreutzer v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 28843


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


153 Cal. App. 3d 62; 200 Cal. Rptr. 322; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1755

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for attorney's fees in his action to reinstate and renew an outdoor entertainment license suspended by respondent county.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was issued an outdoor entertainment license by respondent county for his recreational facility. The license was issued for one year and was subject to certain conditions including attendance limits at outdoor concerts. Appellant applied for renewal and was denied because of repeated violations. Duffy v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 21206


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


152 Cal. App. 3d 1156; 199 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1744



March 13, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 286753, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for the entry of summary judgment in favor of defendant California State Board of Equalization.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Board of Equalization appealed a judgment in favor of plaintiff tailor by Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) in plaintiff's challenge of sale tax collected by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant State Board of Equalization assessed plaintiff tailor back taxes after it audited plaintiff. The tax was assessed upon amounts received by plaintiff for initial alterations of garments furnished by his customers but never worn except for trying on or fitting. Defendant based its assessment of tax on Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 1524, which declared charges for alterations to new clothing to be taxable. Plaintiff petitioned for redetermination of tax, and later for a refund of the taxes paid. The trial court opined that due process required that the meaning of statutes be definite and certain and granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed the decision of the trial court on the ground that the applicable statutes and regulations, Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 6006 (b), Cal. Admin. Code tit. 18, § 1526, clearly required that plaintiff pay tax on alterations to new clothing and the trial court erred when it concluded that new clothing needed to be further defined by regulation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed because the statute on which the judgment was based clearly made amounts received by a tailor for alterations to new clothing supplied by customers subject to sales tax.

In re Ramirez


No. B001519.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


152 Cal. App. 3d 1050; 200 Cal. Rptr. 11; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1733



March 9, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Mar. 13, 1984; Hg. granted May 3, 1984 (See 39 Cal. 3d 931)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the state supreme court to determine the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 2932 in light of the ex-post facto clause, on petitioner prisoner's writ of habeas challenging the loss of good time credits which were ordered forfeited under the statutory provision not in effect at the time he was convicted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prisoner filed a writ of habeas seeking review of Cal. Penal Code §§ 2932- 35, which were new statutory provisions affecting prisoner's ability to earn and forfeit good time credits. Petitioner contended that Cal. Penal Code § 2932 violates the ex post facto clauses of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9, because the statute was passed after he was convicted. The lower court denied petitioner's writ for failure to exhaust administrative remedies. After appeal to the state Supreme Court, the matter was remanded for determination of the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 2932. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 2932 violated neither the state nor federal constitutions because the new statute only applied prospectively, not retroactively, to petitioner's actions taken after his conviction. Therefore, petitioner had prior notice of prohibited conduct that would result in the forfeiture of good time credit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed its prior ruling because the penal statute that caused petitioner prisoner to lose good credit applied only to violations in prison occurring after the statute had been passed and therefore the statute was not an unconstitutional ex-post facto law.

 

1173 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jackson


Crim. No. 43810


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


152 Cal. App. 3d 961; 199 Cal. Rptr. 848; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1723



March 8, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - At the direction of the court, and pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976 and 976.1, the portion of the opinion certified for publication follows.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 31, 1984. Kaus, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 084370, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Due to the omission of an instruction relating premeditation to Jackson's mental state defense, the verdict of attempted murder in the first degree cannot be upheld. The defendant is entitled to a reduction to attempted murder in the second degree. Pursuant to section 1260, 10 we remand the matter and direct the trial court to reduce the judgment of conviction to attempted murder in the second degree.



10 Section 1260 provides that "[the] court may reverse, affirm, or modify a judgment or order appealed from, or reduce the degree of the offense or attempted offense or the punishment imposed, and may set aside, affirm, or modify any or all of the proceedings subsequent to, or dependent upon, such judgment or order, and may, if proper, order a new trial and may, if proper, remand the cause to the trial court for such further proceedings as may be just under the circumstances."

 [***2] In view of the court's failure to state sufficient facts justifying the imposition of the upper term, the matter is further remanded for the limited purposes of resentencing appellant for his conviction of attempted murder in the second degree. Should the court choose to impose the upper term, it should state sufficient factors justifying the imposition of said term pursuant to section 1170, subdivision (b), and rule 439(c).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted appellant of attempted murder in the first degree and assault with a deadly weapon following a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant asserted that a mental defect precluded him from having the specific intent, malice aforethought, and premeditation necessary to sustain his conviction for attempted murder. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The trial court limited the testimony of appellant's psychiatrists pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 28-29. The court held that §§ 28-29 did not impose an unconstitutional limit on defendant's due process right to present a defense. The court held that § 28, which precluded defendant from introducing evidence that he lacked the capacity to formulate malice aforethought and to premeditate, reflected the legislature's determination that for reasons of reliability or public policy, capacity evidence was inadmissible. The court held that § 29, which prohibited experts from testifying that defendant did not have the required mental status, merely placed the determination of whether defendant had the required mental status in the exclusive province of the trier of fact. The court reversed the conviction for attempted murder in the first degree because there was no instruction relating premeditation to defendant's mental state defense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part the judgment of the trial court. The court reversed appellant's conviction for attempted murder in the first degree because of the omission of an instruction relating premeditation to appellant's mental state defense. The court remanded and directed the trial court to reduce the judgment of conviction to attempted murder in the second degree. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment.

Griffin Development Co. v. City of Oxnard


Civ. No. 64281.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


152 Cal. App. 3d 846; 199 Cal. Rptr. 739; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1713



March 7, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 28, 1984 (See 39 Cal. 3d 256)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer challenged on constitutional grounds the California trial court's judgment upholding respondent city's decision to deny appellant a special use permit for conversion of apartments to condominiums pursuant to Oxnard, Cal., Ordinance No. 1805.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city denied appellant developer's application for a special use permit for conversion of apartments to condominiums on the grounds it did not comply with Oxnard, Cal., Ordinance No. 1805. The ordinance provided that an existing apartment unit could not be converted into a condominium without a special-use permit and that an apartment building, which was a nonconforming use because of parking, was not entitled to conversion. Appellant challenged the decision, claiming that a change in form of ownership of the property was not a change in "use," that the ordinance exceeded respondent's police power under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 7, and that the ordinance violated the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court agreed and concluded the ordinance was invalid under the state and federal constitutions. The court reversed the decision and remanded to the trial court for issuance of a writ of mandate requiring respondent to grant the permit. The court held that respondent's denial of the permit exceeded its police powers because the ordinance as applied to appellant was unreasonable, oppressive and not substantially related to the public health, safety or welfare.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded with directions to issue its writ of mandate requiring respondent to issue the special use permit to appellant developer. Respondent city could not withhold the permit because the city ordinance was invalid and an unlawful exercise of police power.

People v. Reeder


Crim. No. 11309


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


152 Cal. App. 3d 900; 200 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1719



March 7, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 25680, Terence J. Keeley, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed as to all counts. The matter is remanded for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict from the trial court, which convicted him of four counts of forcible oral copulation in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a)(c) and four counts of rape by means of threat in violation of former Cal. Penal Code § 261(3), now Cal. Penal Code § 261(2). The court affirmed, and the California Supreme Court remanded to the court for reconsideration in light of a recently decided opinion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of four counts of forcible oral copulation in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a)(c) and four counts of rape by means of threat in violation of former Cal. Penal Code § 261(3), now Cal. Penal Code § 261(2). Upon remand from the Supreme Court, the court affirmed the judgment, but remanded for resentencing because it agreed with defendant that the proper statute to be applied in this case was Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(c) instead of Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(d). The court rejected the state's argument that the offenses against the last victim, who was raped more than one time, were committed on separate occasions because the termination of the first rape through sexual climax was when the first occasion ended and another began. The court held that the crimes against the last victim occurred on a single evening in one continuous, uninterrupted sequence over a short period of time in a car parked at the same spot. Therefore, the "same victim on separate occasions" clause of § 667.6(d) could not be invoked to require the defendant serve full, separate, and consecutive sentences for each of the offenses committed against the last victim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, but remanded for resentencing. The court held that just because the victim had been raped more than one time by defendant, the "same victim on separate occasions" requirement was not met that would allow full, separate, and consecutive sentences for each of the offenses committed in one continuous, uninterrupted sequence over a short period of time.

 

1176 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Main


Crim. No. 6723


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


152 Cal. App. 3d 686; 199 Cal. Rptr. 683; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1698



February 29, 1984 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Part II of the principal opinion is not published because it does not meet the standards for publication contained in California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1. Part II of the concurring and dissenting opinion of Andreen, J., relates to part II of the principal opinion and for that reason is not published.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied March 29, 1984, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 24, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 21225, William Silveira, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which sentenced him to three years in state prison for committing a robbery with a gun shortly after he turned 18. Appellant claimed that his sentence was cruel and unusual because the statutory scheme of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1732.5 and Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06 made him ineligible for either commitment to the California Youth Authority or probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant robbed a restaurant at gunpoint a few days after his 18th birthday. Appellant pleaded guilty to robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211 and firearm use under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. Appellant was not eligible for a California Youth Authority (CYA) commitment under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1732.5 because he was over 18 or probation under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.0 because a firearm was used. Appellant claimed that the statutory scheme resulted in a mandatory prison term and subjected him to cruel and unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. The court affirmed and held that even though appellant's gun was unloaded, the victim could have responded with great bodily force, thus, his offense was a potentially violent crime. Further, the court held that appellant's argument was not that the length of sentence was disproportionate, but that the place of confinement, prison, rather than CYA or probation, was cruel or unusual. Imprisonment for armed robbery was recognized as an acceptable means of punishment for armed robbery and was not so disproportionate to the crime that it shocked the conscience and offended fundamental notions of human dignity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sentenced appellant to three years in a state prison for armed robbery because imprisonment was a recognized, acceptable means of punishment for the very serious crime of armed robbery, and the automatic denial of probation or commitment to California Youth Authority did not constitute cruel or unusual punishment.

 

1177 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Shapiro v. Wells Fargo Realty Advisors


Civ. No. 70061


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


152 Cal. App. 3d 467; 199 Cal. Rptr. 613; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1679; 119 L.R.R.M. 2520; 1 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1803; 100 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,454



February 28, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 17, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC76192, Raymond Choate, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) sustaining defendant employer's demurrer and dismissing plaintiff's first amended complaint in an action for breach of implied contract, wrongful and malicious termination, and breach of an implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant employer terminated plaintiff employee after three and one-half years of employment. Plaintiff alleged that defendant breached an implied-in-fact contract not to terminate him without good cause, that his termination was wrongful and malicious, and that defendant had breached an implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer as to all causes of action, and the court affirmed. The court noted that Cal. Lab. Code § 2922 provided that employment having no specified term could be terminated at the will of either party, but that application of the statute had been limited pursuant to three theories: wrongful discharge in violation of public policy; breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing; and breach of an implied-in-fact covenant to terminate only for good cause. The court held that plaintiff had failed to identify any statutory right or public policy violated, to allege either lengthy employment or express policies or procedures violated by defendant, or to allege that the parties had agreed, expressly or impliedly, that plaintiff could be terminated only for good cause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the complaint because plaintiff employee had failed to allege facts which would support a tort cause of action for wrongful discharge in violation of public policy, a breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing, or the breach of an implied-in-fact covenant to terminate only for good cause.

 

1178 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Asuncion


Crim. No. 43196


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


152 Cal. App. 3d 422; 199 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1673



February 24, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 17341, Stephen J. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate salesmen sought review of their conviction by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which found appellants guilty of usury under Cal. Civ. Code § 1916(3)(b), and appellants asserted that the law was not applicable to them and was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Appellant real estate salesmen were convicted of usury under Cal. Civ. Code § 1916(3)(b) for making a loan at an interest rate of 288 percent. Appellants challenged the conviction and asserted that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and that it did not apply to them because they were licensed real estate salesmen. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and found that § 1916(3)(b) was not vague. The court held that appellants could have reasonably understood that the statute prohibited making a loan at an interest rate of 288 percent. The court held that appellants did not fall into any of the exceptions under § 1916(3)(b) because Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10132 did not give appellants authority to negotiate or make loans. The court held that only real estate brokers, under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10132, were authorized to negotiate and make loans, and appellants could only act as agents on behalf of a licensed broker.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because appellant real estate salesmen had no authority to make or negotiate loans, and they could have reasonably understood that making a loan at 288 percent interest clearly exceeded the limits of the usury statute.

 

1179 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Star-Kist Foods, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 69023.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


152 Cal. App. 3d 258; 199 Cal. Rptr. 440; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1661



February 23, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 31, 1984 (See 42 Cal. 3d 1)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 2280083, Julius M. Title, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which refused to refund ad valorem taxes paid by plaintiff to defendants, county and cities. Plaintiff maintained that said taxes were prohibited pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 225.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation sought a refund of ad valorem taxes imposed by defendant county and cities. Plaintiff's refund claim was based on Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 225, which exempted from taxation property manufactured outside of the United States and brought into the state for trans-shipment out of the state. The court first determined that defendants had standing to raise the issue of the constitutionality of § 225 because defendants stood to suffer economic injury in the form of lost tax revenues. The court then found that § 225 was not unconstitutional. The statute promoted uniformity in commercial relations with foreign nations. In analyzing a potential conflict with the commerce clause, the court found § 225 was tied to an activity with a substantial nexus with the state and did not discriminate against interstate commerce. The court held that plaintiff was entitled to the relief sought and the trial court's judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court, permitting the levying of ad valorem tax on appellant corporation was reversed. Statute providing exemption from the tax was consistent with the federal import-export clause and nonviolative of the federal commerce clause.

 

1180 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Conservatorship of Valerie N.


Civ. No. 53699.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


152 Cal. App. 3d 224; 199 Cal. Rptr. 478; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1660



February 23, 1984 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 20, 1984 (S.F. 24745)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 100974, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which appointed them as conservators over respondent mentally incompetent adult but denied appellants' request to have respondent sterilized.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parents sought review of an order, which denied their request to have respondent mentally incompetent adult sterilized. On appeal the court affirmed the order because the jurisdiction of the probate court was statutory and no statute authorized the probate court to order sterilization on an incompetent person. The court found that Cal. Probate Code § 2356 was not pertinent to the case, because it was not in effect at the time of appellant's petition. The court reviewed the constitutionality of § 2356 because appellants challenged its constitutionality. The court held that Cal. Probate Code § 2356 was constitutional because it protected a person in respondent's situation from being sterilized against her will. The court held that respondent was not being denied her equal protection rights under the constitution because competent and incompetent persons differed significantly in their ability to give informed consent and as a result classification based on that difference was reasonable and the state had a duty to take reasonable steps to protect an incompetent person's rights to privacy, which included the right to bear or not bear children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which granted appellant parents their request to be conservators over respondent mentally incompetent adult, and denied appellants' request to have respondent sterilized because the probate court's jurisdiction was solely granted by statute and there were not any statutes in effect at the time which authorized the probate court to order sterilization of an incompetent person.

 

1181 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Theroux v. Cal.


Civ. No. 68469


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


152 Cal. App. 3d 1; 199 Cal. Rptr. 264; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1640



February 17, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA000573, Arthur Baldonado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state and state controller, appeal the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that granted plaintiff former employees' motion for summary judgment of their action to recover a lump sum payment as retroactive salary adjustments for a time period when they were employees of defendant state.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former employees of defendant state sought damages when they were excluded from the lump sum payment to certain classes of state employees, including current employees on or after May 31, 1979, and those who retired between October 1, 1978, and May 31, 1979. The payments were expressly to provide compensation equivalent to a seven-percent increase if same had been awarded on October 1, 1978. Defendants, state and state controller, and plaintiffs filed cross motions for summary judgment, and plaintiffs' motion was granted. The court affirmed the award of summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, holding that plaintiffs' exclusion from the lump sum payments violated the Civil Service Act which required that like salaries be paid for comparable duties and responsibilities. Because plaintiffs performed the same jobs during the subject time period as those employees who remained in employment, to exclude them from the admitted salary adjustment bonuses impermissibly created two pay scales for workers doing the same job. Further, the utilization of a cutoff date to withhold compensation for the same services from one group of employees was arbitrary and unjustified.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting summary judgment in favor of plaintiff former employees was affirmed because the court determined that denying the payments to plaintiffs, who were employees of the same time period as those who did not leave before the cutoff date, violated the Civil Service Act and was an unconstitutional impairment of a vested contractual right.

People v. Pointer


No. AO17295


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


151 Cal. App. 3d 1128; 199 Cal. Rptr. 357; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1632



February 17, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 16, 1984, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 26, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 79071, Christopher C. Cottle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the condition of probation prohibiting conception is overbroad, as less restrictive alternatives are available that would feasibly provide the protections the trial court properly believed necessary. In order to provide the trial court an opportunity to devise a specific alternative condition or conditions we reverse only that portion of the judgment prohibiting conception as a condition of probation and remand for resentencing consistent with the views herein expressed. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which convicted her of felony of child endangerment in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273a(1), found her to be in violation of a custody decree, and ordered that defendant was prohibited from conceiving a child as a condition of her probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was devoted to a macrobiotic diet, was convicted of felony of child endangerment in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273a(1). Defendant argued that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that section 273a(1) was a general rather then a specific intent crime. The court disagreed, affirmed the trial court jury instruction, and held that it would have been error for the trial court to apply the specific intent instruction because felony child endangerment was a general intent crime. Further, defendant challenged the probation condition that she not conceive during the probationary period as an unconstitutional restriction of her fundamental rights to privacy and to procreate. The court concluded that the condition in question was reasonable but overbroad. The court reversed and remanded only the portion of the judgment prohibiting conception as a condition of probation because less restrictive alternatives were available that would feasibly provide the protections the trial court properly believed necessary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of defendant for conviction of felony child endangerment because the jury instruction on general intent was proper. The court reversed only that portion of the judgment prohibiting conception as a condition of probation finding it was overbroad, as less restrictive alternatives were available. The court remanded the case to provide the trial court an opportunity to devise a specific alternative condition.

Beck v. County of San Mateo


No. A015501


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


154 Cal. App. 3d 374; 201 Cal. Rptr. 365; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1893



February 16, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 251052, John J. Bible, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents appealed decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County's (California), which granted summary judgment for and demurrer to respondents, county and commission, in appellants' action challenging the annexation of their property as improper based on the denial of a right to vote on the annexation issue under Cal. Gov't Code § 35150 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 860 et seq..


OVERVIEW: Respondents, county and commission, annexed some land that belonged to appellant residents pursuant to the Municipal Reorganization Act of 1977, Gov. Code, § 35000 et seq.. Appellants claimed that their land on a residential island was improperly annexed without an election. The trial court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment and sustained their demurrer. The court affirmed and held that no election on annexation was required under Cal. Gov't Code § 35150(f). The court held that appellants had no fundamental right to a vote on the annexation because the legislature had no duty to provide a right to such a vote, and that its policy was based on the legitimate interest of promoting an orderly, efficient, and economical formation and determination of city boundaries. The court held that no municipal reorganization was at issue under § 35300, and that an election on the annexation was contrary to the legislature's purpose. Appellants' property, which had been previously annexed, was not exempt from a later, partial annexation under §§ 35014 and 35150(f), and that appellants' property was only part of the island. Thus, its annexation was permitted under § 35041.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that annexation of appellant residents' land on residential island was properly conducted by respondents, county and commission, because the annexation of the entire island was not required, and such was not a municipal reorganization. The court held that appellants had no fundamental right to an election because the legislature's denial of the right to vote was related to a state legitimate purpose.

 

1184 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Villasenor


Crim. No. 43724


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


152 Cal. App. 3d 30; 199 Cal. Rptr. 349; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1644



February 9, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 21, 1984. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 4, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 17658, Lawrence Storch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which convicted him following his plea of nolo contendere to a charge of robbery. Defendant was sentenced to nine years in state prison, including one year for an enhancement per Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b), plus five years for a prior robbery conviction per Cal. Penal Code § 667.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667 to a total of nine years in state prison following his conviction of armed robbery. Defendant admitted to having used a weapon to commit the robbery and to having been previously convicted of robbery. Defendant appealed his sentence contending that § 667 was unconstitutional because it constituted cruel and unusual punishment and violated due process of law. The court affirmed the ruling because it found that the statute had a reasonable and proper purpose in discouraging persons who committed serious felonies from doing it again. Defendant committed armed robbery and then again committed robbery with the use of a deadly weapon. These crimes were dangerous to society. Thus the punishment was proportionate to the offense and had a direct relation to the culpability of the defendant and in no way offended fundamental notions of human dignity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed trial court's sentencing of defendant because it found that the enhancement statute was constitutionally valid and had a real and substantial relation to the object sought to be obtained, which was to discourage the behavior of serious felons.

 

1185 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Powers


Crim. No. 43045


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


151 Cal. App. 3d 905; 199 Cal. Rptr. 142; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1611



February 8, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 3, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 450374, William E. McGinley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment entered upon appellant's plea of guilty to murder in the first degree is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted appellant of first degree burglary and robbery, and sentenced him to 25 years to life for his plea of guilty to first degree murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree burglary and robbery, and pled guilty to murder in the first degree under Cal. Penal Code § 187. Appellant was 17 at the time of the offense. In light of changes in the law subsequent to his plea, appellant contended that his plea should have been set aside because it was induced by the improper threat of unlawful felony-murder special circumstances allegation carrying a life sentence without parole, and because he received only an illusory benefit from his bargain. The court affirmed, holding the fact that subsequent decisions established that a felony-murder special circumstance allegation required an intent to kill or aid in a killing, and that a minor could not be sentenced to life without possibility of parole did not affect appellant's voluntary guilty plea. Appellant declared his guilt under the then existing law. Appellant's contention that his plea bargain was illusory was negated by the fact that appellant received a guarantee that his sentence would provide for the possibility of parole no matter how subsequent cases ruled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for first degree burglary and robbery, and his sentence of 25 years to life for his guilty plea to first degree murder because changes in the law subsequent to his plea, regarding potential maximum penalties, did not require that his plea be set aside.

Wesley Inv. Co. v. County of Alameda


No. AO19484


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


151 Cal. App. 3d 672; 198 Cal. Rptr. 872; 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1587



February 3, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H 80606-8, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate challenging respondent county's rejection of appellant's proposed use of its real property to construct a convenience store in a C-1 zone.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation petitioned for a writ of mandate after respondent county rejected appellant's proposed use of its real property to build a convenience store. The lower court denied the writ, and appellant sought review. The court, on appeal, affirmed the judgment of the lower court. The court acknowledged that the site in question was in a zone where a convenience store could have been lawfully maintained. However, this did not diminish respondent's power to determine that a particular development was not suitable for that location. The court also held that respondent did not improperly delegate legislative power because respondent was following the mandate of Alameda County, Cal., Ordinance Code § 8-95-5, which allowed respondent to exercise its judgment to deny a site review application. Finally, the court concluded that respondent's action was supported by substantial evidence, and respondent did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's site review application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the writ of mandate. The court found that although appellant corporation's proposed use was permitted under the zoning ordinance, respondent county could reject appellant's proposed use under its site development review procedure. The court also found that respondent's rejection of appellant's site review application was supported by substantial evidence, and that it did not constitute an abuse of discretion.
